\title{CalibDrive: Are Driving VLAs Calibrated? \\
A Benchmark for Uncertainty-Aware Autonomous Driving}

\author{
  Raj Dandekar\thanks{Equal contribution.} \\
  \texttt{raj@vizuara.ai} \\
  \And
  Rajat Dandekar\footnotemark[1] \\
  \texttt{rajat@vizuara.ai} \\
  \And
  Sreedath Panat \\
  \texttt{sreedath@vizuara.ai} \\
  \And
  Claude Code \\
  Anthropic \\
}

\begin{document}
\maketitle

\begin{abstract}
Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models are rapidly emerging as unified architectures for autonomous driving, jointly processing visual observations, linguistic instructions, and action generation. However, a critical gap exists: \emph{these models lack reliable uncertainty quantification}, producing confident predictions even in novel or dangerous scenarios where they are likely to fail. We introduce \textbf{CalibDrive}, the first systematic benchmark for evaluating the calibration of driving VLAs across 8 scenario categories spanning routine highway driving to safety-critical long-tail events. Our analysis of OpenVLA-7B reveals severe miscalibration: the baseline model exhibits an Expected Calibration Error (ECE) of 0.399, with catastrophic miscalibration (ECE $>$ 0.70) in long-tail scenarios such as emergency vehicles and occluded agents, where the model is confidently wrong in 100\% of cases. We evaluate five uncertainty quantification methods---MC Dropout, Deep Ensembles, Temperature Scaling, Conformal Prediction, and a novel Prompt Ensemble approach---and propose \textbf{CalibDrive-Combined}, a hybrid method that combines ensemble disagreement with conformal coverage guarantees and Platt-scaled confidence mapping. MC Dropout achieves the largest calibration improvement (58\% ECE reduction), while Conformal Prediction achieves perfect failure detection (AUROC = 1.0). Through selective prediction, calibrated VLAs reduce failure rates by 43\% at 50\% coverage, demonstrating that uncertainty-aware VLAs can identify their own failure modes and abstain in dangerous situations. Critically, we discover that OpenVLA-7B assigns nearly identical confidence to highway driving and completely out-of-distribution noise images---large-scale validation ($N$=200) reveals the gap is \emph{inverted} ($\Delta = -0.020$) and not statistically significant ($p = 0.321$). The model ships with all 211 dropout layers set to $p = 0$, making standard MC Dropout inapplicable without modification. Most significantly, we discover that \emph{cosine distance} in the model's hidden state space provides near-universal OOD detection (AUROC = 0.979) across both pixel-level and semantic OOD, requiring only 25 calibration images and a single centroid vector. Code and benchmark are available at \url{https://github.com/sreedath/Vizuara-VLA-Research}.
\end{abstract}

%==============================================================================
\section{Introduction}
\label{sec:intro}
%==============================================================================

Vision-Language-Action (VLA) models represent a paradigm shift in autonomous driving, unifying visual perception, natural language reasoning, and continuous action generation within a single end-to-end architecture \cite{kim2024openvla, tian2024drivevlm, chen2024autovala}. Recent systems such as DriveVLM \cite{tian2024drivevlm}, AutoVLA \cite{chen2024autovala}, Alpamayo-R1 \cite{sima2025alpamayo}, and ELF-VLA \cite{elf2025vla} demonstrate impressive performance across complex driving scenarios, often matching or exceeding modular pipeline approaches on standard benchmarks like NAVSIM \cite{dauner2024navsim} and nuScenes \cite{caesar2020nuscenes}.

However, a fundamental question remains unanswered: \emph{when these models predict a trajectory, how confident should we be in that prediction?} Current driving VLAs produce point predictions without any calibrated measure of uncertainty. This is particularly dangerous in safety-critical deployment, where a model that is confidently wrong in an unfamiliar scenario---such as encountering an emergency vehicle, an occluded pedestrian, or unusual road debris---can lead to catastrophic failures.

The need for calibrated uncertainty in driving models is well-established in the classical autonomous driving literature \cite{feng2021review, mcallister2017concrete}, yet the unique challenges of VLA architectures---which tokenize continuous actions into discrete bins and autoregressively decode multi-dimensional trajectories---create novel calibration challenges that have not been studied. Only one prior work \cite{zollo2025calibration} has investigated VLA calibration, focusing exclusively on tabletop robotics (LIBERO benchmark), finding ECE values ranging from 0.046 to 0.381 across tasks. No study has examined VLA calibration in the safety-critical driving domain.

\textbf{Contributions.} We make the following contributions:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{CalibDrive Benchmark}: The first systematic benchmark for evaluating VLA calibration in autonomous driving, spanning 8 scenario categories across 3 difficulty levels with standardized metrics (Section~\ref{sec:benchmark}).

    \item \textbf{Miscalibration Analysis}: A comprehensive study revealing that driving VLAs are severely miscalibrated (ECE = 0.399 overall), with catastrophic miscalibration (ECE $>$ 0.70) in long-tail safety-critical scenarios (Section~\ref{sec:experiments}).

    \item \textbf{UQ Method Evaluation}: Systematic comparison of five uncertainty quantification methods adapted for driving VLAs, including a novel Prompt Ensemble approach that exploits VLAs' language conditioning (Section~\ref{sec:methods}).

    \item \textbf{CalibDrive-Combined}: A hybrid method combining ensemble disagreement, MC Dropout variance, and conformal coverage guarantees with Platt-scaled confidence mapping (Section~\ref{sec:combined}).

    \item \textbf{Selective Prediction}: Demonstration that calibrated VLAs can reduce failure rates by 43\% through uncertainty-aware abstention, with implications for safe deployment (Section~\ref{sec:selective}).
\end{enumerate}

%==============================================================================
\section{Related Work}
\label{sec:related}
%==============================================================================

\subsection{Vision-Language-Action Models for Driving}

VLA models for autonomous driving can be categorized into three generations. \textbf{Modular VLAs} like DriveVLM \cite{tian2024drivevlm} and InsightDrive \cite{insightdrive2025} use VLMs for scene understanding while maintaining separate planning modules. \textbf{End-to-end VLAs} such as OpenVLA \cite{kim2024openvla}, AutoVLA \cite{chen2024autovala}, and LatentVLA \cite{latentvla2025} directly output control actions from multimodal inputs. \textbf{Reasoning-augmented VLAs} like Alpamayo-R1 \cite{sima2025alpamayo} and CoC-VLA \cite{cocvla2025} incorporate chain-of-thought reasoning for complex scenarios. Despite rapid progress in prediction accuracy, none of these models provide calibrated uncertainty estimates.

\subsection{Uncertainty Quantification in Deep Learning}

Established UQ methods include MC Dropout \cite{gal2016dropout}, which approximates Bayesian inference through stochastic dropout at test time; Deep Ensembles \cite{lakshminarayanan2017simple}, which capture epistemic uncertainty through model diversity; Temperature Scaling \cite{guo2017calibration}, a post-hoc recalibration via a learned temperature parameter; and Conformal Prediction \cite{angelopoulos2023conformal}, which provides distribution-free coverage guarantees. While extensively studied for classification and regression, their adaptation to the autoregressive, tokenized action space of VLAs remains unexplored.

\subsection{Calibration of Foundation Models}

Recent work on LLM calibration \cite{kadavath2022language, tian2023just} reveals that larger language models tend to be better calibrated on factual questions but exhibit systematic overconfidence on adversarial or out-of-distribution inputs. For VLMs, calibration studies \cite{chen2024vlm_calib} show similar overconfidence patterns. For VLAs specifically, Zollo et al.~\cite{zollo2025calibration} provide the only calibration study to date, evaluating on the LIBERO robotics benchmark and finding task-dependent miscalibration. Our work extends this to the safety-critical driving domain with more diverse scenarios and a broader set of UQ methods.

\subsection{Safety in Autonomous Driving}

Selective prediction \cite{geifman2017selective, el2010foundations} enables models to abstain from making predictions when uncertain. In driving, this corresponds to requesting human intervention or executing a minimal risk condition (MRC). Prior work on safe driving under uncertainty \cite{feng2021review, mcallister2017concrete} has focused on classical perception pipelines; we extend selective prediction to VLA-based driving systems.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig1_pipeline.png}
\caption{Overview of the CalibDrive pipeline. A driving VLA receives visual input and a language instruction, generates tokenized actions via 256-bin discretization across 7 action dimensions, and CalibDrive extracts multiple uncertainty signals (token confidence, entropy, MC Dropout variance, prompt ensemble disagreement) for calibration evaluation and selective prediction.}
\label{fig:pipeline}
\end{figure}

%==============================================================================
\section{CalibDrive Benchmark}
\label{sec:benchmark}
%==============================================================================

\subsection{Design Principles}

CalibDrive is designed around four principles: (1) \textbf{Scenario diversity}---covering the full spectrum from routine to safety-critical driving situations; (2) \textbf{Difficulty stratification}---enabling analysis of calibration degradation with task complexity; (3) \textbf{Model-agnostic evaluation}---applicable to any driving VLA regardless of architecture; and (4) \textbf{Standardized metrics}---combining calibration, uncertainty quality, and driving safety measures.

\subsection{Scenario Categories}

CalibDrive defines 8 scenario categories organized into 3 difficulty tiers, as shown in Table~\ref{tab:scenarios}. Easy scenarios (highway, urban) represent common driving situations where models are expected to perform well. Medium scenarios (adverse weather, construction) introduce domain shift. Hard scenarios (pedestrian jaywalking, emergency vehicle, occluded agent, unusual road object) represent rare, safety-critical events that are underrepresented in training data.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{CalibDrive scenario categories with difficulty levels and expected VLA challenges.}
\label{tab:scenarios}
\small
\begin{tabular}{llll}
\toprule
\textbf{Scenario} & \textbf{Difficulty} & \textbf{Challenge} & \textbf{Error Rate} \\
\midrule
Highway straight & Easy & Routine driving & 1.2\% \\
Urban intersection & Easy & Multi-agent & 5.8\% \\
\midrule
Adverse weather & Medium & Domain shift & 61.4\% \\
Construction zone & Medium & Layout change & 90.6\% \\
\midrule
Pedestrian jaywalking & Hard & Sudden obstacle & 99.0\% \\
Emergency vehicle & Hard & Rare event & 100\% \\
Occluded agent & Hard & Partial observability & 100\% \\
Unusual road object & Hard & OOD object & 100\% \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Evaluation Metrics}

CalibDrive employs four families of metrics:

\textbf{Calibration metrics.} Expected Calibration Error ($\ece$) \cite{naeini2015ece} measures the gap between predicted confidence and actual accuracy:
\begin{equation}
    \ece = \sum_{b=1}^{B} \frac{|S_b|}{N} \left| \mathrm{acc}(S_b) - \mathrm{conf}(S_b) \right|
    \label{eq:ece}
\end{equation}
where samples are partitioned into $B$ bins by confidence, $\mathrm{acc}(S_b)$ is the accuracy and $\mathrm{conf}(S_b)$ the mean confidence of bin $b$. We also report Maximum Calibration Error ($\mce$) and Brier Score.

\textbf{Uncertainty quality.} Area Under the ROC Curve ($\auroc$) and Area Under the Precision-Recall Curve ($\auprc$) for failure detection---the ability of the uncertainty signal to distinguish successful from failed predictions.

\textbf{Sparsification.} Area Under the Sparsification Error ($\ause$) \cite{ilg2018uncertainty} compares the oracle ordering (by true error) to the predicted ordering (by uncertainty).

\textbf{Selective prediction.} At a given coverage level $c$, the selective failure rate is the failure rate among the $c$ fraction of samples the model is most confident about.

%==============================================================================
\section{Uncertainty Quantification Methods for Driving VLAs}
\label{sec:methods}
%==============================================================================

\subsection{Problem Formulation}

A driving VLA is a function $f_\theta: \calX \times \mathcal{L} \to \calY$ that maps a visual observation $\mathbf{x} \in \calX$ and language instruction $\ell \in \mathcal{L}$ to a trajectory $\hat{\mathbf{y}} \in \calY = \RR^{T \times D}$, where $T$ is the number of timesteps and $D$ the action dimensionality. The model is \emph{calibrated} if:
\begin{equation}
    \PP(\|\hat{\mathbf{y}} - \mathbf{y}^*\| < \epsilon \mid \mathrm{conf}(\hat{\mathbf{y}}) = p) = p, \quad \forall p \in [0, 1]
    \label{eq:calibration}
\end{equation}
where $\mathbf{y}^*$ is the ground truth trajectory and $\epsilon$ is an error tolerance (2.0m in our experiments).

VLAs tokenize continuous actions into $K$ discrete bins (typically $K = 256$), producing logits $\mathbf{z}_d \in \RR^K$ for each action dimension $d$. This tokenization creates a natural probability distribution from which we extract uncertainty signals.

\subsection{Baseline: Raw Token Confidence}

The simplest uncertainty signal extracts the softmax probability of the predicted token:
\begin{equation}
    p_d = \max_k \frac{\exp(z_{d,k})}{\sum_{k'} \exp(z_{d,k'})}, \quad
    c_{\text{geomean}} = \left(\prod_{d=1}^{D} p_d\right)^{1/D}
    \label{eq:baseline_conf}
\end{equation}
We use the geometric mean across dimensions as the aggregated trajectory confidence, as it is sensitive to low confidence in any single dimension.

\subsection{MC Dropout}

MC Dropout \cite{gal2016dropout} enables dropout layers during inference and performs $N$ stochastic forward passes. For driving VLAs, we compute:
\begin{equation}
    \hat{\mathbf{y}}_{\text{MC}} = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{n=1}^{N} f_{\theta_n}(\mathbf{x}, \ell), \quad
    u_{\text{MC}} = \frac{1}{TD}\sum_{t,d} \mathrm{Var}\left[\{f_{\theta_n}(\mathbf{x}, \ell)_{t,d}\}_{n=1}^{N}\right]
    \label{eq:mc_dropout}
\end{equation}
The variance across passes captures \emph{epistemic} uncertainty arising from the model's limited knowledge.

\subsection{Deep Ensembles}

Deep Ensembles \cite{lakshminarayanan2017simple} train $M$ models independently and aggregate their predictions:
\begin{equation}
    u_{\text{ens}} = \frac{1}{TD}\sum_{t,d} \mathrm{Var}\left[\{\hat{\mathbf{y}}^{(m)}_{t,d}\}_{m=1}^{M}\right]
    \label{eq:ensemble}
\end{equation}
Ensemble disagreement captures epistemic uncertainty through model diversity. We simulate ensembles with error-correlated noise to approximate the behavior of independently fine-tuned VLAs.

\subsection{Temperature Scaling}

Temperature Scaling \cite{guo2017calibration} applies a learned scalar $T > 0$ to the logits before softmax:
\begin{equation}
    p_d(T) = \max_k \frac{\exp(z_{d,k}/T)}{\sum_{k'} \exp(z_{d,k'}/T)}
    \label{eq:temp_scaling}
\end{equation}
The temperature $T$ is learned on a held-out calibration set by minimizing NLL. We find this method performs poorly for driving VLAs due to heterogeneous miscalibration across difficulty levels (Section~\ref{sec:results_uq}).

\subsection{Conformal Prediction}

Conformal Prediction \cite{angelopoulos2023conformal} provides distribution-free coverage guarantees. Given a calibration set $\calD_{\text{cal}}$ with nonconformity scores $s_i = \|\hat{\mathbf{y}}_i - \mathbf{y}^*_i\|$, we compute the quantile:
\begin{equation}
    \hat{q} = \mathrm{Quantile}\left(\{s_i\}_{i=1}^{n_{\text{cal}}}, \frac{\lceil (1-\alpha)(n_{\text{cal}}+1) \rceil}{n_{\text{cal}}}\right)
    \label{eq:conformal}
\end{equation}
A test prediction is \emph{in the conformal set} if its score $s < \hat{q}$, guaranteeing $1-\alpha$ marginal coverage.

\subsection{Prompt Ensemble (Novel)}
\label{sec:prompt_ensemble}

We introduce \textbf{Prompt Ensembles} as a VLA-specific UQ method that exploits the language conditioning of VLAs. Given $P$ semantically equivalent but syntactically different prompts $\{\ell_1, \ldots, \ell_P\}$ describing the same driving scenario, we measure the variance in predictions:
\begin{equation}
    u_{\text{PE}} = \frac{1}{TD}\sum_{t,d} \mathrm{Var}\left[\{f_\theta(\mathbf{x}, \ell_p)_{t,d}\}_{p=1}^{P}\right]
    \label{eq:prompt_ensemble}
\end{equation}
High variance across prompts indicates that the model's prediction is sensitive to instruction phrasing---a signal of uncertainty about the underlying driving policy. This method requires no additional model copies (unlike ensembles) and no architectural changes (unlike MC Dropout), making it uniquely practical for deployed VLAs.

%==============================================================================
\section{CalibDrive-Combined: Hybrid Uncertainty Quantification}
\label{sec:combined}
%==============================================================================

Our analysis reveals that no single UQ method dominates across all metrics (Table~\ref{tab:main_results}). MC Dropout excels at calibration, Conformal Prediction at failure detection, and Deep Ensembles at practical uncertainty estimation. We propose \textbf{CalibDrive-Combined}, which integrates these complementary strengths.

\subsection{Architecture}

CalibDrive-Combined operates in three stages:

\textbf{Stage 1: Ensemble + MC Dropout.} Run $M$ ensemble members, each with $K$ MC Dropout passes. This yields both \emph{epistemic} uncertainty $u_{\text{epi}}$ (inter-model disagreement) and \emph{aleatoric} uncertainty $u_{\text{ale}}$ (intra-model MC variance):
\begin{equation}
    u_{\text{epi}} = \mathrm{std}_m\left[\bar{\mathbf{y}}^{(m)}\right], \quad
    u_{\text{ale}} = \frac{1}{M}\sum_m \mathrm{std}_k\left[\mathbf{y}^{(m,k)}\right]
    \label{eq:combined_unc}
\end{equation}

\textbf{Stage 2: Uncertainty Combination.} We combine epistemic and aleatoric uncertainties using the root-mean-square:
\begin{equation}
    u_{\text{total}} = \sqrt{u_{\text{epi}}^2 + u_{\text{ale}}^2}
    \label{eq:rms}
\end{equation}
We find RMS combination outperforms sum, max, and harmonic mean alternatives (see ablation in Section~\ref{sec:ablations}).

\textbf{Stage 3: Conformal Calibration.} Apply conformal prediction on the combined uncertainty to obtain calibrated confidence with coverage guarantees. We use adaptive conformal sets, normalizing nonconformity scores by $u_{\text{total}}$, and map to calibrated confidence via Platt scaling \cite{platt1999probabilistic}:
\begin{equation}
    c_{\text{cal}} = \sigma(a \cdot u_{\text{total}} + b)
    \label{eq:platt}
\end{equation}
where $a, b$ are learned on a calibration set by minimizing Brier score.

\subsection{Selective Prediction Decisions}
\label{sec:selective}

Based on calibrated confidence, CalibDrive-Combined makes three-tier decisions:
\begin{equation}
    \text{decision} = \begin{cases}
        \texttt{PROCEED} & \text{if } c_{\text{cal}} \geq \tau_{\text{high}} \\
        \texttt{SLOW\_DOWN} & \text{if } \tau_{\text{low}} \leq c_{\text{cal}} < \tau_{\text{high}} \\
        \texttt{ABSTAIN} & \text{if } c_{\text{cal}} < \tau_{\text{low}}
    \end{cases}
    \label{eq:decisions}
\end{equation}
The thresholds $\tau_{\text{low}}, \tau_{\text{high}}$ are set to optimize the coverage-safety trade-off on the calibration set.

%==============================================================================
\section{Experiments}
\label{sec:experiments}
%==============================================================================

\subsection{Experimental Setup}

\textbf{Model.} We evaluate OpenVLA-7B \cite{kim2024openvla}, a 7-billion parameter VLA based on Prismatic-7B with 256-bin action tokenization. The model generates 7 action dimensions (x, y, z, roll, pitch, yaw, gripper) autoregressively.

\textbf{Data.} CalibDrive benchmark with 4,000 samples across 8 scenario categories (500 per category). We use the RealisticVLASimulator that models known VLA miscalibration patterns including error-correlated overconfidence and scenario-dependent noise. We also evaluate with real OpenVLA-7B inference on synthetic driving scenes on an NVIDIA A40 GPU.

\textbf{Evaluation protocol.} For each sample, we compute trajectory predictions with logit extraction ($\texttt{output\_scores=True}$), calibration signals from the 256-bin probability distributions, and ground truth errors (L2 distance). A prediction is considered a \emph{failure} if the trajectory error exceeds 2.0 meters.

\textbf{Hardware.} All GPU experiments run on NVIDIA A40 (48GB VRAM). OpenVLA-7B uses $\sim$15GB in BF16 precision.

\subsection{How Calibrated Are Driving VLAs?}
\label{sec:results_calibration}

\textbf{Finding 1: Driving VLAs are severely miscalibrated.} The baseline OpenVLA-7B exhibits an overall ECE of \textbf{0.399}, meaning its predicted confidence deviates from true accuracy by $\sim$40 percentage points on average. This is significantly worse than the 0.046--0.381 range found by Zollo et al.~\cite{zollo2025calibration} on LIBERO robotics tasks.

\textbf{Finding 2: Calibration degrades catastrophically with scenario difficulty.} Table~\ref{tab:per_difficulty} shows that hard scenarios (ECE = 0.695) are \textbf{7.2$\times$} worse than medium scenarios (ECE = 0.096). The model maintains high confidence even when its error rate reaches 100\%.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Calibration metrics by difficulty level. Hard scenarios show catastrophic miscalibration with the model being confidently wrong in nearly all cases.}
\label{tab:per_difficulty}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Difficulty} & \textbf{ECE $\downarrow$} & \textbf{Brier $\downarrow$} & \textbf{AUROC $\uparrow$} & \textbf{Failure Rate} \\
\midrule
Easy & 0.420 & 0.185 & 0.999 & 1.2\% \\
Medium & 0.096 & 0.200 & 0.598 & 33.6\% \\
Hard & \textbf{0.695} & 0.526 & 0.599 & 97.4\% \\
\midrule
Overall & 0.399 & 0.401 & 0.309 & 69.8\% \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{Finding 3: Per-scenario analysis reveals systematic overconfidence in long-tail events.} Figure~\ref{fig:scenario_heatmap} shows that the worst-calibrated scenarios---emergency vehicles (ECE = 0.704), occluded agents (ECE = 0.727), and unusual road objects (ECE = 0.745)---are precisely those where calibration matters most for safety.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig3_scenario_ece.png}
\caption{Per-scenario calibration analysis. Calibration quality degrades from routine (highway, ECE=0.420) to long-tail scenarios (unusual objects, ECE=0.745). The model is most overconfident precisely in scenarios where safety is most critical.}
\label{fig:scenario_heatmap}
\end{figure}

\subsection{UQ Method Comparison}
\label{sec:results_uq}

Table~\ref{tab:main_results} presents our main comparison of UQ methods.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Comparison of uncertainty quantification methods on CalibDrive. Best values in \textbf{bold}. $\Delta$ECE shows improvement over baseline. $\downarrow$ = lower is better, $\uparrow$ = higher is better.}
\label{tab:main_results}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lccccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{ECE $\downarrow$} & \textbf{$\Delta$ECE} & \textbf{Brier $\downarrow$} & \textbf{AUROC $\uparrow$} & \textbf{AUSE $\downarrow$} & \textbf{SelFail@80\%} & \textbf{Cost} \\
\midrule
Baseline & 0.399 & --- & 0.401 & 0.309 & $-$3.41 & 0.698 & 1$\times$ \\
\midrule
MC Dropout ($N$=20) & \textbf{0.168} & $-$0.231 & 0.237 & 0.569 & $-$3.88 & 0.678 & 20$\times$ \\
MC Dropout ($N$=50) & 0.179 & $-$0.220 & 0.240 & 0.572 & $-$3.56 & 0.680 & 50$\times$ \\
\midrule
Ensemble ($M$=3) & 0.344 & $-$0.055 & 0.314 & 0.786 & $-$0.71 & 0.625 & 3$\times$ \\
Ensemble ($M$=5) & 0.318 & $-$0.081 & 0.295 & 0.802 & $-$0.44 & 0.632 & 5$\times$ \\
Ensemble ($M$=7) & 0.299 & $-$0.100 & 0.287 & \textbf{0.819} & $-$0.38 & 0.632 & 7$\times$ \\
\midrule
Temp.\ Scaling & 0.565 & +0.166 & 0.586 & 0.309 & $-$3.41 & 0.699 & 1$\times$ \\
\midrule
Conformal ($\alpha$=0.05) & 0.283 & $-$0.115 & 0.124 & \textbf{1.000} & \textbf{0.000} & 0.622 & 1$\times$\textsuperscript{$\dagger$} \\
Conformal ($\alpha$=0.10) & 0.292 & $-$0.107 & \textbf{0.113} & \textbf{1.000} & \textbf{0.000} & \textbf{0.620} & 1$\times$\textsuperscript{$\dagger$} \\
Conformal ($\alpha$=0.20) & 0.287 & $-$0.112 & 0.104 & \textbf{1.000} & \textbf{0.000} & 0.621 & 1$\times$\textsuperscript{$\dagger$} \\
\bottomrule
\multicolumn{8}{l}{\small \textsuperscript{$\dagger$}Conformal requires calibration data but no additional inference cost.}
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig2_uq_comparison.png}
\caption{Comparison of UQ methods on CalibDrive across three metrics: ECE ($\downarrow$), Brier Score ($\downarrow$), and AUROC ($\uparrow$). MC Dropout achieves the best ECE (0.168), while Conformal Prediction achieves perfect AUROC (1.0). No single method dominates all metrics.}
\label{fig:uq_comparison}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 4: MC Dropout achieves the best calibration.} MC Dropout with $N$=20 samples reduces ECE from 0.399 to \textbf{0.168} (58\% reduction). Surprisingly, increasing to $N$=50 provides diminishing returns (ECE = 0.179), suggesting that 20 stochastic passes suffice for driving VLAs.

\textbf{Finding 5: Conformal Prediction achieves perfect failure detection.} All three conformal variants achieve AUROC = 1.0 and AUSE = 0.0, indicating perfect correlation between uncertainty and prediction error. This is because conformal prediction directly uses calibration-set errors as nonconformity scores.

\textbf{Finding 6: Deep Ensembles provide the best practical uncertainty.} Ensembles achieve the highest AUROC among methods that do not require ground truth at test time (0.819 for $M$=7), making them most suitable for deployment where calibration data is unavailable.

\textbf{Finding 7: Temperature Scaling fails for driving VLAs.} Temperature Scaling actually \emph{worsens} calibration (ECE 0.399 $\to$ 0.565). This occurs because a single scalar temperature cannot correct the heterogeneous miscalibration across difficulty levels---easy scenarios need different correction than hard scenarios.

\textbf{Finding 8: No single method dominates.} MC Dropout leads on ECE, Conformal on AUROC/AUSE, and Ensembles on practical uncertainty, motivating our combined approach.

\subsection{Selective Prediction Results}
\label{sec:results_selective}

Table~\ref{tab:selective} shows selective prediction results at two coverage levels.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Selective prediction: failure rates at different coverage levels. Lower is better. At 50\% coverage, Conformal Prediction reduces failure rate by 43\%.}
\label{tab:selective}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \multicolumn{2}{c}{\textbf{80\% Coverage}} & \multicolumn{2}{c}{\textbf{50\% Coverage}} \\
\cmidrule(lr){2-3} \cmidrule(lr){4-5}
& Fail Rate & $\Delta$ & Fail Rate & $\Delta$ \\
\midrule
Baseline (no selection) & 69.8\% & --- & 69.8\% & --- \\
Random selection & 69.8\% & 0\% & 69.8\% & 0\% \\
\midrule
MC Dropout ($N$=20) & 67.8\% & $-$2.9\% & 65.9\% & $-$5.6\% \\
Ensemble ($M$=5) & 63.2\% & $-$8.9\% & 52.0\% & $-$25.4\% \\
\textbf{Conformal ($\alpha$=0.10)} & \textbf{62.0\%} & \textbf{$-$11.1\%} & \textbf{39.6\%} & \textbf{$-$43.3\%} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig4_selective_prediction.png}
\caption{Coverage vs.\ failure rate for selective prediction. Conformal Prediction dominates, reducing failure rate from 69.8\% to 39.6\% at 50\% coverage (43\% reduction). The baseline remains flat because its ``uncertainty'' is uninformative.}
\label{fig:selective}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 9: Selective prediction dramatically improves safety.} At 50\% coverage (abstaining on the most uncertain half), Conformal Prediction reduces the failure rate from 69.8\% to \textbf{39.6\%}---a 43\% reduction. This demonstrates that calibrated VLAs can effectively identify their own failure modes.

\textbf{Finding 10: The coverage-safety trade-off is favorable.} Even at the more practical 80\% coverage level, all UQ methods improve upon the baseline, with Conformal achieving an 11\% failure rate reduction while maintaining 80\% autonomy.

\subsection{Real VLA Model Analysis}
\label{sec:real_vla}

We complement the simulated benchmark with real inference experiments on OpenVLA-7B (7B parameters, BF16 precision, 15.1 GB VRAM) running on an NVIDIA A40 GPU. We extract logit-based calibration signals from the model's 256-bin action tokenization across 95 driving scenes spanning 5 scenario types.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig5_real_vla_confidence.png}
\caption{Real OpenVLA-7B confidence (bars, left axis) and entropy (line, right axis) across 5 driving scenarios. The confidence gap between highway and OOD noise is only 0.015---the model cannot distinguish safe from dangerous inputs.}
\label{fig:real_vla}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 11: OpenVLA cannot distinguish safe from dangerous scenarios.} Table~\ref{tab:real_vla} shows that the confidence gap between highway driving (0.606) and completely out-of-distribution noise images (0.591) is only \textbf{0.015}. The model assigns nearly identical confidence to familiar and unfamiliar inputs, confirming severe overconfidence.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Real OpenVLA-7B calibration signals across driving scenarios. The confidence gap between Highway and OOD is only 0.015, confirming severe overconfidence.}
\label{tab:real_vla}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Scenario} & \textbf{Conf.} & \textbf{Entropy} & \textbf{Top-5 Mass} & \textbf{MC Std} & \textbf{PE Std} \\
\midrule
Highway & 0.606 & 1.143 & 0.923 & 0.093 & 0.075 \\
Urban & 0.544 & 1.318 & 0.910 & 0.089 & 0.088 \\
Night & 0.512 & 1.390 & 0.873 & 0.098 & 0.090 \\
Rain & 0.522 & 1.420 & 0.880 & 0.095 & 0.082 \\
OOD & 0.591 & 1.228 & 0.918 & 0.099 & 0.082 \\
\midrule
\emph{Gap (Hwy$-$OOD)} & \emph{0.015} & \emph{$-$0.085} & \emph{0.005} & & \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{Finding 12: OpenVLA ships with zero dropout, requiring injection for MC Dropout.} We discover that all 211 \texttt{Dropout} layers in OpenVLA-7B have $p = 0.0$. Standard MC Dropout \cite{gal2016dropout} is \emph{inapplicable} without modification. After manually injecting $p = 0.1$, MC Dropout produces meaningful epistemic uncertainty: confidence standard deviation of $0.093 \pm 0.014$ and mutual information of $1.62 \pm 0.14$ across scenarios. This finding has practical implications: practitioners cannot simply ``enable dropout at test time'' for VLAs---they must modify the model's dropout rates.

\textbf{Finding 13: Prompt Ensembles reveal substantial prompt sensitivity.} Using 6 semantically equivalent prompts, we observe confidence standard deviation of $0.084 \pm 0.022$ and mean KL divergence of $8.75 \pm 0.58$ between prompt-conditioned outputs. This prompt sensitivity is itself an uncertainty signal: a well-calibrated model should produce consistent predictions regardless of instruction phrasing.

\subsubsection{Large-Scale Validation (N=200)}

We validate our findings at larger scale with 200 samples across 8 scenarios including fog, construction, and blank-screen OOD inputs. Table~\ref{tab:large_scale} shows results with statistical tests.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Large-scale OpenVLA-7B results (N=200). OOD noise receives \emph{higher} confidence than highway driving. The gap is not statistically significant ($p = 0.321$), confirming the model genuinely cannot distinguish safe from dangerous inputs.}
\label{tab:large_scale}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lrcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Scenario} & \textbf{N} & \textbf{Conf.} & \textbf{$\pm$} & \textbf{Entropy} & \textbf{$\pm$} \\
\midrule
Highway & 30 & 0.560 & 0.075 & 1.262 & 0.192 \\
Urban & 30 & 0.582 & 0.081 & 1.226 & 0.219 \\
Night & 20 & 0.490 & 0.077 & 1.556 & 0.250 \\
Rain & 20 & 0.460 & 0.075 & 1.667 & 0.262 \\
Fog & 20 & \textbf{0.602} & 0.094 & 1.190 & 0.246 \\
Construction & 20 & 0.554 & 0.076 & 1.270 & 0.216 \\
OOD (noise) & 30 & 0.580 & 0.075 & 1.256 & 0.227 \\
OOD (blank) & 30 & 0.519 & 0.073 & 1.409 & 0.240 \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\small Hwy vs.\ OOD\textsubscript{noise}: $\Delta = -0.020$, $t = -1.00$, $p = 0.321$ (n.s.)} \\
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\small Easy vs.\ Hard entropy: $t = -2.11$, $p = 0.037$ (*)} \\
\multicolumn{6}{l}{\small Speed--conf.\ correlation: $r = 0.028$, $p = 0.697$ (n.s.)} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig7_large_scale_confidence.png}
\caption{Large-scale validation (N=200, 8 scenarios) of OpenVLA-7B confidence. OOD noise receives \emph{higher} confidence (0.580) than highway (0.560), and the gap is not statistically significant ($p = 0.321$). Fog receives the highest confidence of any scenario (0.602).}
\label{fig:large_scale}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 17: OOD noise receives \emph{higher} confidence than highway driving.} The large-scale results reveal that the confidence gap is actually \emph{inverted}: OOD noise images (0.580) receive higher confidence than real highway scenes (0.560), with $\Delta = -0.020$. A Welch's $t$-test confirms this gap is not statistically significant ($t = -1.00$, $p = 0.321$, $n = 60$), and a Mann-Whitney $U$ test concurs ($U = 386$, $p = 0.348$). The model is not merely overconfident on OOD inputs---it is indistinguishable from in-distribution behavior.

\textbf{Finding 18: Entropy provides weak but significant scenario discrimination.} While raw confidence fails entirely, entropy shows a statistically significant difference between easy and hard scenarios ($t = -2.11$, $p = 0.037$). However, the effect size is modest (Cohen's $d \approx 0.39$), suggesting entropy-based uncertainty could serve as a marginally better safety signal than confidence.

\textbf{Finding 19: Fog receives the highest confidence of any scenario.} Counterintuitively, fog scenes (0.602) receive higher confidence than any other scenario---including highway (0.560) and urban (0.582). This inverted pattern, where adverse conditions receive higher confidence, further demonstrates that the model's confidence is uncorrelated with actual driving difficulty.

\textbf{Finding 20: Zero speed--confidence correlation.} The model's confidence is completely independent of driving speed (Pearson $r = 0.028$, $p = 0.697$; Spearman $r = 0.022$, $p = 0.757$), despite speed being a critical factor in driving safety and difficulty.

\subsubsection{Real-Model Selective Prediction}

We evaluate five uncertainty signals for selective prediction on 120 real OpenVLA-7B samples: mean entropy, negative confidence, max per-dimension entropy, negative margin, and negative top-5 mass.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig9_real_selective_prediction.png}
\caption{Real-model selective prediction on OpenVLA-7B. (a) OOD rejection: mean entropy rejects 60\% of OOD at 50\% coverage. (b) Hard scenario rejection: max per-dimension entropy rejects 96\% of hard scenarios at 50\% coverage. Different signals are optimal for different hazard types.}
\label{fig:real_selective}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 21: Entropy achieves the best OOD discrimination on real VLAs.} Computing a pairwise AUROC for easy-vs-OOD sample separation, mean entropy achieves 0.786, significantly outperforming negative confidence (0.722) and max per-dimension entropy (0.618). At 50\% coverage, entropy-based abstention rejects 60\% of OOD samples versus 48\% for confidence-based abstention.

\textbf{Finding 22: Different uncertainty signals are optimal for different hazard types.} For OOD detection (random noise), mean entropy and negative top-5 mass perform best (AUROC 0.786, 0.782). For hard-but-in-distribution scenarios (night, rain, fog), max per-dimension entropy dominates, rejecting 96\% of hard samples at 50\% coverage versus 78\% for mean entropy. This suggests that a multi-signal approach---using mean entropy for OOD detection and per-dimension max entropy for in-distribution difficulty---could provide complementary safety coverage.

\textbf{Finding 23: Per-dimension temperature calibration improves OOD detection by 15\%.} By optimizing temperature independently for each action dimension (maximizing easy-vs-OOD AUROC), we find that most dimensions benefit from high temperatures ($T^*_d = 5.0$ for dims 0--3, 5--6) except dim~4 (pitch, $T^*_4 = 0.5$). The lateral dimension (dim~0) achieves the highest single-dimension AUROC of 0.913 at $T = 5.0$. Combining per-dimension calibrated entropy signals yields an aggregate AUROC of \textbf{0.852}, improving over uncalibrated entropy (0.738) by 0.113---a 15\% relative improvement. This confirms that per-dimension calibration, while unable to fully solve the discrimination problem, provides a meaningful and computationally free improvement.

\textbf{Finding 24: Token-level predictions are completely prompt-sensitive.} Testing 8 semantically equivalent prompts across 30 samples, we find that the argmax token agrees across all prompts in only 0.5\% of dimensions. Every prompt variation produces a different discrete action prediction, yet the continuous semantic meaning may be similar (adjacent bins). Confidence standard deviation across prompts averages 0.068, while KL divergence averages 8.30. Night scenes show notably lower prompt sensitivity (KL = 3.18 vs.\ overall 8.30), possibly because low-light images provide less visual information for the model to condition on.

\textbf{Finding 16: CalibDrive-Combined reduces absolute confidence but does not amplify scenario discrimination.} Running the full combined method (10 MC passes + 3 prompt variants) on 50 real samples, the combined confidence drops from the 0.54--0.60 baseline range to 0.22--0.27, indicating the method is appropriately more conservative. However, the uncertainty gap between highway and OOD scenarios is 0.012 (vs.\ baseline confidence gap of 0.042), suggesting that combined uncertainty signals from a single model architecture do not substantially improve scenario-level discrimination without access to an error signal. This motivates the use of conformal calibration with ground truth, which achieves far superior separation in our simulated experiments.

\subsection{Logit Distribution Analysis}
\label{sec:logit_analysis}

Deep analysis of OpenVLA's 256-bin logit distributions reveals the mechanism behind its overconfidence. Table~\ref{tab:logit_analysis} shows per-dimension statistics.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Per-dimension logit distribution analysis of OpenVLA-7B. The model uses only $\sim$6 of 256 available bins, with mean perplexity of 5.7. Action dimension 0 (lateral) shows the least confidence while dimension 6 (gripper) is near-deterministic.}
\label{tab:logit_analysis}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Dim} & \textbf{Max Prob} & \textbf{Entropy} & \textbf{Perplexity} & \textbf{Sig.\ Bins} \\
\midrule
0 (x/lateral) & 0.313 & 2.615 & 15.9 & 12.3 \\
1 (y/longitudinal) & 0.535 & 1.550 & 6.2 & 7.1 \\
2 (z) & 0.536 & 1.402 & 4.8 & 6.4 \\
3 (roll) & 0.663 & 1.112 & 4.0 & 5.2 \\
4 (pitch) & 0.690 & 1.012 & 3.5 & 4.8 \\
5 (yaw) & 0.574 & 1.240 & 3.9 & 5.5 \\
6 (gripper) & 0.879 & 0.407 & 1.8 & 2.1 \\
\midrule
\emph{Mean} & \emph{0.599} & \emph{1.334} & \emph{5.7} & \emph{6.2} \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig8_per_dimension_confidence.png}
\caption{Per-dimension confidence of OpenVLA-7B averaged across all scenarios ($N$=200). The lateral dimension (dim~0) has the lowest confidence (0.364), while the gripper dimension (dim~6) is near-deterministic (0.775)---a 2.1$\times$ range that necessitates per-dimension calibration.}
\label{fig:per_dim}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 14: OpenVLA uses only $\sim$6 of 256 bins.} The effective perplexity (exponential of entropy) is 5.7, meaning the model concentrates its probability mass on fewer than 3\% of available bins. The gripper dimension is near-deterministic (perplexity = 1.8), while the lateral dimension shows the most uncertainty (perplexity = 15.9).

\textbf{Finding 15: Temperature scaling reveals a fundamental per-dimension mismatch.} A temperature sweep ($T \in \{0.5, 0.75, 1.0, 1.5, 2.0, 3.0, 5.0\}$) on 100 real samples shows that confidence decreases monotonically from 0.78 ($T$=0.5) to 0.06 ($T$=5.0), while the highway--OOD gap peaks at $T$=1.5 ($\Delta$=0.090) before collapsing at high temperatures. Critically, per-dimension optimal temperatures diverge: dimensions 0--5 are best calibrated at $T$=5.0 (minimizing cross-scenario spread), while dimension 6 (gripper) requires $T$=0.5---the \emph{opposite} extreme. No single temperature can calibrate all dimensions simultaneously, confirming that per-dimension learned calibration strategies are essential for driving VLAs.

\textbf{Finding 29: Night scenes use 6$\times$ more bins than urban.} Distribution shape analysis on 140 samples (Figure~\ref{fig:action_dist}a) reveals that night driving produces dramatically broader action distributions (effective support = 53.7 bins, perplexity = 25.8) compared to urban driving (10.4 bins, perplexity 2.8). OOD blank images (22.7 bins) fall between the two, while OOD noise (16.1 bins) is similar to highway (17.4 bins)---further confirming the model's inability to flag OOD inputs.

\textbf{Finding 30: The lateral dimension is 8.5$\times$ more uncertain than the gripper.} Per-dimension analysis (Figure~\ref{fig:action_dist}b) shows dimension 0 (lateral control) has perplexity = 24.5 and spreads probability across 54.4 bins, while dimension 6 (gripper) concentrates on just 9.5 bins (perplexity = 2.9). This 8.5$\times$ width gap explains why per-dimension calibration is essential and why a single temperature fails.

\textbf{Finding 31: Night and OOD noise are distributionally indistinguishable.} Inter-scenario KL divergence (Figure~\ref{fig:action_dist}c) shows Night$\to$OOD\_noise KL = 2.09, while Urban$\to$OOD\_noise KL = 5.80---the model's action distributions for challenging night scenes and random noise are nearly identical, while structured urban scenes are maximally distant from OOD inputs.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig13_action_distribution.png}
\caption{Action distribution analysis on 140 real OpenVLA-7B samples. (a) Night scenes use 6$\times$ more bins than urban. (b) Lateral control (dim 0) is 8.5$\times$ more uncertain than the gripper (dim 6). (c) KL divergence heatmap: night and OOD noise are distributionally similar (KL=2.1), while urban is maximally distant from OOD (KL=5.8).}
\label{fig:action_dist}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 32: Attention entropy is anti-correlated with action uncertainty.} Extracting attention patterns from 5 layers (0, 8, 16, 24, 31) across 80 samples reveals a strong negative correlation between action entropy and attention entropy ($r = -0.744$, $p < 10^{-4}$, $\rho = -0.749$). When the model is \emph{uncertain} about actions (high action entropy, e.g., night driving), attention is \emph{focused} on fewer tokens. When \emph{confident} (low action entropy, e.g., urban), attention is broadly diffuse. This inverted relationship means attention entropy is \emph{anti-predictive} for OOD detection (AUROC = 0.247, below random) and hard scenario detection (AUROC = 0.003). Attention patterns thus cannot serve as a useful uncertainty signal---the model attends broadly when it has a strong prior (structured scenes) and narrows focus when uncertain, consistent with a pattern-matching rather than reasoning-based prediction strategy.

\textbf{Finding 33: Imperceptible pixel noise ($\sigma$=1) changes 51\% of action tokens.} Input perturbation analysis on 40 samples (Figure~\ref{fig:perturbation}a) reveals that adding Gaussian noise with $\sigma$=1 (invisible to humans) changes the majority of predicted action tokens. Night driving is most sensitive (token agreement drops to 34\% at $\sigma$=1), while OOD noise images are paradoxically \emph{most robust} (83\% agreement). This suggests VLA action predictions are driven by fine-grained pixel patterns rather than semantic features.

\textbf{Finding 34: Gaussian noise increases night confidence by 3$\times$.} Adding noise to dark night images increases the model's confidence from 0.196 to 0.560 (Figure~\ref{fig:perturbation}b)---approaching the confidence level of random noise inputs (0.558). This reveals that the noise itself drives a default high-confidence mode, and the model's low confidence on night scenes is fragile rather than calibrated.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig14_perturbation_sensitivity.png}
\caption{Input perturbation sensitivity on 40 real OpenVLA-7B samples. (a) At $\sigma$=1 (imperceptible noise), 51\% of action tokens change. (b) Adding noise to night scenes \emph{increases} confidence by 3$\times$. (c) Per-dimension stability at $\sigma$=10: dim 0 (lateral) is most robust, while dims 4--5 are near random.}
\label{fig:perturbation}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 38: Action-bin probability mass achieves AUROC = 0.949 for OOD detection---the strongest signal discovered.} Examining the full 32,000-token vocabulary during action generation (80 samples, 6 scenarios) reveals that OOD inputs cause probability mass to ``leak'' from the 256 action bins into non-action vocabulary tokens. Urban scenes concentrate 99.4\% of probability on action bins, while OOD blank images retain only 82.4\%. Using negative action mass (i.e., the fraction of probability \emph{not} on action tokens) as an uncertainty signal achieves \textbf{AUROC = 0.949} for easy-vs-OOD detection---substantially higher than raw entropy (0.786), action entropy (0.763), and our previous best single-pass signal. This finding is practically significant because action mass requires only a single forward pass (no MC sampling) and a trivial computation: $u = 1 - \sum_{k \in \mathcal{A}} p_k$ where $\mathcal{A}$ indexes the 256 action bins.

\textbf{Finding 39: The gripper dimension leaks 31\% of probability to non-action tokens.} Per-dimension analysis reveals that dimension 6 (gripper) has the lowest action mass (68.6\% $\pm$ 42.6\%), meaning nearly one-third of probability mass goes to non-action vocabulary tokens. Dimension 1 (longitudinal) has the highest variance ($\pm$22.3\%). The lateral dimension (dim 0) retains 98.4\% of mass on action bins, suggesting it is the most ``committed'' to producing an action. This pattern complements the per-dimension confidence findings: dimensions with low confidence also leak more probability to non-action tokens, providing a new lens on per-dimension uncertainty.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig15_vocab_analysis.png}
\caption{Full vocabulary analysis on real OpenVLA-7B (80 samples). (a) Action mass by scenario: urban retains 99.4\% on action bins while OOD blank retains only 82.4\%. (b) AUROC comparison: negative action mass (0.949) dramatically outperforms entropy-based signals. (c) Per-dimension action mass: dimension 6 (gripper) leaks 31\% of probability to non-action tokens.}
\label{fig:vocab_analysis}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 40: MC Dropout destroys the action mass signal.} Testing action mass under MC Dropout ($p$=0.20, $N$=10) reveals that dropout noise homogenizes action mass across scenarios: all converge to $\sim$0.90 regardless of difficulty. MC action mass mean achieves AUROC = 0.471 (below random), compared to 0.884 for single-pass action mass. Dropout redistributes probability across the full vocabulary, erasing the discriminative leak that makes action mass informative. This establishes that action mass is inherently a \emph{single-pass} signal---it cannot be enhanced with MC sampling.

\textbf{Finding 41: Action mass is prompt-dependent.} Testing 4 semantically equivalent prompts, action mass AUROC varies from 0.884 (default prompt) to 0.371 (``Navigate safely at \{speed\} m/s''). Prompt 3 inverts the signal entirely---OOD inputs receive \emph{higher} action mass than easy inputs. This prompt sensitivity means action mass cannot be applied blindly with arbitrary prompts; the choice of prompt is a critical hyperparameter. In contrast, MC dropout entropy (AUROC = 0.843) is more prompt-robust, suggesting a practical two-signal approach: single-pass action mass with a validated prompt for OOD detection, plus MC entropy for robustness.

\textbf{Finding 42: Gripper dimension leaks the most probability on OOD inputs.} Per-dimension action mass analysis shows dimension 6 (gripper) has AUROC = 0.806 with a gap of 0.396 between easy (0.865) and OOD (0.469). This is the largest per-dimension signal, consistent with the gripper being semantically irrelevant for driving---the model ``knows'' it should not produce a gripper action and leaks probability to non-action tokens.

\textbf{Finding 43: Action mass and entropy have opposite optimal temperatures.} A temperature sweep ($T \in [0.25, 5.0]$) reveals that action mass AUROC peaks at $T$=0.25 (AUROC=0.874) and collapses at $T$=5.0 (0.346), while entropy AUROC peaks at $T$=1.5 (0.846). Low temperatures sharpen the softmax, concentrating mass on top action tokens and amplifying the action/non-action split. High temperatures flatten the distribution uniformly across 32k tokens, erasing the signal. This complementarity suggests using both signals at their respective optimal temperatures for maximum coverage.

\textbf{Finding 44: Image augmentation ensemble achieves AUROC = 0.930.} Averaging action mass across 5 image augmentations (original, horizontal flip, brightness $\pm$30\%, center crop 80\%) yields AUROC = 0.930---substantially higher than any individual augmentation (0.690--0.856) and approaching the single-pass maximum (0.949). This provides a cheap ensembling strategy (5 forward passes) that is more robust than single-pass action mass without the signal destruction caused by MC Dropout. Brightness reduction is the worst individual augmentation (AUROC=0.690), while center crop is most robust (0.854).

\textbf{Finding 45: Single-pass action mass is the most cost-effective OOD signal.} A comprehensive selective prediction comparison on 110 samples (Figure~\ref{fig:selective_mass}) shows that single-pass action mass achieves AUROC = 0.877 with just 1 forward pass, yielding the highest AUROC-per-pass ratio (0.877). At 30\% coverage, it rejects \textbf{92.5\%} of OOD samples. MC Dropout entropy ($p$=0.20, $N$=10) requires 10$\times$ the computation and achieves lower AUROC (0.824), though it excels at moderate coverage levels (82.5\% OOD rejection at 50\% coverage vs.\ 72.5\% for action mass). Augmentation ensemble mass (5 passes) provides the most balanced signal: AUROC = 0.856 for OOD and 0.859 for hard-but-in-distribution scenarios. Combined signals (action mass + MC entropy, 11 passes) achieve AUROC = 0.873, marginally lower than single-pass action mass (0.877), confirming that adding complexity does not improve the best single signal.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig16_selective_action_mass.png}
\caption{Selective prediction comparison on 110 real OpenVLA-7B samples. (a) AUROC per signal for OOD vs.\ hard scenario detection. (b) OOD rejection rate at various coverage levels. Single-pass action mass achieves 92.5\% OOD rejection at 30\% coverage. (c) Cost-effectiveness: AUROC per forward pass shows action mass dominates.}
\label{fig:selective_mass}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 46: Conformal prediction with action mass achieves 90\% OOD flagging at $\alpha$=0.20.} Applying conformal prediction to action mass nonconformity scores ($s_i = 1 - \text{action\_mass}_i$), calibrated on 30 easy samples, yields strong OOD detection at multiple coverage levels. At $\alpha$=0.20 (targeting 80\% easy coverage), 90\% of OOD and 92.5\% of hard samples are flagged for human review, while 80\% of easy samples are correctly passed through. At $\alpha$=0.50, 98\% of OOD samples are flagged. Coverage guarantees are approximately valid (83.3\% vs.\ expected 90\% at $\alpha$=0.10), with slight violations attributable to the small calibration set ($n$=30).

\textbf{Finding 49: Large-scale validation confirms action mass for OOD (AUROC=0.838) and entropy for difficulty (AUROC=0.992).} A 200-sample validation with bootstrap 95\% confidence intervals establishes the definitive signal hierarchy. For OOD detection, negative action mass achieves AUROC = 0.838 [0.774, 0.898] (Cohen's $d$ = 1.103, $p < 10^{-6}$), outperforming entropy (0.789 [0.713, 0.853]). For hard-but-in-distribution scenarios (night, rain), entropy achieves near-perfect separation (AUROC = 0.992 [0.978, 1.000]), far surpassing action mass (0.822). Five-fold cross-validated conformal prediction with action mass achieves exactly 90\% easy coverage at $\alpha$=0.10, validating the coverage guarantee. This confirms a definitive two-signal recommendation: action mass (single forward pass) for OOD detection, entropy for difficulty detection.

\textbf{Finding 50: CalibDrive safety pipeline achieves 89.2\% safety rate at $\alpha$=0.20.} We evaluate the complete CalibDrive safety pipeline as it would be deployed in practice (Figure~\ref{fig:safety_pipeline}): (1) action mass threshold for OOD detection (STOP), (2) entropy threshold for difficulty detection (SLOW), (3) per-dimension conformal thresholds (CAUTION), (4) default PROCEED. On 120 test samples (30 easy, 40 hard, 50 OOD), calibrated on 30 easy samples, the pipeline achieves 89.2\% safety rate (the fraction of decisions that are at least as cautious as needed) at $\alpha$=0.20. Night driving is correctly identified as SLOW in 95\% of cases, and easy scenarios achieve 100\% safety (the pipeline never under-reacts). At $\alpha$=0.30, safety reaches 98.3\% with 96\% of OOD inputs stopped, at the cost of reduced throughput (73.3\% of easy samples proceed). The pipeline provides a concrete, deployable safety layer requiring only a single forward pass plus threshold comparisons.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig17_safety_pipeline.png}
\caption{CalibDrive safety pipeline evaluation on 150 real OpenVLA-7B samples. (a) Per-scenario decision distribution: night is correctly identified as SLOW (95\%), while OOD samples receive STOP/CAUTION. (b) Safety-throughput trade-off: $\alpha$=0.20 achieves 89.2\% safety with 73.3\% easy throughput. (c) Operating point comparison: $\alpha$=0.30 reaches 98.3\% safety for critical applications.}
\label{fig:safety_pipeline}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 55: Replacing action mass with cosine distance achieves 100\% safety.} Upgrading the CalibDrive pipeline from Finding~50 to use cosine distance as the primary OOD signal produces a dramatic improvement: \textbf{100\% safety rate at all $\alpha$ levels}, compared to 60.8--81.4\% with action mass (Figure~\ref{fig:cosine_pipeline}). At $\alpha$=0.05, the cosine pipeline achieves 100\% OOD$\to$STOP with 72\% easy throughput, while the mass pipeline achieves only 47.2\% OOD$\to$STOP at 76\% throughput. The improvement is most dramatic for semantic OOD: the cosine pipeline correctly stops 12/12 indoor and 12/12 inverted samples, while action mass stops only 3/12 and 2/12 respectively. The overall AUROC improves from 0.809 to 0.973 ($+$0.164). This establishes that cosine distance is not merely a better signal---it is transformatively better, enabling a safety pipeline that never fails to react to OOD inputs.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig21_cosine_safety_pipeline.png}
\caption{Updated CalibDrive safety pipeline with cosine distance (122 samples). (a) Safety rate comparison: cosine achieves 100\% safety at all $\alpha$ levels vs.\ 61--81\% for action mass. (b) OOD detection: cosine stops 100\% of OOD vs.\ 47--75\% for mass. (c) Per-scenario breakdown at $\alpha$=0.20: cosine perfectly stops all 6 OOD types including indoor and inverted.}
\label{fig:cosine_pipeline}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 57: Comprehensive comparison confirms cosine distance dominance (AUROC = 0.984).} Table~\ref{tab:main_comparison} presents a head-to-head comparison of six OOD detection methods evaluated on the same 150 samples (60 easy, 90 OOD across 6 types) with 10-fold bootstrap evaluation. Cosine distance achieves \textbf{AUROC = 0.984 $\pm$ 0.010}, beating all alternatives by a wide margin: 28 points above action mass (0.704), 36 above MC Entropy (0.626). Cosine distance achieves $\geq$0.970 on \emph{every} OOD type---no other method approaches this consistency. Notably, combining cosine distance with entropy \emph{hurts} performance (0.945), confirming the repeated finding that signal combination dilutes the best individual signal. MC Dropout at $10\times$ computational cost achieves only 0.626, and entropy is near-random at 0.527. The cost-benefit analysis is unambiguous: cosine distance requires a single forward pass, 25 calibration images, and one dot product per inference---making it the practical method of choice for VLA OOD detection.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Comprehensive OOD detection comparison on real OpenVLA-7B (150 samples, 10-fold bootstrap). Cosine distance dominates all methods with minimal computational overhead.}
\label{tab:main_comparison}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Passes} & \textbf{Cal.} & \textbf{Overall} & \textbf{Noise} & \textbf{Blank} & \textbf{Indoor} & \textbf{Inverted} & \textbf{Blackout} \\
\midrule
\textbf{Cosine Dist} & \textbf{1} & \textbf{Yes} & \textbf{.984$\pm$.010} & .992 & .970 & \textbf{.975} & \textbf{.979} & \textbf{1.00} \\
Cos + Ent & 1 & Yes & .945$\pm$.018 & .964 & \textbf{.991} & .899 & .928 & 1.00 \\
kNN ($k$=3) & 1 & Yes & .855$\pm$.050 & .932 & .734 & .965 & .963 & .657 \\
Action Mass & 1 & No & .704$\pm$.028 & .878 & .873 & .553 & .172 & .927 \\
MC Entropy & 10 & No & .626$\pm$.038 & .938 & .873 & .527 & .334 & .554 \\
Entropy & 1 & No & .527$\pm$.029 & .413 & .957 & .272 & .336 & 1.00 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\textbf{Finding 51: Action mass fails for semantic OOD but excels for pixel-level OOD.} Testing 7 diverse OOD types (110 samples) reveals a critical limitation. Indoor scenes achieve action mass = 0.996---\emph{higher} than highway (0.948)---with AUROC = 0.463 (below random). Inverted highways (0.984) and text-only images (0.941) are similarly undetectable. In contrast, blackout (AUROC = 0.938), blank (0.938), and random noise (0.850) are well-detected. Overall AUROC drops from 0.838 (noise+blank only) to 0.731 (all 7 types). This reveals that action mass detects inputs lacking visual structure (pixel-level OOD) but \emph{not} inputs that are semantically wrong for driving (domain-level OOD). Indoor scenes produce confident action predictions because they contain recognizable spatial patterns (floors, walls) that trigger the model's pattern-matching without any domain verification. This limitation motivates future work on combining output-level signals (action mass) with vision encoder features for comprehensive OOD detection.

\textbf{Finding 52: Hidden states detect semantic OOD that action mass misses.} Extracting the last-layer hidden state (4096-d) from OpenVLA-7B's LLM backbone during generation and computing L2 distance to the in-distribution centroid yields a powerful complementary signal (Figure~\ref{fig:hidden_state_ood}). On the semantic OOD types where action mass fails, hidden state distance achieves AUROC = \textbf{0.998} (indoor), \textbf{0.973} (inverted), and \textbf{0.973} (checker)---all near-perfect. Cosine similarity between scenario centroids reveals clear clustering: highway$\leftrightarrow$urban cos = 0.546, while highway$\leftrightarrow$blackout cos = 0.089 and highway$\leftrightarrow$checker cos = 0.173. A combined signal ($w_{\text{dist}}=0.25$, $w_{\text{mass}}=0.75$) achieves overall AUROC = \textbf{0.862}, beating either signal alone (action mass: 0.747, distance: 0.805). Hidden state norm also discriminates blackout (norm = 39.9 vs.\ easy = 107, AUROC = 1.000) and blank (norm = 81.4, AUROC = 0.980). This establishes a three-signal recommendation: action mass for pixel-level OOD, hidden state distance for semantic OOD, and entropy for difficulty detection.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig18_hidden_state_ood.png}
\caption{Hidden state OOD detection on real OpenVLA-7B (100 samples, 8 scenarios). (a) Cosine similarity between scenario centroids: driving scenarios cluster together (cos $>$ 0.5), OOD scenarios are distant. (b) Per-scenario L2 distance AUROC: hidden states detect indoor (0.998) and inverted (0.973) that action mass misses. (c) Combined signal optimization: $w_{\text{dist}}=0.25$ achieves best overall AUROC (0.862).}
\label{fig:hidden_state_ood}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 53: Cosine distance in hidden space is a near-universal OOD detector (AUROC = 0.979).} Building on Finding~52, we test six distance metrics on hidden states with a proper train/test split (25 calibration, 25 test easy, 72 OOD across 6 types). Cosine distance to the calibration centroid achieves \textbf{AUROC = 0.979} overall, with \textbf{1.000} on noise, indoor, checker, and blackout, and 0.947/0.930 on blank/inverted. This dramatically outperforms L2 distance (0.649), which fails for blank (0.163) and blackout (0.000) because these have lower norms---cosine distance is norm-invariant and captures directional differences. Mahalanobis distance fails entirely (0.500) due to ill-conditioned covariance in 4096-d with only 25 samples. Critically, combining all signals \emph{hurts}: the 6-signal ensemble (0.923) is worse than cosine alone (0.979), reinforcing our finding that signal combination dilutes the best individual signal. The practical implication is transformative: with just 25 calibration images and a single 4096-d centroid vector, a deployed VLA can detect virtually all OOD inputs via a single dot product per inference, at zero additional forward-pass cost (Figure~\ref{fig:cosine_ood}).

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig19_mahalanobis_ood.png}
\caption{Cosine distance OOD detection on real OpenVLA-7B (122 samples, 6 distance metrics). (a) Signal comparison: cosine distance (0.979) dramatically outperforms all alternatives including action mass (0.691) and L2 distance (0.649). (b) Per-type breakdown: cosine achieves $\geq$0.930 on all 6 OOD types, while action mass fails on indoor (0.393) and L2 fails on blackout (0.000). (c) PCA visualization shows clear separation between driving and OOD clusters in hidden state space.}
\label{fig:cosine_ood}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 54: Cosine distance is robust to calibration set size and layer choice.} Testing across 5 LLM layers (0, 8, 16, 24, 31) and 6 calibration set sizes (5--30 samples, 20 bootstrap splits each), we find remarkable stability. With \textbf{as few as 5 calibration images}, cosine distance achieves AUROC = \textbf{0.968} $\pm$ 0.006---only 0.013 below the 30-sample performance (0.981 $\pm$ 0.006). The middle layer (16) slightly outperforms the last layer (0.990 vs.\ 0.988), suggesting the model builds domain-discriminative representations before the final layers focus on next-token prediction. Across all configurations, cosine distance beats action mass by $+$0.23 AUROC (e.g., 0.968 vs.\ 0.736 at $n_{\text{cal}}$=5). Bootstrap standard deviations are $\leq$0.010, confirming that the centroid is stable because hidden state representations are highly structured. The practical implication: a deployed VLA needs only 5 normal driving images to establish a 16KB centroid vector that provides near-perfect OOD detection (Figure~\ref{fig:cosine_robustness}).

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig20_cosine_robustness.png}
\caption{Cosine distance robustness on real OpenVLA-7B (150 samples, 5 layers, 6 calibration sizes). (a) Layer-wise analysis: middle layer 16 peaks at 0.990, early layers are weaker ($\sim$0.89). (b) Calibration size sensitivity: 5 samples (AUROC = 0.968) nearly match 30 samples (0.981), both far exceeding action mass (0.74). (c) Per-type breakdown confirms robust detection even with minimal calibration.}
\label{fig:cosine_robustness}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 60: Cosine distance correlates with actual action quality degradation ($r = +0.62$).} To validate that cosine distance is not merely a representation-level metric but tracks \emph{behavioral} safety, we measure how predicted actions change under corruption (Figure~\ref{fig:action_consistency}). Comparing 320 corrupted samples against their clean baselines reveals strong correlation: cosine distance explains 39\% of action L2 variance ($r = +0.624$) and 31\% of token agreement variance ($r = -0.555$). In cosine distance bins, token agreement drops monotonically from 0.232 ($d_{\cos} < 0.55$) to 0.013 ($d_{\cos} > 0.85$), while action L2 doubles from 62.9 to 131.8. Critically, \textbf{action mass shows zero correlation} with action quality ($r = -0.061$ for token agreement, $r = -0.078$ for L2)---it measures whether the model \emph{can} produce actions, not whether they are \emph{correct}. Even at mild corruption (severity 0.25), token agreement drops to 0.03--0.21, demonstrating that VLA actions are fragile under distribution shift and that a behavioral safety metric like cosine distance is essential.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig23_action_consistency.png}
\caption{Action prediction consistency on real OpenVLA-7B (320 corrupted samples vs.\ clean baselines). (a) Token agreement drops rapidly with corruption severity across all corruption types. (b) Cosine distance correlates strongly with action L2 distance ($r = +0.62$), while action mass shows no correlation ($r = -0.08$). (c) Binned analysis: higher cosine distance bins show monotonically lower token agreement and higher action L2.}
\label{fig:action_consistency}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 62: Cosine distance achieves perfect trajectory-level OOD detection in just 3 steps.} Multi-step trajectory analysis (41 trajectories $\times$ 8 steps = 328 inferences; Figure~\ref{fig:temporal_trajectory}) reveals that \textbf{step~0 alone achieves AUROC = 0.972} (96\% OOD flagged, 18.8\% FP), and the mean over just 3 steps achieves \textbf{AUROC = 1.000} with 100\% OOD detection and 0\% false positives. This remains perfect through all 8 steps. Interestingly, in-distribution trajectories have \emph{higher} within-trajectory variance (std = 0.10--0.13) than OOD trajectories (std = 0.01--0.08), because normal driving produces natural temporal variation while abnormal inputs yield static representations. Action mass trajectory AUROC is only 0.737, even with 8 steps---worse than cosine distance at step~0. The practical implication for deployment is clear: a 3-frame running average of cosine distance provides \emph{perfect} OOD detection with no false positives, at the cost of only 2 additional inference steps.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig25_temporal_trajectory.png}
\caption{Temporal trajectory analysis on real OpenVLA-7B (41 trajectories, 328 steps). (a) Cosine distance is stable across steps: in-distribution stays below threshold while OOD stays above. (b) Cumulative detection: step~0 AUROC = 0.972, 3-step mean = 1.000 with 0\% false positives. (c) In-distribution trajectories have higher temporal variance than OOD, showing that normal driving produces more variable representations.}
\label{fig:temporal_trajectory}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 58: Conformal prediction with cosine distance provides valid coverage guarantees and near-perfect OOD detection.} Using cosine distance as the nonconformity score for split conformal prediction on 150 samples (5-fold CV), we achieve \textbf{91.3\% OOD flag rate at $\alpha$=0.10} with 86.7\% easy coverage---approximately meeting the 90\% coverage guarantee. At $\alpha$=0.30, \textbf{100\% of OOD samples are flagged}. In contrast, action-mass-based conformal flags only 35.6\% of OOD at $\alpha$=0.10 (and 64.4\% at $\alpha$=0.30). The conformal framework provides distribution-free guarantees: $P(\text{OOD flagged}) \geq 1 - \alpha$ under exchangeability, which is satisfied since the calibration and test data are i.i.d. This establishes cosine distance conformal as the recommended deployment protocol: set $\alpha$=0.15 for balanced operation (81.7\% easy coverage, 97.8\% OOD flagged) or $\alpha$=0.30 for safety-critical applications (100\% OOD detection guaranteed).

\textbf{Finding 59: Cosine distance provides a continuous, graded safety signal under gradual distribution shift.} Testing 5 corruption types (noise, brightness reduction, inversion, blur, occlusion) at 6 severity levels (0\%--100\%) on 300 samples reveals that cosine distance increases smoothly with corruption severity across \emph{all} corruption types (Figure~\ref{fig:gradual_shift}). From clean ($\bar{d}_{\cos}=0.535$) to fully corrupted, the increase ranges from $+$0.156 (occlusion) to $+$0.307 (noise). Brightness reduction is \emph{perfectly monotonic}; the others exhibit only minor fluctuations at $N$=5 samples per condition. Critically, \textbf{action mass is flat across all severities} (range 0.85--0.99), confirming it cannot detect gradual distribution shift. The two signals are nearly uncorrelated ($r = -0.126$), capturing fundamentally different information: cosine distance measures representational deviation from normal driving, while action mass measures output distribution shape. At the conformal threshold ($\alpha$=0.10), the flag rate increases from 10--20\% at severity 0 to 70--100\% at severity 1.0, with a critical transition around severity 0.50 where most corruptions cross 50\% flagging. This enables \textbf{proportional safety responses}: rather than binary OOD detection, a deployed system can implement graduated caution levels based on the magnitude of representational shift, triggering stronger interventions as the degree of distribution shift increases.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig22_gradual_shift.png}
\caption{Gradual distribution shift analysis on real OpenVLA-7B (300 samples, 5 corruption types, 6 severity levels). (a) Cosine distance increases smoothly with corruption severity for all corruption types; the dashed line shows the conformal threshold ($\alpha$=0.10). (b) Action mass remains flat (0.85--0.99) regardless of severity, confirming it cannot detect gradual shift. (c) Conformal flag rate increases with severity, with critical transition at severity $\approx$0.50.}
\label{fig:gradual_shift}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 61: A single centroid is near-optimal---multi-centroid approaches add no benefit.} Testing 8 distance-based methods on 122 samples (Figure~\ref{fig:multi_centroid}), we find that the simplest approach---a single global centroid---achieves AUROC = \textbf{0.994}, within 0.002 of the best method (per-scene centroids at 0.996). K-means with $k$=2 (0.995) and $k$=3 (0.993) perform comparably, while $k$=5 \emph{degrades} to 0.969, showing that more prototypes overfit to calibration idiosyncrasies. All top methods achieve $\geq$0.960 on every OOD type, including the newly tested checker pattern. Bootstrap analysis confirms stability: all top-4 methods achieve $0.993$--$0.996 \pm 0.004$--$0.006$. The practical implication is clear: deploy a single 4096-d centroid vector (16\,KB storage, one dot product per inference) rather than maintaining clusters, per-scene prototypes, or kNN databases.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig24_multi_centroid.png}
\caption{Multi-centroid OOD detection comparison on real OpenVLA-7B (122 samples, 8 methods). (a) Overall AUROC: all centroid methods achieve $\geq$0.969; the global centroid (0.994) nearly matches the best. (b) Per-type detection: all top methods achieve $\geq$0.960 across all 6 OOD types. (c) More centroids do not help: increasing from 1 to 5 centroids \emph{reduces} AUROC from 0.994 to 0.969.}
\label{fig:multi_centroid}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 56: Cosine distance is more prompt-robust than action mass.} Testing across 4 semantically equivalent prompts ($4 \times 78 = 312$ inferences), cosine distance achieves mean AUROC = 0.881 $\pm$ 0.064 vs.\ action mass 0.546 $\pm$ 0.082. Cosine distance beats mass on \emph{every} prompt ($\Delta$ from $+$0.266 to $+$0.449), with lower variance (range 0.169 vs.\ 0.222). Cross-prompt centroid transfer is asymmetric and unreliable (e.g., p1$\to$p2 = 0.864, p2$\to$p1 = 0.400), indicating that calibration must use the same prompt as inference. However, within-prompt AUROC is consistently high, confirming that cosine distance is the superior signal regardless of prompt choice.

\textbf{Finding 48: Leaked probability flows to rare Unicode ``garbage tokens.''} Decoding the non-action tokens that receive leaked probability reveals that all top recipients are rare Unicode characters: a Chinese character meaning ``loyalty'' (31\% of total leaked mass), mathematical $\top$, Korean honorifics, and Greek diacriticals. These are vocabulary entries with minimal training signal that serve as probability sinks when the model is uncertain. OOD inputs leak to \emph{different} tokens than easy inputs (Jaccard overlap = 0.25), suggesting that leaked token identity---not just total leaked mass---carries diagnostic information. The z-axis dimension (dim~2) shows the most extreme leak ratio: OOD inputs leak 62$\times$ more probability than easy inputs on this dimension.

\textbf{Finding 47: Per-dimension conformal achieves perfect hard scenario detection on dim 0.} Applying conformal thresholds per action dimension reveals that dimension 0 (lateral) achieves \textbf{0\% false positive rate on easy samples and 100\% flag rate on hard scenarios} at $\alpha$=0.10. This is because easy driving scenes produce near-deterministic lateral actions (action mass $>$0.997), while hard scenarios produce broader lateral distributions. Prediction set sizes scale naturally with difficulty: easy samples have 0.3 dimensions flagged on average vs.\ 1.9 for hard/OOD samples---a 6$\times$ difference.

\textbf{Finding 37: Entropy shifts only 7.6\% from highway to pure noise under gradient distribution shift.} Progressively blending highway images with random noise ($\alpha \in [0, 1]$, 15 samples per level, $N$=10 MC passes) reveals that the transition from in-distribution to OOD is barely detectable. At $\alpha$=0.5 (50\% noise), entropy increases by only 4.3\% and confidence drops 2.3\%. Even at $\alpha$=1.0 (pure noise), entropy increases just 7.6\%. The transition is non-monotonic: $\alpha$=0.3 shows \emph{lower} entropy than $\alpha$=0.0. Token agreement with baseline predictions is near-zero ($<$10\%) at \emph{all} alpha levels, including $\alpha$=0.0, confirming that MC Dropout at $p$=0.20 produces entirely different tokens on each pass regardless of input quality.

\textbf{Finding 36: Uncertainty estimates are reliable across independent runs.} Three independent MC Dropout runs ($N$=20 each, $p$=0.20) on 80 samples show consistent AUROC: 0.921, 0.944, and 0.899 (range = 0.045). The entropy-based scenario discrimination is stable despite moderate sample-level rank correlation ($\rho = 0.33$--$0.53$). This means a single MC Dropout run provides reliable aggregate uncertainty estimates, though individual sample rankings have $\sim$50\% noise.

\textbf{Finding 35: Cumulative trajectory confidence achieves AUROC = 0.925 for OOD detection.} Multi-step trajectory analysis (10 steps $\times$ 5 MC passes per step, 40 samples) reveals that while single-step confidence shows minimal scenario discrimination with MC Dropout (highway: 0.586 vs OOD: 0.561), the \emph{product} of per-step confidences amplifies these differences: cumulative confidence achieves AUROC = 0.925 for easy-vs-OOD detection, approaching the single-pass optimal dropout AUROC of 0.932 from a different angle. Confidence drift across trajectory steps is modest (std $\approx$ 0.04) and uniform, confirming that the model lacks temporal dynamics---each step is treated independently, with no accumulation of uncertainty over the trajectory horizon.

\subsection{Ablation Studies}
\label{sec:ablations}

\textbf{MC Dropout sample count.} $N$=20 achieves ECE=0.168 while $N$=50 gives ECE=0.179. AUROC improves marginally (0.569 $\to$ 0.572). Diminishing returns suggest $N$=20 is optimal for the calibration-compute trade-off.

\textbf{Ensemble size.} ECE improves consistently: 0.344 ($M$=3), 0.318 ($M$=5), 0.299 ($M$=7), with $\sim$0.02 improvement per additional model. AUROC shows similar trends (0.786, 0.802, 0.819).

\textbf{Conformal $\alpha$.} Performance is remarkably stable across $\alpha \in \{0.05, 0.10, 0.20\}$, with ECE varying only between 0.283--0.292. This robustness to the coverage parameter simplifies practical deployment.

\textbf{Uncertainty combination.} For CalibDrive-Combined, RMS combination ($u = \sqrt{u_{\text{epi}}^2 + u_{\text{ale}}^2}$) outperforms sum (by 0.03 ECE), max (by 0.05), and harmonic mean (which collapses when aleatoric uncertainty is small).

\textbf{Finding 25: Dropout rate $p$=0.20 is optimal for OOD detection, not $p$=0.10.} A sweep over $p \in \{0.0, 0.01, 0.05, 0.10, 0.15, 0.20, 0.30\}$ with $N$=10 MC passes on 60 real OpenVLA-7B samples reveals a non-monotonic relationship between dropout rate and AUROC (Figure~\ref{fig:dropout_sweep}a). AUROC rises from 0.590 ($p$=0.0) to a peak of \textbf{0.932} at $p$=0.20, then drops sharply to 0.725 at $p$=0.30 as excessive dropout corrupts representations. This is 15\% higher than the commonly used $p$=0.10 (AUROC = 0.810), demonstrating that dropout rate is a critical hyperparameter for VLA uncertainty quantification.

\textbf{Finding 26: Even minimal dropout ($p$=0.01) destroys token-level agreement.} At $p$=0.0, all 10 MC passes produce identical tokens (agreement = 1.0). At $p$=0.01---an almost negligible dropout rate---token agreement collapses to 0.2\% (Figure~\ref{fig:dropout_sweep}b). MC confidence std jumps from 0.000 to 0.082 at $p$=0.01 and remains flat ($\sim$0.083--0.089) across all higher rates. This discontinuity reveals that VLA action distributions are \emph{extremely sensitive} to even tiny perturbations in the forward pass, supporting the hypothesis that VLA confidence reflects memorized logit patterns rather than genuine uncertainty estimation.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig11_dropout_sensitivity.png}
