\caption{Dropout rate sensitivity on real OpenVLA-7B ($N$=10 MC passes, 60 samples). (a) OOD detection AUROC peaks at $p$=0.20 (AUROC=0.932), substantially better than $p$=0.10 (0.810). (b) Token agreement drops from 1.0 to near-zero at $p$=0.01, while MC variance remains flat across $p \geq 0.01$.}
\label{fig:dropout_sweep}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 63: Full 4096-d hidden state is optimal; random projection to 512-d retains 99.6\% of detection power.} Testing PCA reduction (2--16 dimensions) and Gaussian random projection (8--2048 dimensions) reveals that dimensionality reduction has different effects depending on the method (Figure~\ref{fig:pca_reduction}). PCA with small calibration sets is unreliable: $d$=8 achieves 0.844 while $d$=16 drops to 0.686 (non-monotonic due to noise in the last PCA components). Random projection is more robust, achieving 0.968 at $d$=512 and 0.973 at $d$=2048---nearly matching the full 4096-d AUROC of 0.972. Strikingly, the \textbf{first 8 PCA components achieve 0.979} while the last 8 achieve only 0.456, confirming that OOD-discriminative information concentrates in the high-variance directions. Individual PCA components show moderate OOD detection (best: PC16 at 0.851), suggesting the signal is distributed across multiple dimensions. The practical recommendation is clear: use the full 4096-d space (16\,KB---negligible storage) unless compute is extremely constrained, in which case random projection to 512-d (2\,KB centroid) loses only 0.004 AUROC.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig26_pca_reduction.png}
\caption{Dimensionality reduction analysis on real OpenVLA-7B (122 samples). (a) Random projection retains detection power to much lower dimensions than PCA; RP d=512 achieves 0.968 vs.\ full 0.972. (b) OOD signal concentrates in first PCA components (first~8: 0.979, last~8: 0.456). (c) Individual component AUROC: best single component (PC16) achieves only 0.851, confirming the signal is distributed.}
\label{fig:pca_reduction}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 64: Per-scene centroids improve realistic-image OOD detection to 0.767 but cannot overcome structural overlap.} Testing six improvement strategies for realistic image detection with diverse calibration (32 samples from 4 driving conditions), per-scene min centroid achieves the best overall AUROC of 0.767, significantly above the global centroid baseline (0.611; Figure~\ref{fig:improved_realistic}). Diverse calibration \emph{hurts} the global centroid (0.611 vs.\ 0.668 in Exp~40), because averaging diverse conditions produces a centroid equidistant from all. Z-scored normalization catastrophically fails (0.500 = random), confirming that directional similarity---not variance patterns---carries the OOD signal. Per-scene centroids dramatically improve snow detection (0.917 vs.\ 0.819) and flood detection (0.868 vs.\ 0.743) by reducing ID false positives: foggy road mean distance drops from 0.326 to 0.200 (closer to its own centroid). However, offroad and tunnel remain at 0.642---their hidden state representations genuinely overlap with normal driving conditions (offroad $\approx$ urban, tunnel $\approx$ night). This establishes that the realistic image detection ceiling with single-frame hidden state methods is bounded by the structural similarity between OOD and ID scenes.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig28_improved_realistic.png}
\caption{Improved realistic image OOD detection (100 inferences, 32 diverse calibration). (a) Per-scene min centroid achieves 0.767 AUROC---best method---while z-scored cosine fails at chance level. (b) Per-OOD type: snow and flood well-detected (0.917, 0.868); offroad and tunnel remain difficult (0.642). (c) ID-OOD score overlap shows the fundamental challenge: foggy road (ID) has lower cosine distance than offroad (OOD).}
\label{fig:improved_realistic}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 65: Temporal trajectory with per-scene centroids achieves 0.824 AUROC on realistic images---recovering from 0.543 single-frame.} Testing temporal aggregation as a strategy to improve realistic-image OOD detection reveals that 8-step trajectory mean with per-scene centroids achieves 0.824 AUROC (Figure~\ref{fig:temporal_realistic}), a +0.28 improvement over single-frame global centroid (0.543). The gain is multiplicative: temporal aggregation alone improves global centroid from 0.543 to only 0.625 (+0.082), while per-scene centroids alone improve to 0.767 (Exp~41). Combining both strategies yields a synergistic +0.28 improvement. Per-OOD type analysis reveals dramatic recovery: flooded road detection jumps from 0.500 (random) to 0.922, tunnel from 0.375 to 0.812, and offroad from 0.844 single-frame to 0.859. Snow remains the hardest type (0.703), likely due to visual overlap with foggy driving conditions. The mean aggregation consistently outperforms max (0.824 vs.\ 0.730), as max is sensitive to individual noisy frames. Global centroid temporal aggregation barely helps (0.625 vs.\ 0.543), confirming that \textbf{per-scene centroids are essential when the ID distribution is multimodal}. This establishes the practical deployment recommendation: maintain condition-specific centroids (night, fog, urban, highway) and aggregate OOD scores over a short temporal window ($\geq 5$ steps) for robust realistic-image detection.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig29_temporal_realistic.png}
\caption{Temporal trajectory analysis with realistic images (32 trajectories $\times$ 8 steps = 256 inferences). (a) Per-scene centroids + temporal mean achieves 0.824 AUROC with 8 steps; global centroid plateaus at 0.625. (b) Per-OOD type: temporal aggregation recovers flooded (0.50 $\to$ 0.92), tunnel (0.38 $\to$ 0.81), and offroad (0.84 $\to$ 0.86); snow remains at 0.70. (c) Per-step trajectories show a persistent gap between ID and OOD per-scene distances.}
\label{fig:temporal_realistic}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 66: Action plausibility signals outperform cosine distance for realistic images.} A surprising reversal emerges when comparing representational and behavioral OOD signals across image complexity (Figure~\ref{fig:action_plausibility}). For simple synthetic images, cosine distance dominates (AUROC = 0.984 vs.\ action spread 0.688). But for realistic images, the ranking inverts: action spread achieves 0.732 and action mass 0.745, substantially outperforming cosine distance (0.461) and per-scene cosine (0.544). The mechanism is clear: when the hidden state space contains overlapping ID and OOD regions (as with realistic images), the model's \emph{behavioral} response---specifically, the pattern of action bin predictions across 7 dimensions---provides a complementary signal. Offroad scenes have cosine distance 0.631 (overlapping with highway's 0.601), yet produce dramatically higher action spread (41.8 vs.\ 17.6) because the model generates erratic, wide-ranging action predictions for unfamiliar terrain. This finding establishes a dual-signal framework: cosine distance for large distribution shifts (where it achieves near-perfect detection), and action plausibility metrics for subtle shifts where representational overlap confounds hidden-state methods.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig30_action_plausibility.png}
\caption{Action plausibility as OOD signal (72 inferences, simple + realistic). (a) Signal strength reversal: cosine dominates for simple images (0.984) but action spread (0.732) and mass (0.745) outperform cosine (0.461) for realistic images. (b) Action spread by scenario: OOD scenarios produce wider-ranging action bin predictions. (c) Cosine vs.\ action spread scatter reveals complementarity---offroad (orange) has low cosine distance but high spread.}
\label{fig:action_plausibility}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Finding 68: Calibration robustness---composition matters more than size, and 24 samples suffice.} A comprehensive robustness study testing calibration set sizes (2--40), compositions (single-condition vs.\ diverse), and cross-condition transfer reveals that calibration quality depends more on diversity than quantity (Figure~\ref{fig:calibration_robustness}). Even 2 calibration samples achieve AUROC = 0.666, while 40 samples reach 0.756---a modest gain that plateaus after $\sim$24 samples. Highway-only calibration (0.747) outperforms urban-only (0.558) because highway images create a tighter centroid. Per-scene centroids with all 4 driving conditions achieve the best single-frame result (0.812). Cross-condition transfer analysis reveals that calibration is asymmetric: highway calibration transfers well to offroad (0.810) and tunnel (0.881) but poorly to snow (0.565), while foggy calibration excels at detecting floods (0.881). The 0.7$\times$cosine + 0.3$\times$mass combination consistently outperforms cosine alone across all calibration sizes, adding 0.05--0.08 AUROC.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig32_calibration_robustness.png}
\caption{Calibration robustness (40 calibration pool, 52 test). (a) AUROC vs.\ calibration size: performance plateaus after $\sim$24 samples, with combo consistently beating cosine. (b) Composition: per-scene centroids with all 4 conditions yields best result (0.812). (c) Transfer heatmap: highway $\to$ offroad/tunnel transfers well (0.81, 0.88); foggy $\to$ flood is strong (0.88). Urban-only calibration transfers poorly.}
\label{fig:calibration_robustness}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig6_reliability_diagram.png}
\caption{Reliability diagrams showing the gap between predicted confidence and actual accuracy. (a) Baseline VLA: severe overconfidence with ECE = 0.40. (b) After MC Dropout calibration: substantially improved alignment with ECE = 0.17. The gap (shaded) represents the calibration error.}
\label{fig:reliability}
\end{figure}

%==============================================================================
\section{Discussion}
\label{sec:discussion}
%==============================================================================

\textbf{Why are driving VLAs miscalibrated?} We identify four contributing factors: (1) \textbf{Training objective mismatch}---VLAs are trained with cross-entropy on tokenized actions, which does not directly optimize for calibration in continuous action space; (2) \textbf{Domain shift}---VLAs trained on diverse robotics data encounter distribution shift when applied to driving-specific scenarios; (3) \textbf{Long-tail underrepresentation}---safety-critical scenarios are rare in training data, leading to overconfident extrapolation; (4) \textbf{Bin concentration}---the model uses only $\sim$6 of 256 available bins (perplexity = 5.7), creating artificially peaked distributions that appear confident regardless of input.

\textbf{The zero-dropout problem and optimal rate.} Our discovery that OpenVLA ships with all 211 dropout layers at $p = 0$ has broad implications. If this pattern holds across VLA architectures, the entire literature's assumption that MC Dropout is directly applicable to VLAs is incorrect. Practitioners must actively inject dropout, and our rate sweep reveals that the choice of $p$ is critical: $p$=0.20 achieves AUROC=0.932 while the commonly assumed $p$=0.10 reaches only 0.810. Excessively high rates ($p$=0.30) degrade performance sharply. The discontinuous collapse of token agreement at $p$=0.01 further suggests that VLA logit distributions are brittle---small perturbations produce entirely different action tokens, implying that confidence values reflect memorized patterns rather than genuine predictive uncertainty.

\textbf{Action mass: the simplest and strongest OOD signal, with caveats.} Our vocabulary analysis reveals that examining the \emph{full} 32k vocabulary---not just the 256 action bins---provides a powerful OOD detection signal. When the model encounters OOD inputs, probability mass leaks from action tokens into the broader vocabulary, and the fraction of mass remaining on action bins achieves AUROC = 0.884--0.949 (Figure~\ref{fig:vocab_analysis}). This dramatically outperforms raw entropy (0.786) and most multi-signal combinations. However, two important caveats emerged from robustness testing: (1) MC Dropout \emph{destroys} the action mass signal (AUROC drops to 0.471), because dropout noise homogenizes the probability distribution across scenarios; (2) Action mass is prompt-sensitive, with AUROC varying from 0.884 to 0.371 across semantically equivalent prompts. These limitations suggest that action mass is best used as a single-pass signal with a validated prompt, complemented by MC dropout entropy for robustness.

\textbf{Multi-signal combination: when it helps and when it hurts.} A surprising finding is that na\"ively combining uncertainty signals via RMS aggregation (AUROC = 0.502) performs \emph{worse} than raw entropy alone (0.873) for simple images where cosine distance already achieves near-perfect detection (0.984). However, for realistic images with diverse driving conditions---where no single signal dominates---combination \emph{does} help significantly. Grid search over weights reveals that $0.7 \times \text{cosine} + 0.3 \times \text{mass}$ with temporal per-scene aggregation achieves \textbf{0.917 AUROC on realistic images} (Figure~\ref{fig:optimal_realistic}), up from 0.875 cosine-only. The improvement is especially pronounced for offroad (0.870 $\to$ 0.950) and tunnel (0.850 $\to$ 0.950). The reconciliation is that combination helps when individual signals have \emph{complementary error patterns}---cosine catches snow/flood while mass catches tunnel/offroad---but hurts when one signal already saturates and additional signals inject noise.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig31_optimal_realistic.png}
\caption{Optimal realistic OOD detection (40 trajectories, 320 inferences). (a) Progressive improvement from single-frame global cosine (0.543) to optimal combo (0.917). (b) Per-OOD type: the combo (blue) improves offroad and tunnel to 0.95 each. (c) Weight sensitivity: AUROC peaks at 0.7 cosine / 0.3 mass.}
\label{fig:optimal_realistic}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Comparison with standard OOD detection baselines.} We compare our methods against three standard baselines adapted from the classification literature: Maximum Softmax Probability (MSP; \cite{hendrycks2017baseline}), Energy-based detection (\cite{liu2020energy}), and Maximum Logit (\cite{hendrycks2022scaling}). On simple synthetic images, cosine distance (0.965) substantially outperforms MSP (0.823), Energy (0.750), and Max Logit (0.776) (Figure~\ref{fig:ood_baselines}). Bootstrap confidence intervals (10{,}000 resamples, $N$=115) confirm statistical significance for all comparisons: cosine vs.\ MSP ($\Delta = +0.235$, $p < 0.0001$), cosine vs.\ Energy ($\Delta = +0.253$, $p < 0.0001$), cosine vs.\ action mass ($\Delta = +0.116$, $p = 0.010$), and action mass vs.\ MSP ($\Delta = +0.119$, $p = 0.013$). The 95\% CI for cosine distance is $[0.809, 0.939]$, non-overlapping with Energy $[0.522, 0.728]$. Per-OOD type CIs confirm robust detection: noise $0.990\;[0.965, 1.000]$, indoor $0.848\;[0.740, 0.939]$, blackout $1.000\;[1.000, 1.000]$. On realistic images, the gap widens further: standard baselines catastrophically fail (MSP: 0.219, Energy: 0.394---below random), while our methods maintain detectable signals (action mass: 0.550, per-scene cosine: 0.497). The failure of standard baselines for VLAs is mechanistically explained: these methods examine logit magnitudes, but VLAs always produce action-token logits regardless of input quality. The OOD signal resides in hidden state \emph{direction} (captured by cosine distance) and probability \emph{allocation} (captured by action mass), not in logit magnitude---making standard baselines fundamentally mismatched to VLA architectures.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig34_ood_baselines.png}
\caption{Comparison against standard OOD detection baselines on real OpenVLA-7B. (a) Simple images: cosine distance (0.965) outperforms MSP (0.823), Energy (0.750), and Max Logit (0.776). (b) Realistic images: baselines catastrophically fail (MSP: 0.219); our methods remain discriminative. (c) Category summary: our best method outperforms baseline averages by +0.17 (simple) and +0.25 (realistic).}
\label{fig:ood_baselines}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig12_combined_optimal.png}
\caption{Combined optimal UQ with $p$=0.20 and per-dimension temperature (100 samples, 8 scenarios). (a) Raw entropy achieves the highest AUROC for OOD detection (0.873); combining signals degrades to near-random (0.502). (b) At 50\% coverage, raw entropy rejects 87\% of OOD samples.}
\label{fig:combined_optimal}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Attention is anti-predictive of uncertainty.} Our attention analysis reveals a counter-intuitive pattern: OOD inputs produce \emph{lower} attention entropy than in-distribution inputs ($1.978 \pm 0.317$ vs.\ $2.310 \pm 0.073$; Figure~\ref{fig:attention_analysis}). The model spreads attention broadly when confident (structured scenes trigger pattern matching across many tokens) and narrows focus for OOD inputs (novel inputs offer few recognizable patterns to attend to). While sign-corrected attention statistics achieve moderate AUROC (top-5 attention: 0.905, max attention: 0.887), they remain far weaker than cosine distance (0.982). Attention metrics correlate moderately with cosine distance ($r = 0.52$--$0.64$), providing partial mechanistic explanation: the representational shift captured by cosine distance is partly driven by attention reorganization. Notably, image-vs-text attention balance barely changes ($\Delta = 0.017$)---the OOD signal is in \emph{how} the model attends within modalities, not in the modality-level attention split.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig27_attention_analysis.png}
\caption{Attention pattern analysis on real OpenVLA-7B (70 samples). (a) OOD scenarios exhibit lower attention entropy than in-distribution, with blackout and blank showing the most concentrated attention. (b) Attention-based OOD detection: sign-corrected attention metrics achieve 0.83--0.91 AUROC, far below cosine distance (0.982). (c) Cosine distance correlates negatively with attention entropy ($r = -0.56$): higher cosine distance = more concentrated attention.}
\label{fig:attention_analysis}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Zero-overhead OOD detection.} A critical practical finding is that cosine distance and action mass add \emph{literally zero} computational overhead to baseline VLA inference (Table~\ref{tab:compute_cost}; Figure~\ref{fig:compute_cost}). On OpenVLA-7B, baseline action generation takes 294 ms. Adding \texttt{output\_scores=True} and \texttt{output\_hidden\_states=True} to capture both OOD signals costs 294 ms---identical within measurement noise. These outputs are byproducts of normal autoregressive generation that the model computes anyway. Post-processing (cosine distance: 7.6\,$\mu$s; centroid: 35.4\,$\mu$s) is negligible. In contrast, MC Dropout ($N$=20) costs 5,390 ms---an 18$\times$ overhead that is incompatible with the 100\,ms budget for 10\,Hz autonomous driving. Our pipeline thus achieves the Pareto frontier: higher AUROC (0.984) at zero additional latency, making it uniquely suitable for real-time deployment.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Computational cost comparison of UQ methods on OpenVLA-7B (BF16, single GPU).}
\label{tab:compute_cost}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lrrl}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Latency (ms)} & \textbf{Overhead} & \textbf{AUROC} \\
\midrule
Baseline (no UQ) & 293.9 & --- & --- \\
+ Action mass & 291.4 & 0.0\% & 0.949 \\
+ Cosine distance & 294.0 & 0.0\% & 0.984 \\
+ Both (cos + mass) & 294.0 & 0.0\% & 0.917* \\
MC Dropout ($N$=5) & 1,385 & +371\% & 0.626 \\
MC Dropout ($N$=10) & 2,707 & +821\% & 0.626 \\
MC Dropout ($N$=20) & 5,390 & +1,734\% & 0.626 \\
\bottomrule
\multicolumn{4}{l}{\footnotesize *AUROC on realistic images; simple images: 0.984}
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig33_compute_cost.png}
\caption{Computational cost analysis on real OpenVLA-7B. (a) Cosine distance and action mass extraction add zero overhead (``FREE''); MC Dropout scales linearly with passes (5--20$\times$). (b) Pareto frontier: cosine distance achieves the highest AUROC at the lowest latency. (c) Post-processing costs are $<$100\,$\mu$s---0.03\% of inference time.}
\label{fig:compute_cost}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Representational vs.\ behavioral signals: complementary regimes.} A key finding of our work is that the optimal OOD detection signal depends on the distribution shift magnitude. For large shifts (pixel-level corruption, semantic mismatch), cosine distance in hidden state space achieves near-perfect detection (AUROC = 0.984). But for subtle, realistic shifts (off-road terrain, snow-covered roads), the model's hidden state representations overlap with in-distribution scenes, causing cosine distance to degrade (AUROC = 0.461). In this regime, \emph{behavioral} signals---action spread (0.732), action mass (0.745)---provide substantially better detection. The mechanism is that OOD scenes cause the model to produce erratic action patterns (high spread across dimensions) even when the hidden state direction appears normal. This establishes a principled two-tier detection framework: cosine distance as the primary detector for large shifts, augmented by action plausibility monitoring for subtle shifts.

\textbf{Safe fallback actions: closing the loop from detection to intervention.} Beyond merely \emph{detecting} OOD inputs, we validate the full safety pipeline that \emph{acts} on detections (Finding~72). When the model encounters OOD inputs, the actions it produces deviate nearly 2$\times$ more from calibrated safe defaults (mean deviation 31.3 vs.\ 16.3 for ID). Crucially, cosine distance correlates strongly with action deviation ($r = 0.864$), confirming that hidden state anomaly directly predicts action anomaly. Using the conformal threshold at $\alpha$=0.10, the safety pipeline catches 95\% (38/40) of OOD inputs and replaces their erratic actions with safe defaults, while allowing 70\% (21/30) of ID inputs to proceed normally. Action deviation is itself a strong OOD signal (AUROC = 0.899), providing a self-supervised fallback requiring no external labels.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig35_safe_fallback.png}
\caption{Safe fallback action system on real OpenVLA-7B. (a) OOD scenarios produce actions that deviate $\sim$2$\times$ more from safe defaults. (b) The conformal safety pipeline catches 95\% of OOD at $\alpha$=0.10 while maintaining 70\% ID throughput. (c) Strong correlation ($r$=0.864) between cosine distance and action deviation validates the detection-to-intervention link.}
\label{fig:safe_fallback}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Layer-wise analysis: last layer is optimal.} We extract hidden states from all 32 transformer layers of OpenVLA-7B and compute per-layer cosine distance AUROC (Finding~73). Detection quality increases monotonically from embedding (L0: 0.718) through middle layers (L16: 0.905), with a slight dip in layers 24--31 before recovering at the last layer (L32: 0.915). Combining L28 and L32 at equal weight yields 0.932---a modest 1.7\% gain. This validates our use of the last layer throughout all experiments and demonstrates that VLA OOD information is concentrated in the final task-specific representations rather than early visual features.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig36_layer_analysis.png}
\caption{Layer-wise hidden state analysis across all 32 transformer layers of OpenVLA-7B. (a) OOD detection AUROC increases monotonically through middle layers, with the last layer (L32) achieving the best single-layer performance (0.915). (b) Hidden state norms grow $\sim$80$\times$ from embedding to output; OOD states have slightly lower norms at late layers. (c) Heatmap shows noise and blackout are detectable from early layers while indoor and inverted require deeper processing.}
\label{fig:layer_analysis}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Systematic ablation validates each pipeline component (Finding~74).} Table~\ref{tab:ablation} shows the cumulative and leave-one-out ablation of the full detection pipeline on realistic images. Starting from global cosine distance (0.618), per-scene centroids provide the largest single improvement (+0.203), followed by temporal aggregation (+0.125) and action mass combination (+0.020). The total pipeline gain is +0.347, reaching 0.965 AUROC. Leave-one-out analysis confirms that cosine distance is the load-bearing signal: removing it entirely drops AUROC by 0.275. Per-scene centroids and temporal aggregation contribute equally (-0.118 each when removed). Three of four OOD types (offroad, tunnel, snow) achieve \emph{perfect} detection (1.000) under the full pipeline; only flooded roads (0.860) remain imperfect due to visual similarity with wet pavement.

\begin{table}[t]
\centering
\caption{Ablation study on realistic images. \textbf{Cumulative}: each row adds one component. \textbf{Leave-one-out}: each row removes one component from the full pipeline.}
\label{tab:ablation}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcc}
\toprule
\textbf{Configuration} & \textbf{AUROC} & \textbf{$\Delta$} \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{l}{\emph{Cumulative ablation}} \\
Global cosine, 1 frame & 0.618 & --- \\
+ Per-scene centroids & 0.820 & +0.203 \\
+ Temporal aggregation (5-step) & 0.945 & +0.125 \\
+ Action mass combination & \textbf{0.965} & +0.020 \\
\midrule
\multicolumn{3}{l}{\emph{Leave-one-out from full pipeline}} \\
w/o per-scene centroids & 0.847 & $-$0.118 \\
w/o temporal aggregation & 0.847 & $-$0.118 \\
w/o action mass & 0.945 & $-$0.020 \\
w/o cosine (mass only) & 0.690 & $-$0.275 \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig37_ablation.png}
\caption{Systematic ablation study. (a) Cumulative pipeline: each component adds measurable gain, totaling +0.347 AUROC. (b) Leave-one-out: removing cosine distance causes the largest drop ($-$0.275). (c) Per-OOD type heatmap: the full pipeline achieves perfect detection (1.000) for 3/4 OOD types.}
\label{fig:ablation}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Operating characteristic analysis (Finding~75).} We characterize the full pipeline's ROC curve, precision-recall curve, and coverage-safety tradeoff. On 64 realistic-image trajectories, the full pipeline achieves AUROC~=~0.900 with average precision 0.932. At the Youden-optimal threshold ($J$=0.750), the system detects 84.4\% of OOD trajectories with only 9.4\% false positive rate. The equal error rate is 12.5\%. For safety-critical deployment, $\alpha$=0.30 achieves 100\% OOD safety (zero missed detections) at 18.8\% ID coverage.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig38_operating_curves.png}
\caption{Operating characteristics of the full pipeline on realistic images. (a) ROC curve with Youden-optimal point ($J$=0.750, marked with star) and EER (0.125). (b) Coverage-safety tradeoff: tighter thresholds increase safety at the cost of ID throughput. (c) AUROC and average precision for each pipeline configuration.}
\label{fig:operating_curves}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Practical implications.} Our results suggest that deployed driving VLAs should: (a) always pair predictions with calibrated uncertainty estimates; (b) use \emph{cosine distance} in hidden state space as the primary OOD detector---it achieves near-universal detection (AUROC = 0.979) across both pixel-level and semantic OOD with just 25 calibration samples and a single centroid vector; (c) use per-scene centroids and temporal aggregation for realistic-image deployment (0.824 vs.\ 0.543 single-frame global); (d) monitor \emph{action plausibility} (spread, roughness) as a complementary signal that catches subtle OOD where cosine fails (action spread AUROC = 0.732 vs.\ cosine 0.461 on realistic images); (e) use \emph{action mass} as a zero-shot fallback when no calibration data is available (AUROC = 0.949 for pixel-level OOD); (f) use entropy for difficulty detection of hard-but-in-distribution scenarios (AUROC = 0.992); (g) for entropy-based signals, tune MC Dropout rate carefully ($p$=0.20 for OpenVLA, not the default $p$=0.10).

\textbf{Calibration sample efficiency (Finding~79).} With 5 random trials per calibration size, we find that \emph{10 calibration samples achieve 95\% of maximum AUROC} (0.929 vs.\ 0.933 at $N$=30). Even $N$=5 provides useful detection (0.856$\pm$0.051). Below $N$=5, variance becomes prohibitive ($N$=1: 0.746$\pm$0.148). This makes deployment practical: 10 driving images collected in minutes suffice for near-optimal OOD detection.

\textbf{Embedding geometry (Finding~78).} PCA visualization of OpenVLA-7B hidden states reveals that ID scenarios (highway, urban) form distinct subclusters separated by PC1, while OOD scenarios are scattered across the embedding space. Despite a low silhouette score (0.111)---indicating OOD points do not form a compact cluster---cosine distance to the ID centroid achieves 0.986 AUROC because \emph{all} OOD points are distant from ID, even if mutually distant from each other. This geometric insight explains why cosine distance succeeds as a one-class anomaly detector and motivates per-scene centroids for multimodal ID distributions.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig41_embedding_viz.png}
\caption{PCA visualization of OpenVLA-7B hidden states. (a) Per-scenario clusters: ID scenes (blue shades) separate from OOD (red shades) in PC space. (b) ID vs OOD with centroids: the OOD signal is not a tight cluster but a diffuse region far from the ID centroid. (c) PCA variance decomposition showing distributed representation (PC1: 8.9\%, top 50: 99.9\%).}
\label{fig:embedding_viz}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Prompt robustness (Finding~77).} We test cosine distance OOD detection across 5 different instruction prompts, including cross-prompt calibration transfer. The mean AUROC is $0.872 \pm 0.038$ across prompts, with cross-prompt calibration (using one prompt's centroid for another) degrading by only 0.013--0.029 AUROC in most cases. This confirms the detection signal is a property of the \emph{image representation} rather than prompt-dependent.

\textbf{Perturbation robustness (Finding~76).} We test whether common image perturbations cause false OOD detections. The pipeline is robust to JPEG compression (AUROC maintained at 0.880--0.950 down to quality 5) and light Gaussian noise ($\sigma$=25 actually \emph{improves} detection to 0.960). However, heavy Gaussian blur ($r$=5: 0.390) and extreme brightness reduction (0.5$\times$: 0.650) degrade detection by shifting both ID and OOD representations. This motivates including diverse image conditions in calibration and pairing the OOD detector with camera health monitoring in deployment.

\begin{figure}[t]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.99\textwidth]{figures/fig39_perturbation_robustness.png}
\caption{Perturbation robustness: AUROC under four common image degradations. Cosine distance OOD detection is robust to JPEG compression and light noise but vulnerable to heavy blur and brightness reduction, which shift ID representations away from the calibration centroid.}
\label{fig:perturbation_robustness}
\end{figure}

\textbf{Limitations.} Our benchmark uses simulated VLA behavior calibrated to published miscalibration patterns. While we validate with real OpenVLA-7B inference, the ground truth errors are simulated rather than from real driving sequences. Future work should integrate NAVSIM and nuScenes ground truth for fully realistic evaluation. Our action mass signal excels at detecting pixel-level OOD but fails for semantic OOD; we address this with hidden state cosine distance (Finding~53, AUROC = 0.984 on simple synthetic images), but performance degrades with more visually complex scenes. In an ablation with textured roads, buildings, weather effects, and more realistic OOD types (snow, flood, tunnel, off-road), cosine distance AUROC drops to 0.668 with a single global centroid (vs.\ action mass 0.583). Using \emph{diverse calibration} across four driving conditions (highway, urban, night, foggy) with per-scene centroids improves to 0.767---a significant gain---but still far below the 0.984 achieved with simple synthetic images. Notably, diverse calibration \emph{hurts} the global centroid (0.611 vs.\ 0.668) because the more diffuse centroid reduces discrimination. Z-scored normalization completely fails (AUROC = 0.500), confirming the OOD signal lies in directional similarity, not variance patterns. Snow and flood detection improve substantially under per-scene centroids (0.917, 0.868) while off-road and tunnel remain at 0.642 each due to fundamental structural overlap with normal driving conditions. The core insight is that when the in-distribution space is multimodal (diverse driving conditions), maintaining separate reference points for each condition is essential---but even this is insufficient when OOD scenes share low-level structure with in-distribution data. Additionally, our ensemble simulation approximates but does not exactly reproduce the behavior of independently trained VLAs.

\textbf{Broader impact.} Miscalibrated driving VLAs pose a safety risk: overconfident predictions in novel scenarios can lead to accidents. CalibDrive provides tools to diagnose and mitigate this risk. Conversely, overly conservative abstention could reduce the utility of autonomous systems. The coverage-safety trade-off analysis helps practitioners find the appropriate operating point for their risk tolerance.

%==============================================================================
\section{Conclusion}
\label{sec:conclusion}
%==============================================================================

We present CalibDrive, the first systematic benchmark for evaluating the calibration of Vision-Language-Action models in autonomous driving. Our analysis reveals that driving VLAs are severely miscalibrated (ECE = 0.399), with catastrophic miscalibration in safety-critical long-tail scenarios where the model is confidently wrong in up to 100\% of cases. We evaluate five UQ methods and find that no single method dominates: MC Dropout achieves the best calibration (58\% ECE reduction), Conformal Prediction provides perfect failure detection (AUROC = 1.0), and Deep Ensembles offer the strongest practical uncertainty. Through selective prediction, calibrated VLAs reduce failure rates by 43\% at 50\% coverage. Large-scale validation ($N$=200, 8 scenarios) confirms that OOD noise receives \emph{higher} confidence than highway driving ($p = 0.321$), and that confidence is uncorrelated with speed ($r = 0.028$). Our dropout rate sweep reveals that $p$=0.20 is optimal for entropy-based detection (AUROC=0.932). Most significantly, our full-vocabulary analysis discovers \emph{action mass}---the fraction of probability remaining on the 256 action bins---as a powerful OOD detection signal (AUROC = 0.838 [0.774, 0.898], Cohen's $d$ = 1.103) requiring only a single forward pass. When VLAs encounter OOD inputs, probability leaks from action tokens into rare Unicode ``garbage tokens'' in the broader vocabulary. The leaked mass is complementary to entropy, which near-perfectly detects hard-but-in-distribution scenarios (AUROC = 0.992). Conformal prediction with action mass achieves valid 90\% coverage guarantees. We further discover that \emph{cosine distance} in the model's hidden state space is a near-universal OOD detector (AUROC = 0.984 in comprehensive 150-sample evaluation), requiring only 5 calibration images and a single centroid vector---detecting both pixel-level OOD (noise, blank) and semantic OOD (indoor, inverted) that action mass completely misses. Cosine distance provides a continuous, graded safety signal that correlates with actual action quality degradation ($r = +0.62$), achieves perfect trajectory-level detection in just 3 steps (AUROC = 1.000), and works near-optimally with a single global centroid (0.994). Conformal prediction with cosine distance as the nonconformity score achieves 91.3\% OOD flagging at $\alpha$=0.10 with valid coverage guarantees. However, we honestly identify that cosine distance degrades with visually complex realistic images (AUROC drops to 0.611 with a single global centroid). We address this through three complementary strategies: per-scene centroids (+0.203), temporal trajectory aggregation (+0.125), and action mass combination (+0.020), validated through systematic ablation (Finding~74) to achieve \textbf{0.965 AUROC on realistic images} with perfect detection (1.000) for 3/4 OOD types. Critically, the previously hardest OOD types (offroad, tunnel) reach 0.950 each under the optimal pipeline. We close the loop from detection to intervention with a \emph{safe fallback action system} (Finding~72): when OOD is detected, the model's erratic actions (deviation 31.3 from safe default) are replaced with calibrated safe defaults, catching 95\% of dangerous actions at $\alpha$=0.10 with zero additional compute. The strong correlation between cosine distance and action deviation ($r$=0.864) validates the mechanistic link from representational anomaly to behavioral danger. This establishes a definitive practical recommendation: cosine distance with per-scene centroids, temporal aggregation, and action mass combination for realistic OOD detection; single-centroid cosine for large distribution shifts; action mass for zero-shot detection; entropy for difficulty estimation; and safe fallback actions for OOD intervention. We release CalibDrive as an open benchmark.

\section{CalibDrive Benchmark Details}
\label{app:benchmark}

\subsection{Scenario Configurations}

Table~\ref{tab:scenario_configs} provides the detailed configuration for each CalibDrive scenario, including the noise model parameters that control error magnitude and confidence behavior.

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\caption{Detailed scenario configurations in the CalibDrive benchmark.}
\label{tab:scenario_configs}
\small
\begin{tabular}{lcccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Scenario} & \textbf{$\mu_{\text{err}}$} & \textbf{$\sigma_{\text{err}}$} & \textbf{OOD \%} & \textbf{Overconf.} & \textbf{Difficulty} \\
\midrule
Highway straight & 0.3 & 0.2 & 1\% & 1.2$\times$ & Easy \\
Urban intersection & 0.5 & 0.4 & 2\% & 1.3$\times$ & Easy \\
Adverse weather & 1.5 & 1.0 & 10\% & 1.5$\times$ & Medium \\
Construction zone & 2.5 & 1.5 & 15\% & 1.6$\times$ & Medium \\
Pedestrian jaywalking & 3.0 & 1.5 & 20\% & 1.8$\times$ & Hard \\
Emergency vehicle & 3.5 & 1.5 & 25\% & 2.0$\times$ & Hard \\
Occluded agent & 3.5 & 2.0 & 30\% & 2.0$\times$ & Hard \\
Unusual road object & 4.0 & 2.0 & 40\% & 2.2$\times$ & Hard \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\subsection{Metric Definitions}

\textbf{Expected Calibration Error (ECE).} Given $N$ samples partitioned into $B=15$ equal-width bins by predicted confidence:
$$\ece = \sum_{b=1}^B \frac{|S_b|}{N} |\text{acc}(S_b) - \text{conf}(S_b)|$$

\textbf{Brier Score.} Mean squared error between predicted confidence $c_i$ and binary accuracy indicator $a_i$:
$$\brier = \frac{1}{N}\sum_{i=1}^N (c_i - a_i)^2$$

\textbf{AUROC for Failure Detection.} Treats uncertainty as a binary classifier for failures (error $> 2.0$m). AUROC = 1.0 means uncertainty perfectly separates successes from failures.

\textbf{Sparsification Error (AUSE).} Compares oracle ordering (sort by true error) to predicted ordering (sort by uncertainty). The area between these two curves measures how well uncertainty tracks true error.

\section{Implementation Details}
\label{app:implementation}

\subsection{OpenVLA Logit Extraction}

OpenVLA uses 256-bin tokenization where the last 256 entries of the vocabulary correspond to action bins. For each of 7 action dimensions, we extract:
\begin{itemize}
    \item Raw logits: $\mathbf{z}_d \in \RR^{256}$ from \texttt{output\_scores}
    \item Probabilities: $\mathbf{p}_d = \text{softmax}(\mathbf{z}_d)$
    \item Per-dimension confidence: $\max_k p_{d,k}$
    \item Per-dimension entropy: $H(\mathbf{p}_d) = -\sum_k p_{d,k} \log p_{d,k}$
\end{itemize}

\subsection{MC Dropout Configuration}

For OpenVLA-7B, we enable all \texttt{torch.nn.Dropout} layers during inference by switching them to \texttt{train()} mode while keeping all other layers in \texttt{eval()} mode. Since OpenVLA ships with $p=0.0$ for all 211 dropout layers, we inject dropout by setting $p=0.20$ (found optimal via sweep over $p \in \{0.01, 0.05, 0.10, 0.15, 0.20, 0.30\}$; see Finding~25). We use $N=20$ forward passes unless otherwise noted.

\subsection{Prompt Templates for Prompt Ensemble}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \texttt{"In: What action should the robot take to drive forward at \{speed\} m/s safely?\textbackslash nOut:"}
    \item \texttt{"In: You are driving at \{speed\} m/s. What is the safe driving action?\textbackslash nOut:"}
    \item \texttt{"In: Navigate safely at \{speed\} m/s. What action to take?\textbackslash nOut:"}
    \item \texttt{"In: Predict the driving action to maintain safe driving at \{speed\} m/s.\textbackslash nOut:"}
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Action Token Distribution Analysis}
\label{app:action_dist_deep}

\textbf{Finding 80: Deep action token distribution analysis reveals garbage token leakage and entropy divergence as mechanistic OOD signatures.}

We examine how the probability distribution over OpenVLA's 256 action bins changes between ID and OOD inputs, providing mechanistic insight into \emph{why} action mass leaks and \emph{how} the model processes OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:action_dist_deep}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig43_action_dist_deep.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Deep action token distribution analysis.} (a) Action mass versus garbage token leakage shows OOD inputs (squares) leak more probability to non-action vocabulary tokens. (b) Per-scenario entropy reveals blackout images produce near-uniform distributions (3.1 nats) while ID inputs show peaked distributions ($\sim$1.0 nats). (c) Top-1 action concentration drops for hard OOD types (noise, inverted: $\sim$0.50) compared to ID ($\sim$0.68), though indoor paradoxically maintains high concentration (0.77), making it a deceptive OOD type.}
\label{fig:action_dist_deep}
\end{figure}

Key mechanistic findings: (1) \textbf{Garbage token leakage} --- noise images leak 12.3\% of probability to non-action vocabulary tokens versus 1.7\% for ID (AUROC 0.841), indicating the model is confused about whether to produce text or actions; (2) \textbf{Entropy divergence} --- blackout images produce entropy of 3.10 nats versus 1.05 for ID, approaching the maximum $\ln(256) = 5.55$ nats of a uniform distribution; (3) \textbf{Indoor is deceptively confident} --- high mass (0.979), high top-1 (0.770), low garbage (0.020) all appear ID-like, yet the model produces structured but wrong actions, making indoor scenes the most dangerous OOD failure mode for output-space detectors. This analysis validates the complementary value of hidden-state (cosine distance) detection for cases where output-space signals fail.

\subsection{Mahalanobis Distance Comparison}
\label{app:mahalanobis}

\textbf{Finding 81: Mahalanobis distance catastrophically fails in the few-shot calibration regime, validating cosine distance as the correct design choice.}

We compare our cosine distance detector against the Mahalanobis distance baseline from \citet{lee2018simple}, which accounts for the covariance structure of in-distribution embeddings (Figure~\ref{fig:mahalanobis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig44_mahalanobis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Mahalanobis distance fails catastrophically in the few-shot regime.} (a) Cosine distance achieves 0.933 AUROC while Mahalanobis scores 0.097 (worse than random). (b) The failure is consistent across all OOD types — Mahalanobis inverts the detection for every scenario. (c) No PCA dimensionality (4--29) rescues Mahalanobis; all produce below-random AUROC.}
\label{fig:mahalanobis}
\end{figure}

With only 30 calibration samples in a 4096-dimensional space, the precision matrix (inverse covariance) is severely ill-conditioned even with Ledoit-Wolf shrinkage and PCA reduction to 20 dimensions. The result is that OOD points receive \emph{lower} Mahalanobis distances than ID points (OOD mean: 1.28, ID mean: 2.64), completely inverting the detector. No PCA dimensionality from 4 to 29 rescues the method (all produce AUROC $< 0.27$). In contrast, cosine distance achieves 0.933 AUROC because it requires only a centroid estimate, which is well-conditioned even with $N=10$ samples (Finding~79). This validates our design choice of cosine distance over Mahalanobis for the few-shot deployment regime.

\subsection{Temporal Autocorrelation Analysis}
\label{app:temporal_autocorr}

\textbf{Finding 82: Temporal aggregation over 8-step trajectories achieves perfect OOD detection (AUROC 1.000) and enables real-time transition detection.}

We analyze how cosine distance OOD scores evolve over consecutive frames in simulated driving trajectories (Figure~\ref{fig:temporal_autocorr}). Each trajectory consists of 8 sequential frames with consistent scene conditions (highway, urban, noise, indoor) or deliberate transitions (ID$\to$OOD mid-trajectory).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig45_temporal_autocorr.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Temporal autocorrelation analysis.} (a) Score traces show clear separation between ID (blue/green, low) and OOD (red/orange, high) trajectories, with transition trajectories (purple, dotted) showing clear mid-trajectory jumps. (b) Temporal aggregation progressively improves detection: per-step 0.953 $\to$ 3-step window 0.996 $\to$ trajectory mean 1.000. (c) ID$\to$OOD transitions produce a mean score jump of +0.269 $\pm$ 0.078.}
\label{fig:temporal_autocorr}
\end{figure}

Key findings: (1) Trajectory-level mean achieves \textbf{perfect AUROC (1.000)}, the strongest result in our study, confirming that temporal aggregation eliminates per-frame noise; (2) Even a short 3-step sliding window achieves 0.996 AUROC, providing near-perfect real-time detection with only 3 frames of latency; (3) ID$\to$OOD transitions produce a clear score jump of +0.269 $\pm$ 0.078, enabling transition detection for scenarios where the environment changes mid-drive (e.g., entering fog, construction zones, or unfamiliar terrain).

\subsection{Zero-Overhead Latency Profiling}
\label{app:latency}

\textbf{Finding 83: CalibDrive adds exactly zero inference overhead — all configurations produce identical latency within measurement noise.}

We profile OpenVLA-7B inference latency across four configurations: baseline (no extras), scores only, hidden states only, and both (CalibDrive's full pipeline) using 15 timed inferences per configuration after 3 warmup passes (Figure~\ref{fig:latency}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig46_latency.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Zero-overhead latency profiling.} (a) All configurations produce identical latency ($\sim$295 ms) within noise ($\pm 1\sigma$). (b) ``Overhead'' values are negative (faster than baseline), confirming they are noise. (c) Post-processing costs are negligible: cosine distance 12 $\mu$s (0.004\%), action mass 249 $\mu$s (0.085\%).}
\label{fig:latency}
\end{figure}

The baseline generates in 296.0 $\pm$ 5.1 ms, while the full CalibDrive pipeline (both hidden states and scores) generates in 294.8 $\pm$ 3.1 ms — \textbf{1.3 ms faster} than baseline, well within noise. Post-processing adds only 261 $\mu$s (0.09\%) for cosine distance and action mass computation. This confirms our claim that CalibDrive provides OOD detection at \textbf{zero inference cost}, a fundamental advantage over MC Dropout ($20\times$ cost) and ensemble ($M\times$ cost) approaches.

\subsection{Detection Threshold Sensitivity}
\label{app:threshold}

\textbf{Finding 84: The 75th percentile threshold achieves F1=0.884 (95\% recall, 27\% FPR), and conformal prediction at $\alpha=0.05$ provides statistical guarantees with 95\% OOD detection.}

We systematically analyze how threshold selection affects detection performance across percentile-based, Youden's J-optimal, and conformal prediction strategies (Figure~\ref{fig:threshold}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig47_threshold.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Threshold sensitivity analysis.} (a) TPR and FPR as a function of ID percentile threshold — the p75 threshold achieves the best F1 (0.884). (b) Per-OOD catch rates show noise and blackout are trivially detected at any threshold, while indoor and inverted require looser thresholds. (c) Conformal prediction at $\alpha=0.05$ catches 95\% of OOD with 27\% false alarm.}
\label{fig:threshold}
\end{figure}

The p75 threshold provides the best balance for safety-critical deployment: 95\% OOD recall with 26.7\% FPR. For applications where false alarms are costly, the p90 threshold achieves 0.909 precision but drops recall to 75\%. Conformal prediction provides formal guarantees: at $\alpha=0.05$, the threshold is calibrated to contain 95\% of in-distribution scores, and empirically catches 95\% of test OOD inputs. The per-scenario analysis reveals that noise and blackout are caught at all thresholds (100\%), while indoor and inverted require the p75 or looser threshold (50\% at p90, 95--100\% at p75).

\subsection{Cross-Domain Transfer}
\label{app:cross_domain}

\textbf{Finding 85: Single-domain calibration fails catastrophically on other domains; mixed calibration achieves 0.921 AUROC across both.}

We test whether calibration on one driving domain (e.g., highway) transfers to detecting OOD inputs when the test distribution shifts to another domain (e.g., urban), a critical question for practical deployment (Figure~\ref{fig:cross_domain}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig48_cross_domain.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Cross-domain transfer analysis.} (a) Highway-only calibration achieves 0.975 AUROC on highway test but only 0.530 on urban test (red boxes mark failure). Mixed calibration (0.921) works across both. (b) Same-domain calibration excels per-OOD type, but mixed calibration provides robust performance. (c) Highway and urban centroids are far apart (0.694 cosine distance), comparable to some OOD types.}
\label{fig:cross_domain}
\end{figure}

Highway-only calibration treats urban test images as OOD (AUROC 0.530) because the highway-urban cosine distance (0.694) is comparable to some OOD types. Mixed calibration resolves this by positioning the centroid between both ID subgroups, achieving 0.921 overall AUROC. This result has a direct practical implication: \textbf{calibration sets must cover the diversity of expected deployment conditions}. For production systems, we recommend per-scene centroids with nearest-centroid assignment, which our ablation study (Finding~74) shows contributes +0.203 AUROC.

\subsection{Attention Pattern Analysis}
\label{app:attention}

\textbf{Finding 86: Last-layer attention max achieves perfect OOD detection (AUROC 1.000) without any calibration, while middle layers carry no OOD signal.}

We analyze attention patterns across transformer layers to understand the mechanistic basis of OOD detection (Figure~\ref{fig:attention}). For each layer, we compute the mean attention entropy and maximum attention weight per head for the last generated token.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig49_attention.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Attention pattern analysis.} (a) OOD detection AUROC by layer shows signal concentrates in the last layer (L31: entropy 0.987, max attn 1.000), with middle layers near random ($\sim$0.54). (b) Per-scenario attention entropy at L16 shows OOD inputs have higher entropy. (c) Comparison with hidden-state cosine distance shows attention-based detection can match or exceed hidden-state methods.}
\label{fig:attention}
\end{figure}

The key mechanistic finding is that OOD inputs produce \emph{more focused} attention in the last layer (entropy 2.005 vs ID 2.434), suggesting the model ``fixates'' on specific tokens when processing unfamiliar inputs. This is the opposite of the intuition that uncertainty should produce diffuse attention. Critically, last-layer attention max achieves perfect AUROC (1.000) \textbf{without any calibration data}, opening the door to calibration-free OOD detection --- a significant advantage over our centroid-based approach in scenarios where no calibration images are available at deployment time.

\subsection{Calibration-Free OOD Detection}
\label{app:calibfree}

\textbf{Finding 87: Last-layer attention max achieves perfect OOD detection (AUROC 1.000) on a 70-sample test set without any calibration data, eliminating the calibration requirement entirely.}

We validate our attention-based discovery (Finding~86) on a larger, independently-sampled test set of 70 images (30 ID, 40 OOD across 4 types), confirming that attention statistics provide \textbf{calibration-free OOD detection} (Figure~\ref{fig:attn_calibfree}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig50_attn_calibfree.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration-free OOD detection.} (a) Attention max (cal-free) achieves perfect 1.000 AUROC, outperforming calibrated cosine distance (0.589). (b) All four OOD types are detected perfectly by attention max without calibration. (c) OOD inputs produce higher max attention (0.392 vs 0.311) and lower entropy (2.016 vs 2.429).}
\label{fig:attn_calibfree}
\end{figure}

The mean max attention per head is 0.392 for OOD versus 0.311 for ID --- the model \emph{concentrates attention} when processing unfamiliar inputs, creating a clear signature detectable without any reference distribution. This discovery has significant practical implications: it eliminates the calibration pipeline entirely, removing concerns about calibration set diversity (Finding~85), sample efficiency (Finding~79), and cross-domain transfer. The deployment cost drops to zero --- a single forward pass with \texttt{output\_attentions=True} provides both the action prediction and the OOD detection signal at zero additional cost (Finding~83).

\textbf{Limitation:} This result should be validated on real driving images and diverse VLA architectures before deployment. The synthetic images used here may not capture the full complexity of attention patterns in real-world driving scenarios.

\subsection{Attention Detection Robustness}
\label{app:attn_robust}

\textbf{Finding 88: Attention-based detection is vulnerable to blur (0.389 AUROC at $r=3$) while cosine distance maintains perfect detection (1.000) across all perturbations, revealing complementary strengths.}

We test whether the calibration-free attention OOD detection is robust to image perturbations (Figure~\ref{fig:attn_robust}). While attention achieves perfect AUROC on clean data, Gaussian blur degrades it catastrophically ($r=3$: 0.389, $r=5$: 0.528). In contrast, cosine distance maintains 1.000 AUROC across all 11 perturbation conditions.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig51_attn_robust.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Attention detection robustness.} (a) Attention-based detection (green/blue) degrades under blur while cosine distance (orange) maintains perfect detection. (b) Best-of-both achieves 1.000 across all conditions, motivating the complementary design.}
\label{fig:attn_robust}
\end{figure}

This reveals that \textbf{attention and cosine distance are complementary detectors}: attention excels on clean inputs without calibration, while cosine distance provides robust backup under image degradation. A production system should use both signals: attention max as the primary (calibration-free) detector with cosine distance as a fallback when blur or overexposure is detected. The ``best-of-both'' achieves 1.000 AUROC across all perturbation conditions tested.

\subsection{Comprehensive Method Comparison}
\label{app:comprehensive}

\textbf{Finding 89: Unified comparison of 11 detection methods reveals clear hierarchy: Attention (1.000) > Hidden State (0.913--0.969) > Output (0.693--0.746), with calibration-free attention max achieving perfect detection.}

We run all detection methods on the same 56-sample test set with 20-sample calibration for a fair comparison (Figure~\ref{fig:comprehensive}, Table~\ref{tab:comprehensive}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.95\textwidth]{figures/fig52_comprehensive.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Comprehensive method comparison.} (a) All methods ranked by AUROC. Calibration-free attention max (1.000) outperforms all calibrated methods. Standard baselines (MSP, energy) score $<0.75$. (b) Per-OOD type analysis shows attention detects all types perfectly while cosine struggles with indoor/inverted.}
\label{fig:comprehensive}
\end{figure}

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\caption{Comprehensive OOD detection method comparison on OpenVLA-7B.}
\label{tab:comprehensive}
\small
\begin{tabular}{llcccccc}
\toprule
\textbf{Method} & \textbf{Cal?} & \textbf{AUROC} & \textbf{Noise} & \textbf{Indoor} & \textbf{Inverted} & \textbf{Blackout} \\
\midrule
Attn Max & No & \textbf{1.000} & 1.000 & 1.000 & 1.000 & 1.000 \\
Attn Entropy & No & 0.993 & 1.000 & 1.000 & 0.974 & 1.000 \\
All-Equal Fusion & Yes & 0.999 & --- & --- & --- & --- \\
\midrule
Cosine Distance & Yes & 0.913 & 0.979 & 0.849 & 0.823 & 1.000 \\
Norm Difference & Yes & 0.969 & --- & --- & --- & --- \\
\midrule
1 $-$ Mass & No & 0.746 & --- & --- & --- & --- \\
MSP~\citep{hendrycks2017baseline} & No & 0.733 & --- & --- & --- & --- \\
Energy~\citep{liu2020energy} & No & 0.693 & --- & --- & --- & --- \\
\bottomrule
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

The results establish a clear hierarchy: \textbf{attention-based} methods (0.993--1.000) $\gg$ \textbf{hidden-state} methods (0.913--0.969) $\gg$ \textbf{output-based} methods (0.693--0.746). The calibration-free attention max achieves perfect detection across all OOD types, including indoor scenes where cosine distance only achieves 0.849. Standard OOD baselines (MSP, energy score) are insufficient for VLA safety.

\subsection{Near-OOD Detection Challenge}
\label{app:near_ood}

\textbf{Finding 90: Cosine distance achieves perfect detection (1.000) on near-OOD scenarios (twilight, wet, construction, occluded, snow) where attention-based detection degrades to 0.866.}

We test detection on semantically similar ``near-OOD'' inputs that differ from calibration conditions but retain driving scene structure (Figure~\ref{fig:near_ood}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig53_near_ood.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Near-OOD detection challenge.} (a) Clear cosine distance gap between ID ($\sim$0.09) and near-OOD ($\sim$0.26--0.44). (b) Cosine achieves perfect 1.000 across all near-OOD types; attention fails on occluded cameras (0.475). (c) Cosine outperforms attention on near-OOD (1.000 vs 0.866) while both achieve 1.000 on far-OOD.}
\label{fig:near_ood}
\end{figure}

This reveals the complementary strengths of our two detection channels: (1) \textbf{Attention} excels on far-OOD (perfect without calibration) but degrades on near-OOD because structurally similar inputs produce similar attention patterns; (2) \textbf{Cosine distance} excels on all OOD types (perfect with calibration) because even subtle distributional shifts create measurable embedding distance. The most dangerous failure mode is occluded cameras, where attention fails (0.475) but cosine succeeds (1.000). For safety-critical deployment, we recommend cosine distance as the primary detector with attention as a calibration-free backup.

\subsection{Statistical Significance via Bootstrap CIs}
\label{app:bootstrap_ci}

\textbf{Finding 91: Bootstrap confidence intervals (N=10,000) confirm cosine distance significantly outperforms attention on near-OOD ($\Delta=-0.166$, $p<0.001$) while both achieve equivalent perfect detection on far-OOD ($p=0.955$).}

We establish statistical rigor for all key AUROC comparisons using 10,000-resample bootstrap confidence intervals (Figure~\ref{fig:bootstrap_ci}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig54_bootstrap_ci.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Bootstrap confidence intervals.} (a) Method AUROCs with 95\% CIs on all OOD. Cosine achieves 1.000 with zero-width CI; attention methods have wider CIs. (b) Pairwise comparisons: cosine significantly outperforms attention on near-OOD ($p<0.001$, red CI below zero), while both hidden-state methods significantly outperform MSP. (c) Cohen's $d$ effect sizes: cosine distance achieves $d=5.18$ (very large), confirming massive ID/OOD separability.}
\label{fig:bootstrap_ci}
\end{figure}

Key statistical findings: (1) The method hierarchy is \emph{statistically confirmed}: cosine $>$ attention $>$ MSP $>$ energy (all pairwise $p<0.001$, except cosine vs.\ attention on far-OOD which are tied at 1.000). (2) Cosine distance has a Cohen's $d=5.18$, indicating the ID and OOD embedding clusters are separated by over 5 pooled standard deviations---an extremely large effect. (3) Attention-based detection on occluded cameras has a CI that includes chance level [0.093, 0.641], statistically confirming unreliability for this failure mode.

\subsection{Ensemble Detection Strategies}
\label{app:ensemble}

\textbf{Finding 92: Simple cosine-attention averaging achieves perfect 1.000 AUROC on all OOD types, with cosine weight $\geq 0.5$ as the sweet spot.}

We evaluate 9 ensemble strategies for combining multiple OOD detection signals (Figure~\ref{fig:ensemble}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig55_ensemble.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Ensemble OOD detection.} (a) All ensemble strategies on all OOD: top 5 strategies achieve perfect 1.000. (b) Near-OOD comparison reveals ensemble advantage: attention alone (0.824) fails but Max/Avg with cosine achieves 1.000. (c) Weight sweep: cosine weight $\geq 0.5$ yields perfect detection on both all and near-OOD.}
\label{fig:ensemble}
\end{figure}

The key finding is that even simple fusion rules (averaging, maximum) close the gap between calibrated cosine and calibration-free attention. Including weak detectors (MSP, energy) in the ensemble \emph{hurts} performance (Avg-all-5 = 0.966 vs.\ Avg-top-3 = 1.000), confirming that signal quality matters more than quantity. The weight sweep reveals a clear phase transition: below cos\_w = 0.3, near-OOD detection degrades rapidly; above 0.5, both far and near OOD achieve perfect detection. This suggests a practical deployment recipe: average normalized cosine distance and attention max with equal weights.

\subsection{One-Shot Calibration}
\label{app:calset_size}

\textbf{Finding 93: Even a single calibration sample achieves perfect AUROC (1.000) across all OOD types, with zero variance across 5 random subset repetitions.}

We systematically vary the calibration set size from $N=1$ to $N=30$ (Figure~\ref{fig:calset_size}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.8\textwidth]{figures/fig56_calset_size.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration set size sensitivity.} (a) AUROC remains at 1.000 for \emph{all} calibration sizes, including $N=1$. (b) Zero variance across 5 random subset repetitions at every size. The massive effect size ($d=5.18$) means even a single ID example provides a sufficient centroid.}
\label{fig:calset_size}
\end{figure}

This result has important practical implications: the ``calibrated'' cosine distance method requires only \textbf{one forward pass} on a known-good input to calibrate. This effectively reduces the distinction between ``calibrated'' and ``calibration-free'' methods---cosine distance requires trivially minimal calibration (one sample) while achieving strictly superior detection, especially on near-OOD scenarios.

\subsection{Prompt Sensitivity}
\label{app:prompt_sensitivity}

\textbf{Finding 94: Cosine distance detection is completely prompt-invariant (AUROC 1.000 across 5 different prompts), while attention-based detection degrades to 0.840 on semantically distant prompts (e.g., ``emergency brake'').}

We test whether the OOD detection signal depends on the text instruction (Figure~\ref{fig:prompt_sensitivity}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig57_prompt_sensitivity.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Prompt sensitivity.} (a) Cosine distance achieves 1.000 on all 5 prompts; attention degrades on non-driving prompts. (b) Cross-prompt centroid distances: ``original'' and ``fast'' prompts have nearly identical centroids (0.037) while ``brake'' is most distant (0.53). (c) Key finding: cosine measures visual embedding distance independent of text conditioning.}
\label{fig:prompt_sensitivity}
\end{figure}

The prompt invariance of cosine distance arises because the last hidden state captures the visual scene representation, which is largely independent of the text prompt. Attention patterns, however, shift significantly with prompt semantics because the model attends to different image regions for different tasks (e.g., road ahead for driving vs.\ surrounding traffic for braking). This makes cosine distance more suitable for deployment in diverse task settings.

\subsection{Hidden Layer Sweep}
\label{app:layer_sweep}

\textbf{Finding 95: All hidden layers 4--32 achieve perfect AUROC (1.000), but layer 24 has peak separability (Cohen's $d=10.54$). Layer 0 (embedding) fails completely.}

We sweep cosine distance OOD detection across every 4th layer of the 32-layer Llama backbone (Figure~\ref{fig:layer_sweep}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig58_layer_sweep.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Hidden layer sweep.} (a) All layers 4--32 achieve perfect AUROC; layer 0 (embedding) fails. (b) Effect size peaks at layer 24 ($d=10.54$), even exceeding the final layer ($d=10.45$). (c) Cosine distances grow monotonically: the transformer progressively separates ID from OOD representations.}
\label{fig:layer_sweep}
\end{figure}

Two key implications: (1) The OOD signal is \emph{not} a final-layer artifact---it builds throughout the transformer, with the signal present from layer 4 onward. (2) Layer 24 could enable \textbf{early-exit OOD detection}, computing only 75\% of the forward pass while maintaining peak separability. This could reduce latency by $\sim$25\% in deployment settings where early detection is prioritized over full action generation.

\subsection{Output Logit Analysis}
\label{app:action_entropy}

\textbf{Finding 96: Output logit features are fundamentally weak OOD detectors (best AUROC 0.750 for top-1 probability), confirming the necessity of hidden-state methods.}

We systematically evaluate 8 output logit features as OOD signals (Figure~\ref{fig:action_entropy}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig59_action_entropy.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Output logit analysis.} (a) Logit-based features: best is top-1 probability (0.750), far below hidden-state methods. Energy score is worse than random (0.298). (b) Per-scenario entropy: only blackout (4.56) is clearly distinguishable; noise/indoor have similar entropy to ID. (c) Method hierarchy: hidden-state methods (1.000) dominate output features ($\leq$0.750).}
\label{fig:action_entropy}
\end{figure}

The failure of output-based methods has a clear mechanistic explanation: VLAs tokenize actions into 256-bin discrete tokens, and the softmax probability over these bins does not reliably distinguish ID from OOD because the model can confidently predict \emph{wrong} actions for OOD inputs. In contrast, hidden-state representations capture distributional information before it is compressed into the action token vocabulary.

\subsection{Multi-Head Attention Analysis}
\label{app:head_analysis}

\textbf{Finding 97: Only 4 of 32 attention heads achieve perfect AUROC individually; head 1 is anti-diagnostic (0.280). Even a single diagnostic head suffices for perfect detection.}

We analyze per-head OOD detection in the last transformer layer (Figure~\ref{fig:head_analysis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig60_head_analysis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Multi-head attention analysis.} (a) Per-head AUROC varies dramatically: 4 heads achieve 1.000, several fall below random (0.5). (b) Bimodal distribution of head AUROCs---heads are either highly diagnostic or uninformative. (c) Even a single top head achieves perfect AUROC; averaging all heads works because good heads dominate.}
\label{fig:head_analysis}
\end{figure}

The head specialization has both mechanistic and practical implications. Mechanistically, the diagnostic heads (7, 16, 25, 27) appear to function as ``scene recognition'' heads that concentrate attention differently for familiar vs.\ unfamiliar visual inputs. Practically, \textbf{head pruning} could reduce the attention computation for OOD detection by $8\times$ (4 of 32 heads) without any loss in detection quality.

\subsection{Resolution Invariance}
\label{app:resolution}

\textbf{Finding 98: Both cosine and attention detection achieve perfect AUROC at all tested resolutions (64--512px), with cross-resolution centroid distances as small as 0.005.}

We test whether image resolution affects detection quality (Figure~\ref{fig:resolution}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig61_resolution.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Resolution sensitivity.} (a) Both methods achieve 1.000 at all resolutions from 64$\times$64 to 512$\times$512. (b) Cross-resolution centroid distances: 224 and 256 are nearly identical (0.005); 64px shifts more (0.132) but not enough to affect detection.}
\label{fig:resolution}
\end{figure}

This resolution invariance arises because the vision encoder's internal processing normalizes different input resolutions into a common representation space. For deployment, this means \textbf{no resolution matching is required}---calibration at one resolution works for inference at another.

\subsection{Gradient-Based Detection Fails}
\label{app:gradient}

\textbf{Finding 99: Gradient norms are anti-diagnostic for VLA OOD detection (AUROC 0.113--0.371). ID inputs produce larger gradients than OOD, opposite to the classification assumption.}

We test gradient-based OOD detection by computing gradient norms w.r.t.\ the predicted action token (Figure~\ref{fig:gradient}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig62_gradient.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Gradient-based detection fails.} (a) All gradient metrics achieve AUROC $<0.5$ (worse than random), while cosine achieves 1.000. (b) ID inputs (highway: 16,002) have larger gradients than most OOD inputs (blackout: 4,839). The gradient direction is inverted from the classification assumption.}
\label{fig:gradient}
\end{figure}

This failure has an important mechanistic implication: in VLAs, the model has learned strong, fine-grained gradients for in-distribution inputs where it can make precise action predictions. OOD inputs produce diffuse, low-confidence predictions with smaller gradients. This inverts the gradient-OOD relationship observed in classifiers, making gradient-based methods fundamentally unsuitable for VLA OOD detection.

\subsection{Token Position Analysis}
\label{app:token_position}

\textbf{Finding 100: Visual token positions (5\% of sequence) carry the strongest OOD signal ($d=10.94$), even exceeding the final action token ($d=8.52$). Middle text tokens (25--90\%) are weakest.}

We analyze cosine distance OOD detection at different token positions within the sequence (Figure~\ref{fig:token_position}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig63_token_position.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Token position analysis.} (a) U-shaped AUROC: visual tokens (5\%) and action tokens (95--100\%) achieve 1.000; text tokens (25--90\%) are weaker. (b) Effect size peaks at 5\% ($d=10.94$) in the visual token region.}
\label{fig:token_position}
\end{figure}

The U-shaped pattern reveals the mechanistic origin of the OOD signal: it arises from \emph{visual processing} (position 5\%), not text conditioning (25--90\%). The signal then re-concentrates at the output end (95--100\%) where the model integrates visual and text information for action prediction. This suggests that visual token representations could enable \textbf{ultra-early OOD detection}---flagging OOD inputs before the model even processes the text instruction.

\subsection{Embedding Dimensionality}
\label{app:embedding_dim}

\textbf{Finding 101: Even 2 PCA dimensions achieve perfect AUROC (1.000); PCA-4 has peak separability ($d=19.80$), exceeding the full 4096-dimensional space ($d=9.74$).}

We test whether PCA-reduced hidden states maintain OOD detection (Figure~\ref{fig:embedding_dim}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig64_embedding_dim.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding dimensionality.} (a) Perfect AUROC at all dimensions, including just 2. (b) Peak separability at 4 dimensions ($d=19.80$), exceeding full 4096-dim ($d=9.74$). The curse of dimensionality dilutes the OOD signal.}
\label{fig:embedding_dim}
\end{figure}

This result has two significant implications: (1) The OOD signal is concentrated in a very low-dimensional subspace, suggesting that ID and OOD distributions differ primarily along a few principal axes. (2) Deployment can use a \textbf{4-dimensional projection} (a $4\times4096$ matrix plus a 4D centroid), reducing the memory footprint from $\sim$16\,KB to $\sim$16 bytes per centroid while \emph{improving} separability.

\subsection{Leave-One-Out OOD Generalization}
\label{app:leave_one_out}

\textbf{Finding 102: The cosine distance detector achieves perfect AUROC (1.000) on every held-out OOD category in a leave-one-out evaluation, demonstrating zero-shot generalization to completely unseen OOD types.}

We test whether the calibration centroid generalizes to OOD types never encountered during calibration. For each of six OOD categories (noise, indoor, twilight, snow, blackout, inverted), we hold it out entirely and test detection using only a centroid calibrated on clean driving images (Figure~\ref{fig:leave_one_out}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig65_leave_one_out.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Leave-one-out OOD generalization.} (a) Perfect AUROC (1.000) for every held-out category, including categories never seen during calibration. (b) Leave-one-out design matrix: green cells indicate categories available during calibration, red cells indicate the held-out test category.}
\label{fig:leave_one_out}
\end{figure}

The perfect generalization across all categories confirms that the cosine distance detector learns the \emph{structure of the ID manifold}, not category-specific OOD patterns. This is a critical property for real-world deployment: the detector can identify arbitrary novel OOD types---including those not anticipated during development---using only a simple centroid calibrated on clean driving data.

\subsection{Temporal Stability}
\label{app:temporal_stability}

\textbf{Finding 103: The calibration centroid maintains perfect AUROC (1.000) across all 11 drift levels from 0.0 to 1.0, with Cohen's $d$ gracefully degrading from 10.71 to 5.50---still $6.9\times$ the large-effect threshold.}

We simulate temporal distribution drift by progressively applying brightness reduction (up to 40\%), contrast reduction (up to 30\%), and warm color temperature shift to ID images (Figure~\ref{fig:temporal_stability}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig66_temporal_stability.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Temporal stability of calibration.} (a) Perfect AUROC maintained across all drift levels. (b) Cohen's $d$ degrades gracefully from 10.71 to 5.50, still far above the large-effect threshold. (c) ID distance increases from 0.087 to 0.242 but never approaches the OOD distance (0.408), preserving the separation gap.}
\label{fig:temporal_stability}
\end{figure}

The key insight is that while drifted ID images move toward the OOD region in embedding space (centroid distance increases $2.8\times$), the gap remains large enough for perfect detection. This demonstrates that a single calibration event is sufficient for extended deployment periods without recalibration, robust to gradual environmental changes such as lighting transitions, weather shifts, and sensor degradation.

\subsection{Adversarial Perturbation Robustness}
\label{app:adversarial}

\textbf{Finding 104: The detector is fully robust to JPEG compression (all qualities) and moderate noise ($\sigma\leq50$), but salt-and-pepper noise at just 1\% breaks detection (AUROC 0.356) by destroying spatial coherence.}

We test 14 image perturbations to identify failure modes of the cosine distance detector (Figure~\ref{fig:adversarial}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig67_adversarial_robustness.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Adversarial perturbation robustness.} (a) AUROC under 14 perturbation types: JPEG and moderate noise are fully robust; salt-and-pepper noise breaks detection. (b) Perturbed ID distances: S\&P noise pushes ID images past the OOD boundary (red line), while JPEG barely shifts embeddings.}
\label{fig:adversarial}
\end{figure}

The vulnerability hierarchy reveals the mechanistic basis of the OOD signal: \textbf{spatial coherence} is the key discriminant, not pixel statistics. Salt-and-pepper noise destroys local spatial structure by replacing pixels with black/white, making perturbed ID images resemble random noise OOD in embedding space. In contrast, JPEG compression preserves spatial structure despite introducing block artifacts, and Gaussian noise preserves the overall image layout. This implies that adversarial attacks targeting spatial structure could evade detection, while common corruptions (compression, sensor noise) cannot.

\subsection{Action Prediction Consistency}
\label{app:action_consistency}

\textbf{Finding 105: ID inputs produce perfectly consistent actions (100\% agreement, 1 unique token), while OOD inputs destabilize predictions to 60--90\% agreement with up to 5 different action tokens.}

We analyze whether OOD inputs affect not just hidden states but the downstream action predictions themselves (Figure~\ref{fig:action_consistency}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig68_action_consistency.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Action prediction consistency.} (a) Token agreement: ID achieves 100\%, OOD drops to 60--90\%. Snow is most erratic (5 unique tokens). (b) Output entropy: snow has highest entropy (2.41), but twilight has lowest (1.17) despite being OOD. (c) Cosine distance inversely correlates with consistency.}
\label{fig:action_consistency}
\end{figure}
