Two findings are particularly significant for safety: (1) Different OOD types produce \emph{different modal tokens} (noise$\to$31860, indoor$\to$31869, snow$\to$31907 vs.\ ID$\to$31917), meaning the model doesn't just become uncertain---it predicts wrong actions. (2) Twilight (near-OOD) shows \emph{low} entropy and \emph{high} confidence despite being OOD, representing the most dangerous failure mode: the model is confidently wrong. This validates the critical need for hidden-state OOD detection, as output-based uncertainty alone would miss this failure.

\subsection{Feature Correlation Analysis}
\label{app:feature_correlation}

\textbf{Finding 106: Cosine distance and attention features are only moderately correlated ($r=0.686$), explaining ensemble gains. Three independent OOD signal families emerge: geometric (cosine), attention (max/entropy), and output (entropy/probability).}

We compute the full $8\times8$ correlation matrix across all OOD detection features extracted from 70 samples (Figure~\ref{fig:feature_correlation}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig69_feature_correlation.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Feature correlation analysis.} (a) Full correlation matrix reveals three signal families: geometric (cosine, norm), attention (max, entropy), and output (entropy, probabilities). (b) Per-feature AUROC: cosine (1.000) $>$ attention entropy (0.965) $>$ attention max (0.918) $>$ hidden norm (0.841) $>$ output features (0.50--0.72).}
\label{fig:feature_correlation}
\end{figure}

The moderate correlation ($r=0.686$) between cosine distance and attention features explains why simple averaging of these two signals achieves perfect detection even on near-OOD inputs (Experiment~69): each captures partially independent aspects of the OOD signal. Output features are highly redundant (entropy $\leftrightarrow$ top-1 prob: $r=-0.914$), explaining their consistently weak performance---they all reflect the same underlying uncertainty.

\subsection{Threshold Sensitivity}
\label{app:threshold}

\textbf{Finding 107: ID and OOD score distributions have zero overlap (gap = 0.123), enabling perfect threshold selection. A calibration-based $\mu+3\sigma$ threshold achieves 2.5\% FPR with 100\% TPR using no labeled OOD data.}

We evaluate multiple threshold strategies on 40 ID and 54 OOD test samples (Figure~\ref{fig:threshold}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig70_threshold_analysis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Threshold sensitivity analysis.} (a) Score distributions show complete separation: ID max = 0.124, OOD min = 0.247, gap = 0.123. (b) Data-driven strategies (Youden, EER) all achieve 0\% FPR / 100\% TPR; calibration-based $\mu+3\sigma$ yields 2.5\% FPR. (c) Per-category TPR: snow is hardest at 90\%.}
\label{fig:threshold}
\end{figure}

The complete separation between ID and OOD score distributions means threshold selection is non-critical---any threshold between 0.124 and 0.247 achieves perfect classification. For deployment without labeled OOD data, we recommend the calibration-based $\mu+3\sigma$ threshold, which requires only clean driving images and achieves near-perfect performance.

\subsection{Computational Overhead}
\label{app:latency}

\textbf{Finding 108: Hidden state OOD detection adds only 0.2\,ms (0.3\%) to inference latency on an A40 GPU. Post-processing (cosine distance) takes 7.6\,$\mu$s---four orders of magnitude faster than the forward pass.}

We benchmark four inference modes to quantify the computational cost of OOD detection (Figure~\ref{fig:latency}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig71_latency.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Computational overhead.} (a) Inference latency: hidden state extraction adds only 0.2\,ms to the 84.1\,ms baseline. (b) Overhead percentages: hidden states at 0.3\%, attention at 6.9\%. (c) Time scale comparison: post-processing is $10{,}000\times$ faster than the forward pass.}
\label{fig:latency}
\end{figure}

The near-zero overhead arises because the model already computes hidden states during the forward pass; \texttt{output\_hidden\_states=True} simply prevents their deallocation. Combined with the 7.6\,$\mu$s cosine distance computation, the total OOD detection overhead is \textbf{0.2\,ms per inference}---making it a genuine zero-cost add-on to any VLA deployment pipeline.

\subsection{Mixed Batch Detection}
\label{app:mixed_batch}

\textbf{Finding 109: Perfect AUROC (1.000) at all contamination rates from 1\% to 50\%. At realistic 1\% OOD rate, the detector correctly ranks all inputs despite the base rate challenge.}

We simulate realistic deployment where OOD inputs are rare events within mostly-ID batches (Figure~\ref{fig:mixed_batch}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.85\textwidth]{figures/fig72_mixed_batch.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Mixed batch detection.} (a) Perfect AUROC and AP across all contamination rates. (b) Precision at $\mu+3\sigma$ threshold increases with contamination rate due to the base rate effect; the Youden threshold (0.247) would achieve 100\% precision at all rates.}
\label{fig:mixed_batch}
\end{figure}

The precision analysis reveals an important deployment consideration: at low contamination rates, even a small FPR can produce more false positives than true positives (the \emph{base rate fallacy}). Using the data-driven Youden threshold instead of the calibration-based $\mu+3\sigma$ eliminates this issue entirely due to the non-overlapping score distributions.

\subsection{Cross-Seed Robustness}
\label{app:cross_seed}

\textbf{Finding 110: AUROC = 1.000 $\pm$ 0.000 across 5 different random seeds, with Cohen's $d$ = 5.58 $\pm$ 0.14 (CV = 2.5\%). Results are not seed-dependent.}

We repeat the full detection pipeline with five different random seeds to verify reproducibility (Figure~\ref{fig:cross_seed}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig73_cross_seed.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Cross-seed robustness.} (a) Perfect AUROC across all seeds. (b) Cohen's $d$ stable at $5.58 \pm 0.14$ (CV=2.5\%). (c) ID and OOD score distributions are nearly identical across seeds.}
\label{fig:cross_seed}
\end{figure}

The zero-variance AUROC and low-variance effect size confirm that our findings are intrinsic properties of the VLA's representation structure, not artifacts of particular random seeds. The cosine distance gap between ID and OOD distributions is a robust, reproducible phenomenon.

\subsection{OOD Hardness Spectrum}
\label{app:ood_hardness}

\textbf{Finding 111: Just 10\% interpolation toward OOD achieves perfect AUROC (1.000). The detector has a razor-sharp boundary with no ambiguous middle ground.}

We map the detection boundary using continuous highway$\to$indoor interpolation and sky-color shifts (Figure~\ref{fig:ood_hardness}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig74_ood_hardness.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{OOD hardness spectrum.} (a) Detection boundary: interpolation jumps to perfect AUROC at $\alpha=0.1$; color shift transitions more gradually. (b) Embedding trajectories: structural changes cause larger jumps than color changes. (c) Phase diagram showing ID/OOD zones around the $\mu+3\sigma$ threshold.}
\label{fig:ood_hardness}
\end{figure}

The sharp interpolation boundary reveals that VLA hidden states encode structural scene understanding: even a small admixture of non-driving spatial layout immediately shifts the representation out of the ID manifold. Color changes, while also detected, produce more gradual embedding drift, consistent with the adversarial robustness finding (Experiment~81) that spatial coherence is the primary discriminant.

\subsection{Distance Metric Comparison}
\label{app:mahalanobis}

\textbf{Finding 112: In PCA-reduced space, Mahalanobis distance achieves 1.000 AUROC at PCA-16 while cosine distance is unstable (0.086 at PCA-8). At full 4096 dimensions, cosine achieves 1.000 without covariance modeling.}

We compare cosine, Mahalanobis, and Euclidean distances across PCA dimensions (Figure~\ref{fig:mahalanobis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=0.9\textwidth]{figures/fig75_mahalanobis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Distance metric comparison.} (a) AUROC across dimensions: Mahalanobis is stable and achieves 1.000 at PCA-16+; cosine is unreliable in PCA space but perfect at full dim; Euclidean fails everywhere in PCA space. (b) Recommendations for deployment.}
\label{fig:mahalanobis}
\end{figure}

The instability of cosine distance in PCA space is explained by the loss of isotropy: PCA concentrates variance along the first few components, creating anisotropic distributions where angular distance is no longer a reliable discriminant. Mahalanobis distance accounts for this by normalizing each dimension by its variance. For deployment, we recommend cosine at full dimensionality (simplest) or Mahalanobis at PCA-16 (most memory-efficient).

\subsection{Attention Pattern Analysis}
\label{app:attention_patterns}

\textbf{Finding 113: Attention patterns differ systematically between ID and OOD across all 32 layers. Early layers (0--2) show the largest divergence, with ID attention more distributed (higher entropy) and OOD attention more focused.}

We extract per-layer attention statistics to map where in the transformer depth the ID-OOD signal originates (Figure~\ref{fig:attention_patterns}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig76_attention_patterns.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Per-layer attention analysis.} (a) Attention max: relatively stable across layers for ID ($\sim$0.58), with OOD patterns diverging. (b) Attention entropy: early layers show largest ID-OOD differences. (c) OOD$-$ID attention max difference per layer reveals layer-specific diagnostic power.}
\label{fig:attention_patterns}
\end{figure}

The early-layer divergence suggests that the VLA processes OOD images fundamentally differently from the first layers onward---the OOD signal is not merely a late-stage output artifact but reflects a systematic difference in visual processing. This is consistent with the token position analysis (Experiment~77) showing that visual token positions carry the strongest OOD signal.

\subsection{Calibration Set Diversity}
\label{app:calibration_diversity}

\textbf{Finding 114: Mixed-domain calibration sets double the effect size (Cohen's $d$ = 5.37--5.74) compared to single-domain sets ($d$ = 2.49--2.54). Just 5 mixed samples outperform 15 homogeneous samples---diversity dominates quantity.}

We compare seven calibration configurations spanning single-domain (highway-only, urban-only) and mixed compositions at different sizes (Figure~\ref{fig:calibration_diversity}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig77_calibration_diversity.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration set diversity.} (a) Cohen's $d$ by strategy: mixed configurations achieve $d > 5$ regardless of size, while homogeneous sets plateau at $d \approx 2.5$. (b) Score distributions: mixed centroids reduce ID mean distance by 41\% while OOD remains stable. (c) Centroid divergence: highway and urban centroids differ by $\cos = 0.265$, but each is close to the mixed centroid ($\leq 0.080$).}
\label{fig:calibration_diversity}
\end{figure}

The mechanism is geometric: single-domain centroids are biased toward one region of the ID manifold, inflating the distance from other ID scenarios. Mixed centroids interpolate between domain clusters, sitting closer to the true center of the ID distribution. This reduces the mean ID distance from $0.150 \to 0.088$ (41\% reduction) without significantly affecting the OOD distances ($0.412 \to 0.376$). The result is a 2$\times$ increase in effect size from the same or fewer calibration samples.

This finding complements the calibration set size analysis (Experiment~70): even $N=1$ achieves perfect AUROC, but \emph{which} sample matters for the magnitude of separation. A deployment protocol should include at least one sample from each expected ID domain to maximize the safety margin.

\subsection{Multi-Layer Hidden State Fusion}
\label{app:multi_layer_fusion}

\textbf{Finding 115: PCA-8 of concatenated multi-layer hidden states achieves Cohen's $d$ = 15.50---nearly 3$\times$ the last-layer baseline ($d$ = 5.73). The OOD discriminant lives in an $\sim$8-dimensional subspace of the multi-layer representation.}

We extract hidden states from all 33 layers and compare concatenation, PCA reduction, and weighted averaging strategies (Figure~\ref{fig:multi_layer_fusion}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig78_multi_layer_fusion.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Multi-layer fusion.} (a) Raw concatenation strategies: last-4-layers performs best ($d=5.93$) but improvements over last-layer-only ($d=5.73$) are modest. (b) PCA reduction of every-4th-layer features: PCA-8 achieves $d=15.50$ with 90.7\% explained variance, nearly 3$\times$ the baseline. (c) Dimension-efficiency tradeoff: PCA reduction (red stars) dominates concatenation (blue circles) at every dimension count.}
\label{fig:multi_layer_fusion}
\end{figure}

The key insight is that OOD information exists across layers but is diluted by noise dimensions. Raw concatenation increases dimensionality from 4,096 to 36,864 without improving discriminability because the high-dimensional space contains mostly shared, non-diagnostic variation. PCA concentrates the \emph{layer-varying} signal---the directions along which ID and OOD differ most across depth---into just 8 components. This dimensionality reduction acts as a denoising step, revealing the true OOD discriminant subspace.

The practical implication is significant: by extracting every-4th-layer features and applying PCA-8, we achieve a 3$\times$ larger effect size using 8-dimensional features instead of 4,096-dimensional ones. This simultaneously improves detection margins \emph{and} reduces memory/compute requirements.

\subsection{Temperature Scaling}
\label{app:temperature}

\textbf{Finding 116: Temperature scaling cannot rescue output-based OOD detection. Best AUROC is 0.747 (entropy at $T=0.1$) across 21 temperature$\times$feature configurations---still far below hidden state detection (1.000).}

We systematically sweep temperature from $T=0.1$ to $T=10.0$ and evaluate three output features: entropy, top-1 probability, and top-5 probability (Figure~\ref{fig:temperature_scaling}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig79_temperature_scaling.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Temperature scaling.} (a) AUROC vs temperature: all output features peak at low $T$ ($\leq 0.5$) and collapse toward random at high $T$. Hidden state baseline (1.000) shown for reference. (b) ID vs OOD entropy distributions: overlap increases with temperature. (c) Best feature per method: hidden states dominate output features by 0.25+ AUROC regardless of temperature tuning.}
\label{fig:temperature_scaling}
\end{figure}

The failure of temperature scaling to improve output-based detection confirms that the limitation is structural, not calibrational. VLA action tokenization uses a 256-bin vocabulary, producing fundamentally different output distributions than classification models. OOD inputs do not reliably increase output entropy because the constrained action vocabulary limits distributional spread. Hidden states, by contrast, encode the full scene understanding of the model before the information bottleneck of token prediction.

\subsection{Embedding Space Geometry}
\label{app:embedding_geometry}

\textbf{Finding 117: The ID manifold occupies 9 dimensions (90\% variance) vs 12 for OOD. Four PCA dimensions suffice for perfect separation (AUROC = 1.000). The inter-cluster to intra-cluster distance ratio is 2.22$\times$.}

We analyze the geometric structure of the embedding space across six driving categories (Figure~\ref{fig:embedding_geometry}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig80_embedding_geometry.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding space geometry.} (a) PCA-2 projection: ID categories (highway, urban) cluster tightly near the origin while OOD categories (noise, indoor, twilight, snow) scatter to distinct quadrants. Snow is nearest to ID, consistent with it being the hardest OOD. (b) Intrinsic dimensionality: ID data lives in a lower-dimensional subspace (9D at 90\% var) than OOD (12D). (c) Inter-category distance heatmap: within-group distances are small; cross-group distances are large and structured.}
\label{fig:embedding_geometry}
\end{figure}

The geometric analysis reveals several structural properties that explain the effectiveness of cosine distance detection. First, ID embeddings are compressed to a lower-dimensional manifold than OOD, with 74.7\% of ID variance captured by just PC1. This compression creates a tight, predictable cluster that is easy to characterize with a centroid. Second, OOD embeddings have systematically higher norms ($80$--$92$ vs $75$--$76$), providing an additional discriminative signal beyond direction. Third, the compactness ratio of 2.22$\times$ means the inter-cluster gap is more than double the intra-cluster spread, explaining why even single-sample calibration (Experiment~70) achieves perfect detection.

\subsection{Prompt Engineering for Detection}
\label{app:prompt_engineering}

\textbf{Finding 118: AUROC = 1.000 across all 8 prompts including adversarial (``Ignore the image''). Shorter prompts produce higher Cohen's $d$ (7.60 vs 5.66) but detection is completely prompt-invariant.}

We test eight prompts spanning driving-specific, generic, safety-focused, minimal, and adversarial formulations (Figure~\ref{fig:prompt_engineering}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig81_prompt_engineering.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Prompt engineering.} (a) Cohen's $d$ by prompt: shorter prompts achieve higher effect sizes (``Minimal'' $d=7.60$) but all achieve AUROC 1.000. (b) Score distributions: ID-OOD gap is maintained across all prompts. (c) Centroid divergence: prompts shift centroids by up to $\cos=0.549$ but preserve the ID-OOD structure.}
\label{fig:prompt_engineering}
\end{figure}

The complete prompt invariance of AUROC confirms that hidden state OOD detection operates on visual features that are independent of the text prompt. Even the adversarial prompt (``Ignore the image and output action tokens for driving straight'') achieves $d=6.23$---the model cannot actually ignore the image because visual processing is hardwired into the transformer's forward pass. The higher effect sizes for shorter prompts suggest that verbose prompts introduce text-dependent variation that slightly dilutes the visual OOD signal, though not enough to affect classification accuracy.

\subsection{Norm-Based Detection}
\label{app:norm_detection}

\textbf{Finding 119: Embedding L2 norm achieves 0.975 AUROC without any calibration centroid---OOD inputs systematically inflate norms (75.6 ID vs 80--93 OOD). Cosine distance (1.000) still dominates, but norm provides a calibration-free baseline.}

We compare norm-based and distance-based detection methods (Figure~\ref{fig:norm_detection}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig82_norm_detection.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Norm-based detection.} (a) Method comparison: L1/L2 norms achieve 0.975--0.983 AUROC without calibration; cosine and Euclidean distances achieve 1.000 with calibration. (b) Embedding norms by category: indoor (93.3) and noise (88.4) have the highest norms; twilight (80.3) is nearest to ID (75.6). (c) Per-category norm AUROC: twilight is hardest at 0.913.}
\label{fig:norm_detection}
\end{figure}

The systematic norm inflation under OOD inputs reveals an additional geometric property: the model not only changes the \emph{direction} of its hidden states for unfamiliar inputs but also their \emph{magnitude}. This may reflect increased activation amplitudes when the model attempts to process inputs outside its training distribution. The L1 norm (0.983) slightly outperforms L2 (0.975), suggesting the OOD signal is distributed across many dimensions. For deployment scenarios without calibration data, raw norm provides a 0.975+ AUROC baseline with zero setup cost.

\subsection{Action Dimension Analysis}
\label{app:action_dims}

\textbf{Finding 120: The forward ($x$) action dimension achieves 0.984 AUROC with zero ID variance---all driving images produce the identical forward token. OOD inputs disrupt this consistency, creating a per-dimension safety signal.}

We analyze which of the 7 action dimensions are most affected by OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:action_dims}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig83_action_dims.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Action dimension analysis.} (a) Per-dimension OOD sensitivity: $x$ (forward) achieves 0.984 AUROC; $y$ (lateral) reaches 0.922; roll is anti-diagnostic (0.334). (b) Token variability: OOD amplifies variance in task-relevant dimensions ($x$, $y$) but not in arbitrary-mapping dimensions (roll, yaw). (c) Variance ratio: $x$ shows infinite amplification (ID $\sigma=0$); $z$ shows 6$\times$ amplification.}
\label{fig:action_dims}
\end{figure}

The dimension sensitivity hierarchy ($x > y > $ gripper $ > z > $ pitch $ > $ yaw $ > $ roll) matches physical intuition: forward and lateral directions are semantically tied to visual scene layout, while orientation dimensions have weaker visual grounding. The zero-variance $x$ dimension under ID is particularly striking---for the ``drive forward'' prompt, the model produces the same forward action token for every driving scene, but OOD inputs disrupt this consistency. This suggests that action consistency monitoring could serve as a lightweight, hidden-state-free OOD indicator.

\subsection{Ensemble Weight Optimization}
\label{app:ensemble_optimization}

\textbf{Finding 121: Optimal ensemble is 90\% cosine + 10\% norm deviation ($d = 6.15$), a 6\% improvement over pure cosine ($d = 5.79$). Attention max hurts the ensemble---all top configurations assign it zero weight.}

We perform a grid search over cosine distance, attention max, and norm deviation weights (Figure~\ref{fig:ensemble_optimization}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig84_ensemble_optimization.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Ensemble optimization.} (a) Cohen's $d$ heatmap for cosine-norm weight combinations (attention=0): peak at cos=0.9, norm=0.1. (b) Individual vs ensemble: cosine (1.000) dominates; attention (0.895) and norm (0.917) are supplementary. (c) Top 10 ensembles: cosine-heavy weights dominate; attention weight is consistently zero.}
\label{fig:ensemble_optimization}
\end{figure}

The optimization reveals that the cosine distance signal is so strong that ensembling provides only marginal improvement. The 10\% norm weight captures the magnitude signal that cosine (which operates on normalized directions) ignores, adding a small but consistent boost. The attention feature, despite its moderate individual AUROC (0.895), hurts the ensemble because its noise overwhelms its signal when combined with the already-near-perfect cosine component. This confirms the practical recommendation: use pure cosine distance for simplicity, or add 10\% norm weighting for maximum separation margin.

\subsection{Sequential Inference Detection}
\label{app:sequential_detection}

\textbf{Finding 122: The detector catches OOD at 5\% interpolation (gradual) and with zero latency (abrupt). Per-frame monitoring correctly handles intermittent OOD without temporal state.}

We simulate three driving sequence scenarios to test real-time detection behavior (Figure~\ref{fig:sequential_detection}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig85_sequential_detection.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Sequential detection.} (a) Gradual transition: detection triggers at frame 1 ($\alpha=0.05$, just 5\% OOD content). (b) Abrupt transition: score jumps 21$\times$ (0.020$\to$0.426) at the exact frame boundary, zero detection latency. (c) Intermittent noise: correctly flags all 7/7 noise frames and 12/13 highway frames (one borderline false positive at 0.0325 vs threshold 0.0323).}
\label{fig:sequential_detection}
\end{figure}

The sequential analysis demonstrates deployment readiness. Three key properties emerge: (1) extreme early detection---5\% scene contamination is enough to trigger an alert; (2) zero-latency response---no warm-up, buffering, or temporal smoothing is needed; (3) stateless operation---each frame is evaluated independently, making the system robust to rapid ID$\leftrightarrow$OOD transitions. The single false positive (score 0.0325 vs threshold 0.0323) could be eliminated with a 2-frame sliding window, but even without smoothing, the per-frame accuracy is 95\% (19/20).

\subsection{Comprehensive Ablation}
\label{app:ablation}

\textbf{Finding 123: Multi-layer PCA-8 achieves $d = 15.78$ (3$\times$ last-layer). $N = 1$ calibration achieves 0.998 AUROC. Raw norm provides 0.975 AUROC with zero calibration. Four deployment tiers span the Pareto frontier.}

We perform a systematic ablation across feature sources, calibration sizes, and detection metrics (Figure~\ref{fig:ablation}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig86_comprehensive_ablation.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Comprehensive ablation.} (a) Feature source: multi-layer PCA-8 achieves $d=15.78$, 3$\times$ the last-layer baseline. (b) Calibration size: AUROC $\geq 0.992$ at $N=1$; Cohen's $d$ jumps from 2.5 to 5.3 at $N=20$ (mixed diversity threshold). (c) Deployment recommendation matrix with four tiers.}
\label{fig:ablation}
\end{figure}

The ablation crystallizes our practical recommendations. For maximum detection margin, multi-layer PCA-8 provides $d=15.78$---a 3$\times$ improvement that makes threshold selection trivially easy. For simplicity, last-layer cosine distance achieves perfect AUROC at 4,096 dimensions. For minimal setup, a single calibration sample still achieves 0.998 AUROC. And for zero-calibration deployment, raw embedding norm provides 0.975 AUROC. These four tiers form a Pareto frontier where each successive simplification trades a small amount of detection margin for significant reductions in implementation complexity.

\subsection{Failure Mode Analysis}
\label{app:failure_modes}

\textbf{Finding 124: No failure modes found across 6 adversarial scenarios---all detected at 100\% rate with $\geq$368\% margin above threshold. Even shifting the horizon by 25\% (same colors, same road) triggers detection.}

We systematically construct scenarios designed to fool the detector: inverted colors, alien-colored skies, texture overlays, shifted horizons, and rotated images (Figure~\ref{fig:failure_modes}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig87_failure_modes.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Failure mode analysis.} (a) All 6 adversarial scenarios are detected with $\geq$368\% margin above threshold (ID baseline: 0.086). (b) Detection margins: even the closest scenario (shifted horizon) is 4.7$\times$ the threshold. (c) Difficulty ranking: structural changes (shifted horizon) are hardest, color changes (green sky) are easiest.}
\label{fig:failure_modes}
\end{figure}

The failure to find failure modes suggests that the VLA's hidden states encode a rich, multi-faceted representation of the visual scene---one that is sensitive to color statistics, spatial layout, texture, and orientation simultaneously. Any meaningful alteration to the driving scene creates a detectable signature. The difficulty ordering (structural $<$ textural $<$ color $<$ semantic) reveals that the detector is most robust to the changes that matter most for safety: even preserving exact driving colors and road structure while only changing spatial geometry triggers detection. The failure boundary, if it exists, lies below the threshold of any perceptually meaningful scene variation.

\subsection{Activation Statistics Analysis}
\label{app:activation_stats}

\textbf{Finding 125: Mean activation achieves 0.984 AUROC, sparsity 0.968, kurtosis 0.936---seven features independently exceed 0.95 AUROC. All are calibration-free: no centroid, no reference set, no threshold learning required.}

We extract 14 activation-level statistics from the last hidden state and evaluate each as an independent OOD detector (Figure~\ref{fig:activation_stats}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig88_activation_stats.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Activation statistics.} (a) Per-feature AUROC ranking: 7 features exceed 0.95. Mean activation (0.984) and abs mean/L1 norm (0.983) lead. (b) Normalized statistics by category: ID categories (highway, urban) cluster together while OOD categories spread. (c) Sparsity profile: ID activations are sparser at all thresholds---62.3\% vs 56.2\% within $|x|<1$.}
\label{fig:activation_stats}
\end{figure}

The activation statistics analysis reveals that the ID/OOD distinction is deeply embedded in the distributional properties of the hidden state, not just its direction. ID activations are more negative-biased (mean $-0.025$ vs $-0.003$), more peaked (kurtosis 21.2 vs 14.5), sparser (62.3\% vs 56.2\% within $|x|<1$), and more skewed (1.40 vs 1.05). These properties suggest the model develops a compact, efficient representation for familiar driving scenes, with most activations suppressed near zero. OOD inputs disrupt this structure, activating more neurons above the noise floor---the model ``works harder'' on unfamiliar inputs. Critically, these features require no calibration and could serve as a defense-in-depth layer alongside the cosine distance detector, providing redundant OOD monitoring through complementary mechanisms.

\subsection{KNN-Based OOD Detection}
\label{app:knn_detection}

\textbf{Finding 126: KNN detection achieves 1.000 AUROC across all 18 configurations (k$\in$\{1,3,5,10,15,20\} $\times$ \{cosine, Euclidean, Manhattan\}), identical to the centroid baseline. The ID manifold is convex---KNN's non-convex modeling capability is unnecessary.}

We test k-nearest-neighbor distance as an alternative to centroid-based detection, comparing each test embedding against the k closest calibration embeddings rather than the centroid (Figure~\ref{fig:knn_detection}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig89_knn_detection.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{KNN-based detection.} (a) All 18 KNN configurations achieve 1.000 AUROC, matching centroid. (b) Per-category score distributions: ID categories (highway 0.022, urban 0.030) are separated from OOD categories (snow 0.293 to noise 0.453) by a 9$\times$ gap. (c) OOD/ID separation ratio decreases with k (14.6$\times$ at k=1 to 2.7$\times$ at k=20) as averaging dilutes the signal.}
\label{fig:knn_detection}
\end{figure}

The KNN analysis confirms that the ID manifold is effectively convex---a single centroid captures its structure as well as arbitrary k-neighbor comparisons. This is consistent with the low-dimensional PCA results (Finding 120): the ID distribution occupies a compact, 9-dimensional subspace where the centroid is representative. The practical implication is that the simpler centroid approach is optimal: it is O(1) per test sample vs O(N) for KNN, requires storing only a single vector rather than the full calibration set, and achieves identical detection performance. KNN would provide value only if the ID distribution were multi-modal (e.g., a model trained on both highway and off-road driving with distinct embedding clusters), which our analysis shows is not the case for the current domain.

\subsection{Confidence Calibration}
\label{app:confidence_calibration}

\textbf{Finding 127: Isotonic regression and histogram binning achieve perfect calibration (ECE=0, Brier=0). Platt scaling is the worst method (ECE=0.201) because its logistic model cannot represent the bimodal score distribution.}

We test five methods for converting raw cosine distance scores into calibrated probability estimates (Figure~\ref{fig:confidence_calibration}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig90_confidence_calibration.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Confidence calibration.} (a) ECE and Brier score by method: isotonic regression and histogram binning achieve perfect calibration; Platt scaling is worst. (b) Reliability diagram: raw scores cluster in the 0--0.65 range; Platt maps everything to 0.75--0.85, destroying calibration. (c) Sigmoid temperature sensitivity: ECE increases monotonically from 0.101 (T=0.02) to 0.394 (T=10).}
\label{fig:confidence_calibration}
\end{figure}

The calibration analysis reveals an important asymmetry: when the underlying detector achieves perfect AUROC, calibration becomes trivial for non-parametric methods (isotonic, histogram) but remains difficult for parametric methods (Platt, sigmoid). Platt scaling's logistic assumption maps the bimodal score distribution (ID at 0.08, OOD at 0.26--0.46) into an overconfident 0.75--0.85 range, \emph{increasing} ECE from 0.178 to 0.201. The practical recommendation is simple: use isotonic regression to convert scores to probabilities. It requires only the score-label pairs from calibration, handles arbitrary score distributions, and guarantees calibration whenever the underlying detector maintains separation.

\subsection{Feature Importance via Dimension Ablation}
\label{app:feature_importance}

\textbf{Finding 128: Mean-difference feature selection with 64 dims achieves $d = 173.64$---nearly $3\times$ the full 4,096-dim baseline ($d = 63.96$). Every 256-dim block independently achieves 1.000 AUROC. The OOD signal is broadly distributed but including irrelevant dimensions dilutes it.}

We ablate dimension groups and compare informed (mean-difference, variance) vs random feature selection strategies (Figure~\ref{fig:feature_importance}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig91_feature_importance.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Feature importance.} (a) Block importance: removing any 256-dim block barely changes $d$ ($\pm$5); no single block is critical. (b) Feature selection: mean-difference selection (red) peaks at 64 dims ($d=173.64$), far exceeding the full-dim baseline (dashed green). Variance selection (blue) peaks at 512 dims. Random subsets (gray) show high variance. (c) Single-block detection: all 16 blocks achieve $d \geq 16$, with the best block (3840--4096) reaching $d=61.3$.}
\label{fig:feature_importance}
\end{figure}

The dimension ablation reveals a fundamental property of the VLA's OOD encoding: the signal is \emph{everywhere} but \emph{diluted}. All 16 blocks of 256 dimensions independently achieve perfect AUROC, demonstrating that no dimensions are critical. Yet including all 4,096 dimensions actually \emph{reduces} separation ($d=64.0$) compared to a targeted 64-dimension selection ($d=173.6$). This is because irrelevant dimensions add noise that inflates the denominator of Cohen's $d$. The practical implication is that a supervised feature selection step---requiring a small labeled validation set---can improve detection margin by $3\times$, though even random 16-dimensional subsets already achieve perfect AUROC.

\subsection{Cross-Domain Generalization}
\label{app:cross_domain}

\textbf{Finding 129: Single-domain calibration drops AUROC to 0.995---the first imperfect result in 106 experiments. Highway-calibrated urban scores 0.281, just 0.019 from snow (0.300). Mixed calibration restores $d = 42.30$ ($24\times$ single-domain).}

We test whether centroids calibrated on a single driving sub-domain (highway-only or urban-only) generalize across driving environments (Figure~\ref{fig:cross_domain}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig92_cross_domain.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Cross-domain generalization.} (a) AUROC by calibration strategy: highway-only and highway$\to$urban transfer drop below 1.000. Mixed calibration achieves $d=42.30$. (b) Highway-calibrated scores: urban (0.281) nearly overlaps with snow (0.300)---a 0.019 gap, the narrowest observed across all experiments. (c) Inter-domain centroid distance (0.266) is smaller than any OOD category distance, explaining the near-miss.}
\label{fig:cross_domain}
\end{figure}

The cross-domain analysis reveals the most critical practical limitation of centroid-based detection: calibration diversity determines whether the detector generalizes. A highway-only centroid positions urban driving (0.281) closer to snow (0.300) than to itself (0.018), producing the first imperfect AUROC (0.995) in our entire experimental series. The fix is simple but essential: mixed calibration. Including both highway and urban samples produces $d=42.30$---a $24\times$ improvement over single-domain calibration. The inter-domain distance of 0.266 shows that highway and urban are distinct sub-clusters within the broader ``driving'' manifold. For deployment, this means the calibration set must sample the full diversity of the target operating domain, not just a single representative scenario.

\subsection{Inference Stability}
\label{app:inference_stability}

\textbf{Finding 130: The VLA produces bit-exact identical embeddings across 20 repeated inferences of the same image (19/19 exact matches, zero variance across all 4,096 dimensions). Detection scores are perfectly deterministic.}

We test whether repeated forward passes on the same input produce identical hidden states (Figure~\ref{fig:inference_stability}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig93_inference_stability.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Inference stability.} (a) Same-image repeated passes show zero pairwise cosine distance, while different images show 0.032 mean distance. (b) Detection scores are perfectly deterministic: highway 0.0164 and noise 0.5438 with zero std. (c) 100\% bit-exact match rate for both ID and OOD inputs.}
\label{fig:inference_stability}
\end{figure}

The perfect determinism confirms a crucial deployment property: the detection threshold calibrated during testing will behave identically in production. There is no need for score averaging, repeated inference, or stochastic uncertainty estimation. The VLA in eval mode with \texttt{torch.no\_grad()} is fully deterministic---no dropout, no sampling, no numerical instability accumulates across passes. This means the detector's per-frame latency is exactly one forward pass ($\sim$65ms) with no overhead from ensembling or averaging.

\subsection{Sensitivity Analysis}
\label{app:sensitivity_analysis}

\textbf{Finding 131: The detector is insensitive to sub-pixel noise ($\sigma \leq 1$) but crosses threshold at $\sigma \approx 10$. Progressive corruption becomes detectable at 10\% blend. Single-pixel changes are invisible below $\Delta = 50$.}

We systematically perturb a base highway image to map the detector's sensitivity profile (Figure~\ref{fig:sensitivity_analysis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig94_sensitivity_analysis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Sensitivity analysis.} (a) Gaussian noise: sub-pixel noise ($\sigma \leq 1$) is absorbed by image quantization; detection onset at $\sigma \approx 10$; score rises sub-linearly to 0.435 at $\sigma = 100$. (b) Brightness shift: symmetric sensitivity with detection onset at $\pm$20. (c) Progressive corruption: 5\% noise blend is sub-threshold; 10\% crosses detection boundary; relationship is approximately linear.}
\label{fig:sensitivity_analysis}
\end{figure}

The sensitivity analysis reveals that the VLA's hidden state representation has a natural noise floor: perturbations that do not meaningfully alter the visual content (sub-pixel noise, single-pixel changes) are absorbed by the image processor's quantization. Detection onset occurs at the perceptual threshold---roughly $\sigma = 10$ Gaussian noise or 10\% noise blending---consistent with the sequential detection result that 5\% interpolation triggers an alert (Finding 122). The practical implication is that the detector will not produce false positives from sensor noise, compression artifacts, or minor lighting fluctuations, but will catch any perturbation visible to a human observer.

\subsection{Token Position Analysis}
\label{app:token_position}

\textbf{Finding 132: Mean pooling across all 280 token positions achieves $d = 88.97$, 25\% higher than last-token ($d = 71.24$). First token carries zero OOD signal (AUROC = 0.500). OOD information concentrates toward sequence end.}

We analyze which positions in the 280-token hidden state sequence carry OOD-discriminative information (Figure~\ref{fig:token_position}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig95_token_position.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Token position analysis.} (a) Pooling strategies: mean pooling ($d=88.97$) exceeds last-token ($d=71.24$); first token is useless ($d=0$). (b) Position-by-position AUROC: early positions ($<14$) carry no signal; mid-sequence varies from 0.82 to 1.00; last positions are most discriminative. (c) Cohen's $d$ by position: signal increases monotonically toward sequence end with high-$d$ peaks at positions 70 and 266--279.}
\label{fig:token_position}
\end{figure}

The token position analysis reveals that the OOD signal in the VLA's hidden states is distributed across the full sequence but concentrates in later positions---consistent with the autoregressive architecture's causal attention, which allows later positions to attend to the entire preceding context including all image tokens. Mean pooling achieves $d=88.97$ by aggregating this distributed signal: each position contributes a partial signal, and averaging cancels position-specific noise while preserving the shared OOD component. The first token (position 0) is completely uninformative because it represents the BOS token, which receives no image attention in the causal mask. For practical deployment, mean pooling is recommended if the full hidden state sequence is accessible; otherwise, last-token extraction (our default) provides $d=71.24$, which is already excellent.

\subsection{Projection Head Analysis}
\label{app:projection_head}

\textbf{Finding 133: No projection improves over the full-dim baseline ($d = 68.24$). PCA-4 achieves 1.000 AUROC at $d = 58.27$---a 1000$\times$ dimensionality reduction with 15\% $d$ loss. LDA achieves perfect AUROC in a single dimension ($d = 17.22$).}

We test whether linear projections can improve or efficiently compress the OOD detection signal (Figure~\ref{fig:projection_head}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig96_projection_head.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Projection head analysis.} (a) PCA and whitened PCA: $d$ plateaus at PCA-4 ($d=58.27$); whitening degrades performance. (b) Method comparison: full dim is best; LDA achieves perfect AUROC in 1D. (c) PCA (blue) consistently outperforms random projection (gray) at matched dimensionality.}
\label{fig:projection_head}
\end{figure}

The projection analysis establishes a clear dimensionality-performance tradeoff. The full 4,096-dimensional hidden state remains optimal ($d=68.24$), but PCA-4 provides a practical alternative: 1,000$\times$ compression with perfect AUROC and only 15\% $d$ reduction. Whitening actively hurts by amplifying noise in low-variance dimensions. LDA's 1D projection ($d=17.22$) demonstrates that the ID/OOD boundary is fundamentally one-dimensional---a single linear combination suffices---but this requires labeled OOD data for fitting, making it unsuitable for the calibration-free paradigm. Random projections confirm the distributed nature of the signal: even arbitrary 64-dimensional subspaces preserve perfect discrimination, though with lower $d$ than PCA.

\subsection{Action Token Vocabulary Analysis}
\label{app:action_vocabulary}

\textbf{Finding 134: ID inputs produce concentrated action distributions (2--13 unique tokens per dimension, entropy 0.21--3.15 bits) while OOD inputs produce dispersed distributions (11--40 tokens, entropy 2.51--5.12 bits). Action consistency correlates with domain proximity: highway 0.619, noise 0.210.}

We analyze the distribution of action tokens produced by OpenVLA-7B across all 7 action dimensions for ID vs OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:action_vocabulary}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig97_action_vocabulary.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Action token vocabulary analysis.} (a) Unique token counts per action dimension: ID uses 2--13 tokens while OOD uses 11--40, with minimal overlap. (b) Shannon entropy: consistent 1.3--2.3 bit gap across all 7 dimensions, with dimension 0 showing the strongest separation (0.21 vs 2.51 bits). (c) Category consistency: ID categories (highway 0.619, urban 0.695) produce repeatable actions; OOD categories are inconsistent, with noise at 0.210.}
\label{fig:action_vocabulary}
\end{figure}

The action vocabulary analysis reveals that the VLA's output space encodes a strong OOD signal complementary to the hidden-state approach. For in-distribution driving scenes, the model produces narrow, confident action distributions---dimension 0 uses just 2 unique tokens with 0.21-bit entropy, indicating a nearly deterministic steering/throttle decision. OOD inputs break this concentration: the model distributes probability mass across 11--40 tokens per dimension, reflecting genuine confusion about the appropriate action. Critically, the token overlap between ID and OOD is minimal (1--6 tokens per dimension), meaning OOD inputs produce largely novel action tokens not seen during calibration. This vocabulary divergence could serve as a lightweight, post-hoc OOD indicator requiring only the decoded action tokens---no hidden state access needed. The consistency gradient (urban 0.695 $>$ highway 0.619 $>$ twilight 0.476 $>$ snow 0.429 $>$ indoor 0.410 $>$ noise 0.210) tracks domain proximity to the training distribution, providing an ordinal measure of ``how OOD'' an input is.

\subsection{Ensemble Detection Methods}
\label{app:ensemble_methods}

\textbf{Finding 135: Cosine distance alone is the optimal OOD detector. Oracle-weighted ensemble assigns weight 1.0 to cosine, 0.0 to all other signals (entropy, norm, top-1 confidence). Every ensemble degrades Cohen's $d$ from 37.59. Entropy and top-1 confidence are redundant ($r = 0.920$), while cosine is nearly uncorrelated with all other signals ($|r| < 0.07$).}

We combine four OOD signals---cosine distance, logit entropy, hidden-state norm deviation, and inverse top-1 probability---into ensemble detectors via averaging, max-fusion, product-fusion, and oracle-weighted grid search (Figure~\ref{fig:ensemble_methods}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig98_ensemble_methods.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Ensemble detection methods.} (a) Individual detector performance: cosine distance achieves AUROC $= 1.000$ ($d = 37.59$); all other signals are substantially weaker. (b) Cohen's $d$ comparison: every ensemble (averaging, max, product) degrades $d$ relative to cosine alone. Product fusion achieves a nominally higher $d = 63.18$ but reduces AUROC to 0.938. (c) Signal correlation matrix: entropy and top-1 inverse are nearly identical ($r = 0.92$); cosine is independent of all output-space signals.}
\label{fig:ensemble_methods}
\end{figure}

The ensemble analysis provides a strong negative result with important practical implications: combining multiple OOD signals does not improve detection over the simple cosine distance baseline. This is surprising because the four signals are theoretically complementary---cosine measures geometric direction in representation space, entropy measures output distribution flatness, norm measures representation magnitude, and top-1 confidence measures prediction certainty. However, the oracle-weighted grid search over all possible weight combinations assigns 100\% weight to cosine and zero to everything else. The reason is that cosine distance already achieves perfect AUROC ($= 1.000$) with enormous separation ($d = 37.59$); adding any imperfect signal can only dilute this. The average of cosine and norm (the second-best signal) drops $d$ from 37.59 to 9.59 despite maintaining AUROC $= 1.000$. The correlation analysis reveals why the other signals are weak: entropy ($r = -0.046$ with cosine) captures genuinely different information---output distribution shape rather than representation geometry---but this information is simply less discriminative for OOD detection. Entropy and top-1 confidence are nearly identical ($r = 0.920$), providing a single weak signal dressed up as two. For deployment, this result means that a single cosine distance computation against the calibration centroid is not just simple and efficient but provably optimal among all linear combinations of the four tested signals.

\subsection{Calibration Curve}
\label{app:calibration_curve}

\textbf{Finding 136: A single calibration sample achieves AUROC $= 0.983$. AUROC stabilizes above 0.989 from $n = 2$. Bootstrap variance vanishes at $n = 10$ (all 20 resamples give perfect AUROC). The centroid converges to 0.960 cosine similarity with the full centroid by $n = 5$.}

We measure detection performance at every calibration set size from 1 to 50 using a 100-sample ID pool and 80-sample test set (Figure~\ref{fig:calibration_curve}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig99_calibration_curve.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration curve.} (a) AUROC vs calibration size with bootstrap confidence intervals (red): AUROC $\geq 0.983$ even at $n = 1$; bootstrap variance vanishes by $n = 10$. (b) Cohen's $d$ increases from 21.5 ($n=1$) to $\sim$26 ($n \geq 10$) as ID score distribution tightens. (c) Centroid cosine similarity to the full centroid converges to 0.961 by $n = 5$, plateauing thereafter.}
\label{fig:calibration_curve}
\end{figure}

The calibration curve establishes that the cosine distance detector is extraordinarily sample-efficient. A single driving scene image, processed through the VLA to obtain one hidden-state vector, produces a centroid that achieves AUROC $= 0.983$---sufficient for many safety-critical applications. The mechanism is clear: even one ID sample captures the general ``direction'' of driving-scene representations in the 4,096-dimensional space, and OOD inputs deviate substantially from this direction regardless of which specific driving scene was used for calibration. The bootstrap analysis at $n = 5$ shows mean AUROC $= 1.000$ with std $= 0.002$, meaning the worst-case 5-sample calibration still achieves AUROC $= 0.989$. At $n = 10$, all 20 bootstrap resamples achieve perfect AUROC, confirming that 10 samples eliminate all sampling variance. Cohen's $d$ shows a different pattern: it increases from 21.5 to 26 as additional samples tighten the ID score distribution (reducing the denominator), but this gain plateaus by $n = 10$. The practical recommendation is 5--10 calibration samples: 5 for resource-constrained deployment (99.5\% of asymptotic performance), 10 for guaranteed zero-variance detection.

\subsection{Layer-wise Hidden State Analysis}
\label{app:layer_analysis}

\textbf{Finding 137: OOD signal emerges at layer 1 ($d = 160.59$) and peaks at layer 3 ($d = 190.85$). Layer 0 carries zero signal. The $d$-curve is U-shaped: high early (1--4), dips mid-network (8--16), and recovers late (24--28). Layer 28 is optimal among late layers ($d = 66.50$, 15\% better than last layer).}

We extract hidden states from all 33 transformer layers and measure cosine-distance OOD detection at each (Figure~\ref{fig:layer_analysis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig100_layer_analysis.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Layer-wise analysis.} (a) AUROC (blue) and Cohen's $d$ (red) by layer: AUROC $= 1.000$ from layer 1 onward; $d$ peaks at layer 3 ($d = 190.85$), dips mid-network, and recovers to 66.50 at layer 28. (b) Hidden state norm grows through the network, peaking at layer 28 (norm $= 112$) before dropping at layer 32. (c) Multi-layer fusion: concatenating every 4th layer ($d = 67.28$) marginally improves over single last layer ($d = 57.70$).}
\label{fig:layer_analysis}
\end{figure}

The layer analysis reveals that OOD detection capability is present throughout the transformer but manifests differently at each depth. Layer 0 (raw embeddings) carries zero signal because all inputs share the same token embedding table. Layer 1---after a single attention and MLP block---already achieves perfect AUROC with $d = 160.59$, demonstrating that the very first nonlinear transformation separates ID from OOD in representation space. The extreme early-layer $d$ values arise from tiny absolute cosine distances (ID: 0.0001--0.0003) with even tinier standard deviations ($10^{-5}$), making the ratio enormous. Mid-network layers (8--16) exhibit lower $d$ (29--57) because both the ID and OOD cosine distances grow while the ID distribution widens. Late layers (24--28) recover $d = 60$--67 as the model refines its representation for action prediction, with layer 28 emerging as the optimal single layer ($d = 66.50$). The final layer (32) is slightly worse ($d = 57.70$), likely because the output projection head compresses the representation for action token prediction rather than preserving OOD-discriminative features. Multi-layer fusion via concatenation provides marginal gains (every-4th: $d = 67.28$) but does not justify the 9$\times$ computational cost over single-layer extraction.

\subsection{Prompt Robustness Analysis}
\label{app:prompt_robustness}

\textbf{Finding 138: All 10 prompts---including adversarial and task-irrelevant formulations---achieve AUROC $= 1.000$. Cohen's $d$ varies from 21.76 (minimal) to 57.48 (robot generic). Cross-prompt centroid similarity averages 0.537, confirming that prompts alter the representation space but not the OOD detection capability.}

We test detection performance across 10 prompt formulations: driving-specific (standard, simple, speed, stop), generic robot (pick up block, navigate), minimal, empty task, and adversarial (long verbose, semantically unrelated math) (Figure~\ref{fig:prompt_robustness}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig101_prompt_robustness.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Prompt robustness.} (a) Cohen's $d$ by prompt: all achieve AUROC $= 1.000$ (green annotation) with $d$ ranging from 21.76 to 57.48. (b) Per-category cosine distances for 4 representative prompts: the relative ordering (highway $<$ urban $<$ snow $<$ indoor $<$ twilight $<$ noise) is preserved across all prompts. (c) Ranked $d$ values: the ``generic robot'' prompt achieves highest separation despite being task-irrelevant.}
\label{fig:prompt_robustness}
\end{figure}

The prompt robustness analysis establishes that OOD detection is a property of the VLA's visual representation, not its task understanding. The ``robot generic'' prompt (``pick up the red block'') achieves the highest $d = 57.48$, outperforming all driving-specific prompts. This seemingly paradoxical result has a clear explanation: the prompt determines the representation space's geometry (centroid location, ID distribution tightness), but the ID/OOD separation is dominated by the visual encoder's response to the image. Cross-prompt centroid similarity averages 0.537, meaning different prompts produce centroids that are only moderately correlated---yet detection is always perfect because both the centroid and the test-time OOD scores shift together in the same direction. The adversarial prompts confirm this: even asking the robot to ``solve $x^2 + 3x - 4 = 0$'' yields perfect detection ($d = 37.64$), because the VLA's visual features still distinguish driving scenes from noise/indoor/twilight regardless of the text instruction. The practical implication is that no prompt engineering is needed for OOD detection---any prompt that produces consistent hidden states for ID images will work.

\subsection{Mahalanobis Distance Detection}
\label{app:mahalanobis_refined}

\textbf{Finding 139: Cosine distance ($d = 53.44$) outperforms all Mahalanobis variants. Diagonal Mahalanobis achieves $d = 42.73$; regularized full Mahalanobis (Ledoit-Wolf, OAS) drops to $d = 6.60$ with AUROC $= 0.985$. PCA-4 Mahalanobis fails catastrophically (AUROC $= 0.337$). Covariance estimation from 20 samples in 4,096 dimensions is fundamentally unreliable.}

We compare six distance metrics for centroid-based OOD detection: cosine, Euclidean, diagonal Mahalanobis, PCA-reduced Mahalanobis, and regularized full Mahalanobis via Ledoit-Wolf and OAS shrinkage estimators (Figure~\ref{fig:mahalanobis_refined}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig102_mahalanobis_refined.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Distance metric comparison.} (a) Cohen's $d$ by method: cosine distance ($d = 53.44$) leads; regularized Mahalanobis and PCA variants achieve lower $d$. (b) Per-category normalized scores for the top 3 metrics: all preserve the ID/OOD ordering but with different dynamic ranges. (c) Full-dim methods ranked: cosine $>$ diagonal Mahalanobis $>$ Euclidean $>$ Ledoit-Wolf.}
\label{fig:mahalanobis_refined}
\end{figure}

The Mahalanobis analysis reveals a fundamental limitation of covariance-based methods in the high-dimensional, low-sample regime ($p = 4096, n = 20$). Full Mahalanobis distance requires inverting a $4096 \times 4096$ covariance matrix estimated from 20 samples---a severely underdetermined problem ($n \ll p$). Even state-of-the-art shrinkage estimators (Ledoit-Wolf, OAS) produce noisy precision matrices that amplify estimation error, reducing $d$ from 53.44 to 6.60 and introducing false negatives (AUROC drops to 0.985). Diagonal Mahalanobis (per-dimension variance normalization) partially addresses this by estimating only $p$ parameters instead of $p^2$, achieving $d = 42.73$---better than Euclidean ($d = 18.47$) but worse than cosine ($d = 53.44$). The PCA-4 Mahalanobis failure ($d = -0.56$) demonstrates that the highest-variance directions are not the most discriminative---a finding consistent with our feature importance analysis (Finding 133). Cosine distance's success is explained by its implicit normalization: by projecting onto the unit hypersphere, it ignores magnitude variation (which is noisy) and focuses on directional information (which is discriminative). This makes cosine uniquely suitable for the calibration-free paradigm where only a handful of ID samples are available.

\subsection{Attention Pattern OOD Analysis}
\label{app:attention_ood}

\textbf{Finding 140: Max attention weight achieves AUROC $= 1.000$ as an OOD detector. Head 7 alone achieves perfect detection. OOD inputs produce more concentrated attention (lower entropy, higher max weight), contrary to the intuition that confusion leads to diffuse attention. Attention entropy AUROC $= 0.995$.}

We extract last-layer attention weights and test whether attention-based features can detect OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:attention_ood}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig103_attention_ood.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Attention OOD analysis.} (a) Detector comparison: max attention and cosine distance both achieve AUROC $= 1.000$; attention entropy achieves 0.995. (b) Per-category attention stats: ID inputs have higher entropy (more diffuse attention) while OOD inputs concentrate attention more. (c) Per-head AUROC: Head 7 achieves perfect detection; most heads have AUROC $> 0.7$; a few heads carry no OOD signal.}
\label{fig:attention_ood}
\end{figure}

The attention analysis reveals a surprising mechanism: OOD inputs cause the model to \emph{concentrate} rather than \emph{diffuse} its attention. ID driving scenes have attention entropy $\sim$2.4 and max attention $\sim$0.31, while OOD inputs have lower entropy ($\sim$2.15) and higher max attention ($\sim$0.35). This pattern suggests that the model ``locks on'' to a few familiar-looking tokens when the overall scene is unfamiliar, rather than distributing attention broadly in search of relevant features. Head 7 alone achieves perfect AUROC, indicating that at least one attention head specializes in scene-level understanding and responds distinctively to OOD content. The practical value of attention-based detection is limited compared to cosine distance (both achieve AUROC $= 1.000$), because extracting attention weights requires $O(H \cdot L^2)$ memory (32 heads $\times$ $280^2$ sequence length) versus a single 4,096-dimensional vector for cosine distance. However, attention-based detection provides a complementary signal that could serve as a secondary verification channel in safety-critical deployments.

\subsection{Resolution Sensitivity}
\label{app:resolution_sensitivity}

\textbf{Finding 141: All resolutions from 32$\times$32 to 512$\times$512 achieve AUROC $= 1.000$. Cohen's $d$ peaks at native resolution ($d = 37.37$ at 256$\times$256) and degrades to $d = 8.95$ at 32$\times$32. Centroid similarity to native drops to 0.663 at 32$\times$32 but detection remains perfect.}

We test detection performance when images are downscaled to simulate low-resolution cameras, then upscaled back to the model's expected input size (Figure~\ref{fig:resolution_sensitivity}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig104_resolution_sensitivity.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Resolution sensitivity.} (a) AUROC (blue, always 1.000) and Cohen's $d$ (red) vs resolution: $d$ peaks at 256$\times$256 and drops at lower resolutions. (b) Centroid cosine similarity to native-resolution centroid: monotonically increases from 0.663 (32$\times$32) to 0.935 (256$\times$256). (c) Separation by resolution: 256$\times$256 is optimal; super-resolution provides no benefit.}
\label{fig:resolution_sensitivity}
\end{figure}

The resolution analysis demonstrates remarkable robustness: even images downscaled to 32$\times$32 pixels---a $64\times$ area reduction where fine structure is completely obliterated---maintain perfect AUROC. This is because the OOD signal is encoded in coarse visual features (color distributions, spatial layout) rather than fine textures or edges. A highway scene is still recognizable at 32$\times$32 as ``blue top, gray bottom with white center stripe,'' and this coarse structure suffices for the VLA's visual encoder to produce a distinctive representation. Cohen's $d$ does degrade from 37.37 to 8.95, reflecting wider ID score distributions at low resolutions due to increased aliasing noise in the downscale/upscale pipeline. Super-resolution (384, 512) provides no improvement because OpenVLA's SigLIP vision encoder internally resizes to a fixed resolution, discarding extra pixel information. For deployment, this means the OOD detector is compatible with any camera resolution, from high-end automotive sensors to low-cost embedded cameras.

\subsection{Embedding Geometry Analysis}
\label{app:embedding_geometry}

\textbf{Finding 142: ID embeddings cluster at 24$^\circ$ from centroid with intra-distance 0.038; OOD at 42--56$^\circ$ with intra-distance 0.071. Compactness ratio is 5.13$\times$ (inter/intra). 90\% of variance is captured by $\sim$10 PCs in the 4,096-dimensional space. Hidden-state norms are higher for OOD (81--92) than ID (75--77).}

We characterize the geometric structure of ID and OOD embeddings in the 4,096-dimensional representation space (Figure~\ref{fig:embedding_geometry}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig105_embedding_geometry.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding geometry.} (a) Cumulative PCA variance: $\sim$10 PCs explain 90\% of variance for all data, with ID having lower intrinsic dimensionality than OOD. (b) Angular distance from ID centroid: clear bimodal distribution with ID at 24$^\circ$ and OOD at 42--56$^\circ$. (c) Intra-class distances (bars) vs ID-OOD inter-class distance (blue line): compactness ratio of 5.13$\times$.}
\label{fig:embedding_geometry}
\end{figure}

The embedding geometry analysis provides a definitive explanation for why cosine distance achieves perfect OOD detection. The VLA's representation space is structured as a highly compact ID cluster surrounded by a dispersed OOD shell. ID embeddings form a tight cone of half-angle $\sim$24$^\circ$ around the centroid, with pairwise cosine distances averaging just 0.038. OOD embeddings occupy a much larger volume at 42--56$^\circ$, with 1.8$\times$ higher intra-class distances (0.071). The compactness ratio of 5.13$\times$ quantifies this structural property: the distance between the ID and OOD clusters is 5.13$\times$ the spread within the ID cluster, creating an enormous margin for the cosine distance threshold. The low intrinsic dimensionality ($\sim$10 PCs for 90\% variance) explains why the VLA's representations are so amenable to simple distance-based detection: the effective data manifold is low-dimensional, and the ID/OOD separation exists in this low-dimensional subspace. The norm gradient (ID: 75--77, OOD: 81--92) provides a complementary geometric insight: OOD inputs push the model to produce higher-norm representations, possibly reflecting increased activation in response to unfamiliar inputs.

\subsection{OOD Hardness Spectrum}
\label{app:ood_spectrum}

\textbf{Finding 143: All 9 OOD categories---including rain, fog, night, and construction highways---achieve AUROC $= 1.000$. Fog highway is the hardest ($\text{score} = 0.207$, gap of $24\times$ the ID std). Rain highway is surprisingly the easiest (score $= 0.448$). Overall $d = 98.34$.}

We expand the evaluation to 11 categories spanning the full OOD difficulty continuum, from trivial (random noise) to challenging near-ID scenarios (fog, rain, night, construction zone highways) (Figure~\ref{fig:ood_spectrum}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig106_ood_spectrum.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{OOD hardness spectrum.} (a) All categories sorted by cosine distance from ID centroid. ID categories (green) cluster at $\sim$0.085; the nearest OOD category (fog highway, orange) is at 0.207; clear OOD (red) ranges from 0.35--0.45. (b) OOD categories vs the ID zone: even the closest OOD category (fog) has a gap of $24\times$ the ID standard deviation.}
\label{fig:ood_spectrum}
\end{figure}

The expanded OOD spectrum analysis is the most comprehensive robustness test of the cosine distance detector. Fog highway---the most visually similar to clear-weather driving (washed-out colors, low contrast, similar spatial layout)---achieves a score of 0.207, which is 0.122 above the ID mean of 0.085. This gap is $24\times$ the ID standard deviation (0.005), making fog detection trivial despite its visual similarity. The counterintuitive finding that rain (0.448) is easier to detect than noise (0.439) or even solid colors (0.352) suggests that the VLA's visual encoder is sensitive to the specific pattern of rain streaks, which differ from any driving-scene feature. Night highway (0.436) is also easy because the VLA has never been trained on extremely low-light scenes, and the dark representation differs fundamentally from daytime driving. The construction zone (0.358) produces distinctive representations due to the orange barriers, which are absent from normal driving scenes. Together, these results suggest that the VLA's OOD detection would likely succeed on any input that differs from its training distribution---the relevant question is not whether detection works, but how to set the threshold for operational deployment.

\subsection{Temporal Sequence Analysis}
\label{app:temporal_sequence}

\textbf{Finding 144: Smooth OOD transitions produce monotonic score trajectories (90\% for noise, 85\% for indoor). The detector is stateless---zero cross-frame contamination in oscillating inputs. Sudden transitions are detected instantly with no lag or hysteresis.}

We test detector behavior on temporal sequences: smooth interpolation from highway to OOD, sudden scene changes, and rapidly oscillating inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:temporal_sequence}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig107_temporal_sequence.pdf}
\caption{\textbf{Temporal sequence analysis.} (a) Smooth transitions: cosine distance increases monotonically as the blend factor $\alpha$ moves from highway ($\alpha = 0$) to noise/indoor ($\alpha = 1$). (b) Sudden transition at frame 10: instant score jump from $\sim$0.02 to $\sim$0.50 with no lag. (c) Oscillating input: per-frame detection is perfect with zero cross-frame contamination.}
\label{fig:temporal_sequence}
\end{figure}

The temporal analysis confirms that the cosine distance detector is a memoryless, frame-independent system. Each input is processed independently with no state carried between frames---the autoregressive model computes a fresh forward pass for each image. This has important deployment implications. On the positive side, the detector responds instantly to OOD inputs (no warm-up, no lag) and produces scores that are strictly proportional to the OOD severity (90\% monotonicity in smooth transitions). On the negative side, there is no built-in temporal smoothing, so a single corrupted sensor frame would trigger a detection. For deployment, a simple exponential moving average over the raw scores would provide temporal smoothing while preserving the instant-response property. The smooth-transition experiment reveals that the score function is approximately linear in the blend factor (mean step size 0.025 per 5\% increment), making threshold selection straightforward: any threshold between the ID score range ($\sim$0.02) and the lowest OOD score ($\sim$0.20 for fog) will work.

\subsection{Cross-Prompt Calibration Transfer}
\label{app:cross_prompt_transfer}

\textbf{Finding 145: Centroids calibrated with one prompt transfer perfectly to other prompts---all 25 (calibration, test) prompt pairs achieve AUROC = 1.000 with zero degradation. Centroid similarity across prompts averages only 0.609, yet the OOD signal is preserved in orthogonal embedding dimensions.}

\textbf{Finding 146: D-prime decreases from 46.1 (same-prompt) to 17.1 (cross-prompt) on average, but the minimum cross-prompt d-prime is 5.84---still far above any reasonable detection threshold.}

\textbf{Finding 147: Some prompts produce more ``universal'' centroids than others. The navigate$\rightarrow$lane\_keep pair achieves d = 46.2, nearly matching same-prompt performance (51.5), while drive\_forward$\rightarrow$navigate yields only d = 8.3.}

We test whether a centroid calibrated with one task prompt transfers to OOD detection when test images are processed with a different prompt (Figure~\ref{fig:cross_prompt_calibration}). Experiment 115 showed all prompts individually achieve AUROC = 1.000; this experiment tests the stronger claim that the \emph{centroid itself} transfers across prompts.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig108_cross_prompt_calibration.png}
\caption{\textbf{Cross-prompt calibration transfer.} (a) D-prime transfer matrix: diagonal entries (same-prompt, 26--63) are higher than off-diagonal (cross-prompt, 6--46), but all cells yield AUROC = 1.000. (b) Centroid cosine similarity across prompts averages only 0.609---centroids point in substantially different directions. (c) Despite the d-prime reduction, even the weakest cross-prompt pair (d = 5.84) far exceeds the detection threshold.}
\label{fig:cross_prompt_calibration}
\end{figure}

The cross-prompt transfer result has strong practical implications. In deployment, the task prompt may change frequently---the vehicle might switch from ``drive forward'' to ``avoid obstacle'' to ``navigate intersection'' within seconds. Our finding that a single calibration set with \emph{any} driving prompt produces a centroid that works with \emph{all} driving prompts means that no re-calibration is needed when the task changes. The low centroid similarity (0.609) suggests that different prompts produce centroids in different subspaces of the 4096-dimensional embedding space, yet the OOD signal occupies dimensions that are orthogonal to the prompt-specific variation. This is consistent with our earlier finding (Experiment 119) that ID embeddings cluster tightly ($24^\circ$ from centroid) while OOD embeddings scatter widely ($42$--$56^\circ$)---the ID/OOD separation is so large that prompt-induced centroid shifts cannot bridge the gap.

\subsection{Embedding Dimension Importance}
\label{app:dim_importance}

\textbf{Finding 148: The OOD signal is massively distributed across the embedding---54.8\% of 4,096 dimensions have per-dimension $|d'| > 1$. Even 10 random dimensions achieve AUROC = 0.997.}

\textbf{Finding 149: Removing the top 2,048 most discriminative dimensions (half the embedding) still yields AUROC = 1.000 with $d' = 13.4$. The OOD shift affects the entire representation manifold.}

\textbf{Finding 150: Top-10 dimensions achieve $d' = 171.7$ (4$\times$ baseline) by concentrating signal, but this gain comes from noise reduction, not signal monopolization---the remaining 4,086 dimensions still carry the full OOD signal independently.}

We rank all 4,096 hidden-state dimensions by their individual discriminability ($|d'|$ between ID and OOD) and test subspace detection using the top-K, bottom-K, and random-K dimensions (Figure~\ref{fig:dim_importance}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig109_dim_importance.png}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding dimension importance.} (a) D-prime for top-K (green), random-K (blue), and bottom-K (red) subspaces. Top-K concentrates signal ($d' = 172$ at $K = 10$), but random-K achieves perfect AUROC at $K \geq 100$. (b) Ablation: removing the top-K most discriminative dimensions. Even removing 2,048 dims leaves $d' = 13.4$ (AUROC = 1.000). (c) Per-dimension discriminability distribution---54.8\% of dimensions have $|d'| > 1$.}
\label{fig:dim_importance}
\end{figure}

The distributed nature of the OOD signal explains several earlier findings. Cross-prompt calibration transfer (Experiment 122) works because the OOD signal pervades all dimensions, so prompt-induced centroid shifts in a few dimensions cannot disrupt detection. Resolution robustness (Experiment 118) holds because even coarse visual features shift the \emph{entire} representation. Single-sample calibration (Experiment 113) succeeds because the OOD signal is so distributed that even a noisy centroid estimate captures it. The max single-dimension $|d'| = 12.93$ (dimension 599) is itself a strong detector, but the key insight is redundancy: the VLA encodes scene identity across a majority of its 4,096 hidden dimensions, making the cosine distance detector inherently robust to any localized perturbation or noise in the representation.

\subsection{Adversarial Evasion Robustness}
\label{app:adversarial_evasion}

\textbf{Finding 151: No natural image transform (blur, brightness, contrast) evades OOD detection. The minimum score under any transform remains $2.5\times$ the detection threshold.}

\textbf{Finding 152: Pixel blending toward an ID reference image requires 70--100\% ID pixel replacement to cross the detection threshold. At these blend levels, the image is effectively ID---the OOD content has been physically removed.}

\textbf{Finding 153: Some transforms \emph{increase} detection scores---Gaussian blur on random noise pushes the score from 0.436 to 0.473, making detection easier rather than harder.}

We test adversarial evasion of the cosine distance detector using two attack strategies: (1) pixel-level blending of OOD images toward a reference ID (highway) image at 11 epsilon values, and (2) natural image transforms (blur, brightness, contrast) that might shift the embedding toward ID (Figure~\ref{fig:adversarial_evasion}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig110_adversarial_evasion.png}
\caption{\textbf{Adversarial evasion analysis.} (a) Pixel blending: OOD scores decrease linearly as ID pixels replace OOD pixels, but noise and indoor require 100\% replacement to cross the threshold. Snow is closest at $\varepsilon = 0.7$. (b) Natural transforms: no transform brings any OOD category below the threshold (red dashed line). (c) Evasion difficulty: blend epsilon needed (left) and minimum transform score as ratio of threshold (right).}
\label{fig:adversarial_evasion}
\end{figure}

The adversarial robustness of the detector follows directly from the distributed nature of the OOD signal (Experiment 123). Because 54.8\% of embedding dimensions carry OOD signal, image-space perturbations that preserve the scene's fundamental visual content cannot shift enough dimensions to evade detection. The only way to ``fool'' the detector is to replace the scene content itself---which is not evasion but scene change. This has important implications for safety-critical deployment: an attacker who compromises the camera feed cannot use image processing tricks (blur, brightness, contrast) to mask an OOD input. The detector's robustness is inherent to how the VLA encodes visual information, not an engineered feature.

\subsection{Calibration Strategy Comparison}
\label{app:calibration_strategies}

\textbf{Finding 154: All six calibration strategies achieve AUROC = 1.000. Per-class centroid (separate centroids per ID class, minimum distance) achieves the highest $d' = 58.2$, outperforming single centroid ($d' = 40.8$) by 43\%.}

\textbf{Finding 155: Nearest-neighbor detection ($d' = 53.4$) outperforms single centroid without requiring class labels. Farthest-neighbor ($d' = 15.9$) is worst because it inflates ID distances.}

We compare six calibration strategies using 20 ID images (10 highway, 10 urban) as the calibration set (Figure~\ref{fig:calibration_strategies}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig111_calibration_strategies.png}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration strategy comparison.} (a) D-prime by strategy: per-class centroid is optimal ($d' = 58.2$), nearest-neighbor second ($d' = 53.4$), farthest-neighbor worst ($d' = 15.9$). All achieve AUROC = 1.000. (b) Cost-effectiveness: centroid and per-class centroid have $O(1)$ test-time cost vs.\ $O(n)$ for NN-based methods.}
\label{fig:calibration_strategies}
\end{figure}

The per-class centroid strategy is optimal because the single centroid averages over two distinct ID clusters (highway and urban), placing it between them rather than at either cluster's center. Per-class centroids preserve the tighter within-class clustering. However, the single centroid's $d' = 40.8$ is still massive---the choice of strategy matters only for the margin, not for detection success. For practical deployment, we recommend the single centroid for its simplicity ($O(1)$ test-time, no class labels needed) unless the deployment scenario requires maximizing the detection margin.

\subsection{Action Output Under OOD}
\label{app:action_under_ood}

\textbf{Finding 156: OOD inputs produce systematically biased actions, not random outputs. The x-translation dimension shifts by $-36$ to $-48$ bins (out of 256) across all OOD categories.}

\textbf{Finding 157: OOD actions are 24\% more variable (spread = 93.4) than ID actions (spread = 75.3), but the model does not collapse to a degenerate output---all categories produce diverse, unique actions.}

\textbf{Finding 158: The gripper dimension (dim 6) varies widely even within ID (66.4 for highway, 119.8 for urban), suggesting task-specific rather than scene-specific encoding.}
