We decode the 7-dimensional action vectors (bins 0--255 from token IDs 31744--31999) predicted for ID and OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:action_under_ood}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig112_action_under_ood.png}
\caption{\textbf{Action output under OOD.} (a) Mean action per dimension by category: OOD categories (red/orange/purple/blue) diverge from ID (green) primarily in x-translation and y-translation. (b) Per-dimension divergence from ID center: x-translation shifts $-36$ to $-48$ bins for OOD. (c) Intra-category spread: OOD actions are 24\% more variable than ID.}
\label{fig:action_under_ood}
\end{figure}

The action analysis reveals that OOD detection is not merely a representation-space curiosity but has direct safety implications. When the VLA encounters an OOD input, it does not ``refuse'' to act or output a safe default; instead, it commands a systematically different trajectory. The consistent x-translation shift of $-36$ to $-48$ bins means the model would steer the vehicle differently when it encounters an unfamiliar scene. Combined with the 24\% increase in action variability, this creates a dangerous combination: the model both changes direction and becomes less consistent. Our cosine distance detector intercepts these OOD inputs before the actions reach the control system, providing a critical safety layer.

\subsection{Token Confidence Analysis}
\label{app:token_confidence}

\textbf{Finding 159: The model's own confidence (softmax probability) is NOT an effective OOD detector---AUROC = 0.558, barely above random. The VLA is equally confident in its OOD predictions (0.654) as in ID ones (0.665).}

\textbf{Finding 160: Token entropy is similarly uninformative for OOD detection (AUROC = 0.574, $d' = 0.09$). The action probability distribution has the same shape for ID and OOD inputs.}

\textbf{Finding 161: The VLA is a ``confidently wrong'' system---it predicts systematically biased OOD actions (Finding 156) with the same confidence as correct ID actions. This makes external OOD detection necessary, not optional.}

We analyze the softmax probability (confidence) and entropy of predicted action tokens for ID vs OOD inputs (Figure~\ref{fig:token_confidence}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig113_token_confidence.png}
\caption{\textbf{Token confidence analysis.} (a) Mean confidence per category: ID (0.665) and OOD (0.654) are nearly identical. (b) Token entropy: similarly indistinguishable. (c) Detection comparison: cosine distance (AUROC = 1.000) vs.\ confidence (AUROC = 0.558) vs.\ entropy (AUROC = 0.574)---internal uncertainty signals fail completely.}
\label{fig:token_confidence}
\end{figure}

This finding is perhaps the most important safety result in our study. The VLA's internal uncertainty signal---the softmax probability over action tokens---is uncalibrated for OOD detection. The model produces OOD actions with confidence 0.654, indistinguishable from its ID confidence of 0.665. This means the model cannot self-diagnose OOD inputs; it will confidently execute wrong actions. The cosine distance detector operates on a fundamentally different signal: the geometry of the hidden-state representation, which encodes scene identity even though the action-prediction head does not use this information for calibration. This decoupling between representation quality and prediction confidence is a well-known phenomenon in large language models, but we provide the first demonstration in the VLA setting with direct safety implications.

\subsection{Multi-Layer Embedding Fusion}
\label{app:multi_layer_fusion}

\textbf{Finding 162: Layer 3 alone achieves $d' = 113.2$, outperforming all multi-layer fusion strategies. The best fusion (averaging all 6 layers) achieves only $d' = 35.2$.}

\textbf{Finding 163: D-prime decreases monotonically with layer depth: layer 3 ($d' = 113.2$) $>$ layer 24 ($d' = 41.2$) $>$ layer 8 ($d' = 37.6$) $>$ layer 16 ($d' = 36.7$) $>$ layer 28 ($d' = 30.7$) $>$ layer 32 ($d' = 28.1$).}

\textbf{Finding 164: Multi-layer fusion (concatenation, averaging, max-score) consistently underperforms the best single layer. Fusion dilutes the strong early-layer signal with weaker late-layer contributions.}

We extract embeddings from layers 3, 8, 16, 24, 28, and 32 and test four fusion strategies (Figure~\ref{fig:multi_layer_fusion}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig114_multi_layer_fusion.png}
\caption{\textbf{Multi-layer fusion.} (a) Single-layer detection: layer 3 dominates with $d' = 113.2$, while the last layer (32) achieves only $d' = 28.1$. (b) Fusion strategy comparison: no fusion strategy matches layer 3 alone. (c) D-prime decreases with layer depth---early layers encode the strongest OOD geometric signal.}
\label{fig:multi_layer_fusion}
\end{figure}

The dominance of layer 3 reveals that OOD detection is fundamentally a low-level visual feature matching problem, even though it occurs within a high-level vision-language-action model. Layer 3 processes early visual features (edges, colors, spatial frequencies) that differ most between driving scenes and OOD inputs. By layer 32, the representation has been heavily modulated by the language model's task-specific processing, which reduces the geometric OOD signal. This finding has a clear practical recommendation: for maximum detection margin, use layer 3 ($d' = 113.2$). For simplest implementation, use the last layer ($d' = 28.1$)---both achieve AUROC = 1.000, and the 4$\times$ d-prime improvement from using layer 3 provides additional safety margin at no computational cost.

\subsection{Replication Stability}
\label{app:seed_stability}

\textbf{Finding 165: Inference is bitwise deterministic---repeated processing of the same image produces identical embeddings (max cosine distance $< 10^{-7}$) and zero-variance detection scores.}

\textbf{Finding 166: Cross-seed variation for same scene types is well-controlled: highway CV = 0.212, noise CV = 0.023. The detector produces consistent scores across instances of the same visual category.}

We test reproducibility by running 10 repeated inferences on each test image and measuring embedding variation (Figure~\ref{fig:seed_stability}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig115_seed_stability.png}
\caption{\textbf{Replication stability.} (a) All repeated inferences produce bitwise identical embeddings. (b) Detection scores have exactly zero variance across 10 repeats. (c) Cross-seed variation: different random seeds for the same scene type produce consistent scores (CV $\leq$ 0.21).}
\label{fig:seed_stability}
\end{figure}

The perfect determinism of inference eliminates a common concern in deployment: whether the detector's decisions are reproducible. Each input deterministically maps to exactly one embedding and one detection score, with no stochastic element. This means the same camera frame will always produce the same detection decision---no need for ensemble averaging, repeated inference, or probabilistic decision rules. The cross-seed variation (CV = 0.02--0.21) confirms that the detector generalizes across instances within a scene category, not just across identical inputs.

\subsection{Threshold Selection and Operating Points}
\label{app:threshold_analysis}

\textbf{Finding 167: A score gap of 0.100 ($17.3\sigma$) separates the maximum ID score (0.097) from the minimum OOD score (0.198). Any threshold in this range achieves perfect detection: precision = recall = F1 = 1.0, FPR = 0.0.}

\textbf{Finding 168: Fog highway is the hardest OOD category (score = 0.209), yet it remains $2\times$ above the maximum ID score. The conservative $3\sigma$ threshold (0.105) provides a 0.093 margin below the hardest OOD case.}

We analyze the full ROC curve and sweep 50 thresholds to characterize operating points (Figure~\ref{fig:threshold_analysis}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig116_threshold_analysis.png}
\caption{\textbf{Threshold analysis.} (a) Per-category score distributions showing the massive gap between ID (green) and OOD (red) categories. Fog is closest to ID but still clearly separated. (b) The detection gap is $17.3\sigma$---any threshold in the yellow zone achieves perfect detection. (c) Recommended thresholds: conservative ($3\sigma = 0.105$), moderate ($5\sigma = 0.117$), and relaxed (midpoint = 0.148), all within the safe zone.}
\label{fig:threshold_analysis}
\end{figure}

The threshold analysis completes the deployment specification for the cosine distance detector. The $17.3\sigma$ score gap means that threshold selection is not a challenging engineering problem---any value between 0.10 and 0.19 works perfectly. We recommend the conservative $3\sigma$ threshold ($t = 0.105$) because it maximizes the margin below the hardest OOD case (fog at 0.198) while remaining safely above all ID scores (max 0.097). In a production system, this threshold could be computed automatically from the calibration set as $t = \mu_\text{ID} + 3\sigma_\text{ID}$, requiring no manual tuning. The absence of any precision-recall tradeoff is the strongest possible result for a binary detector---it achieves the theoretical maximum on all metrics simultaneously.

\subsection{Computational Overhead}
\label{app:compute_overhead}

\textbf{Finding 169: OOD detection adds zero measurable overhead to VLA inference. The \texttt{output\_hidden\_states} flag adds $-1.1$~ms ($-0.8\%$), within noise. The cosine distance computation takes $49.5~\mu$s.}

\textbf{Finding 170: The full OOD detection pipeline (139~ms) is $2.5\times$ faster than action generation (345~ms). Detection can run in parallel with generation, completing before the model finishes producing action tokens.}

We profile each component of the inference and detection pipeline on an NVIDIA A40 GPU (Figure~\ref{fig:compute_overhead}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig117_compute_overhead.png}
\caption{\textbf{Computational overhead.} (a) Latency breakdown: OOD pipeline (139~ms) is $2.5\times$ faster than action generation (345~ms). (b) Detection overhead: effectively zero---hidden state extraction is free, cosine distance takes $50~\mu$s. (c) Deployment scenarios: sequential adds 40\% latency; parallel adds 0\% because detection completes before generation.}
\label{fig:compute_overhead}
\end{figure}

The zero-overhead property is perhaps the most practically important finding of this work. Because the transformer already computes hidden states as part of its normal forward pass, requesting them via \texttt{output\_hidden\_states=True} simply prevents their deallocation---no additional computation is required. The cosine distance between two 4096-dimensional vectors takes $50~\mu$s, which is $0.04\%$ of the forward pass time. In a pipelined deployment, the OOD check can be performed on the single forward pass that precedes autoregressive generation: the hidden states from the forward pass are used for the OOD check while the model simultaneously begins generating action tokens. Under this pipeline, OOD detection adds literally zero latency to the end-to-end inference.

\subsection{Distance Metric Comparison}
\label{app:distance_metrics}

\textbf{Finding 171: All seven distance metrics achieve perfect AUROC$=$1.000 for OOD detection. The OOD signal is so strong that metric choice cannot degrade binary classification.}

\textbf{Finding 172: Direction-based metrics (cosine $d{=}52.0$, correlation $d{=}52.0$) produce $3.1\times$ higher d-prime than magnitude-based metrics (Euclidean $d{=}16.7$, Chebyshev $d{=}9.6$), confirming the OOD signal is primarily directional.}

We compare seven distance metrics for centroid-based OOD detection using the same embedding pipeline (Figure~\ref{fig:distance_metrics}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig118_distance_metrics.png}
\caption{\textbf{Distance metric comparison.} (a) D-prime ranking: cosine and correlation distance lead at $d\approx52$. (b) Family comparison: direction-based metrics (green) consistently outperform magnitude-based metrics (blue). (c) Relative performance: Chebyshev retains only 19\% of cosine's d-prime because L$_\infty$ focuses on a single dimension, diluting the distributed signal.}
\label{fig:distance_metrics}
\end{figure}

The dominance of direction-based metrics is mechanistically consistent with our earlier findings. Experiment~123 showed the OOD signal spans 54.8\% of dimensions---a distributed directional shift rather than a localized magnitude change. Cosine distance captures this global directional shift optimally. Chebyshev distance ($L_\infty$), which focuses on the single largest dimension difference, achieves only $d{=}9.6$ because it ignores the coordinated signal across thousands of dimensions. Correlation distance ($d{=}52.0$) is nearly identical to cosine ($d{=}52.0$) because the embeddings have near-zero mean, making centering a no-op. The practical implication is clear: cosine distance is the optimal default for VLA OOD detection---simplest to implement, scale-invariant, and highest separation strength.

\subsection{Sample Efficiency}
\label{app:sample_efficiency}

\textbf{Finding 173: A single calibration image achieves AUROC$=$0.993 (worst case 0.956). From $n{\geq}8$ calibration samples, detection is perfect (AUROC$=$1.000) across all random subsets.}

\textbf{Finding 174: D-prime variance drops from $\sigma{=}21.3$ at $n{=}1$ to $\sigma{=}0$ at $n{=}30$, but the mean d-prime ($\approx$31) is stable across all calibration sizes, indicating the centroid is remarkably robust to subsampling.}

We measure detection quality as a function of calibration set size by subsampling from a pool of 30 ID embeddings and evaluating against a fixed test set of 10 ID + 80 OOD samples (Figure~\ref{fig:sample_efficiency}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig119_sample_efficiency.png}
\caption{\textbf{Sample efficiency.} (a) AUROC vs calibration size: $n{=}1$ achieves 0.993 mean AUROC; $n{\geq}8$ achieves perfect 1.000 across all trials. (b) D-prime stabilizes around 31$\sigma$ regardless of calibration size, with variance decreasing. (c) Centroid stability: d-prime standard deviation drops from 21.3 at $n{=}1$ to 0 at $n{=}30$.}
\label{fig:sample_efficiency}
\end{figure}

The extreme sample efficiency follows from the geometric structure of VLA embeddings. Because the ID cluster is extremely compact (intra-class spread $\ll$ ID-OOD gap), even a single sample lands close to the true centroid. The worst-case single-sample trial still achieves $d{=}12.2$ and AUROC$=$0.956---far above chance. This has profound deployment implications: a VLA system can be calibrated with as few as 8 representative driving images, requiring minimal data collection effort. Combined with the zero computational overhead (Finding~169) and the absence of threshold tuning (Finding~167), the complete OOD detection system can be deployed with negligible engineering overhead.

\subsection{Embedding Drift Under Input Perturbation}
\label{app:embedding_drift}

\textbf{Finding 175: Occlusion is the most disruptive perturbation, moving embeddings 96\% toward OOD at 90\% occlusion. Color jitter is least disruptive at only 25\% drift even at extreme levels.}

\textbf{Finding 176: Moderate perturbations (blur radius$=$3, noise $\sigma{=}30$, brightness$\times$0.5) push embeddings 40--70\% toward OOD territory, correctly triggering safety alerts via the cosine distance detector.}

We systematically perturb clean highway images with six corruption types at varying severity levels and measure how far the resulting embeddings drift from the clean ID centroid (Figure~\ref{fig:embedding_drift}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig120_embedding_drift.png}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding drift.} (a) All perturbation drift curves: occlusion and noise cause the largest drift; color jitter causes the least. (b) Maximum drift at extreme levels: occlusion reaches 96\% of OOD distance. (c) Drift at moderate perturbation: occlusion (10\%) and blur (radius$=$3) cause the most embedding movement.}
\label{fig:embedding_drift}
\end{figure}

The perturbation sensitivity profile reveals what visual features the VLA relies on for scene understanding. Color jitter causes minimal drift (25\% at extreme levels), indicating the model extracts structural/semantic features rather than color statistics. Occlusion is maximally disruptive because it destroys spatial structure---even 1\% central occlusion shifts embeddings 55\% toward OOD. Brightness changes show an asymmetry: darkening (factor$=$0.1, 81\% drift) is more disruptive than brightening (factor$=$3.0, 63\% drift), consistent with the greater challenge of nighttime driving. Crucially, these drift measurements validate the OOD detector as a \emph{graduated} safety monitor: perturbations that degrade the image quality proportionally increase the cosine distance from the ID centroid, providing a continuous measure of input reliability rather than a brittle binary classification.

\subsection{Prompt-Conditioned Embedding Geometry}
\label{app:prompt_geometry}

\textbf{Finding 177: All 8 diverse driving prompts achieve AUROC$=$1.000 with separation ratios in $[2.3, 2.8]$, suggesting a fundamental geometric property of the VLA embedding space.}

\textbf{Finding 178: Semantically similar prompts (stop$\leftrightarrow$park: 0.854, stop$\leftrightarrow$reverse: 0.842) cluster in centroid space, while overall mean inter-prompt similarity is only 0.616---prompts create distinct but uniformly separable embedding regions.}

We extend the cross-prompt analysis (Experiment~122) to 8 diverse prompts spanning forward driving, navigation, stopping, turning, parking, obstacle avoidance, and reversing (Figure~\ref{fig:prompt_geometry}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig121_prompt_embedding_geometry.png}
\caption{\textbf{Prompt-conditioned geometry.} (a) D-prime by prompt: all 8 prompts achieve $d > 32$; ``park'' is strongest at $d{=}67.4$. (b) Centroid similarity matrix: semantically related prompts (stop/park/reverse) cluster at 0.82--0.85 similarity while unrelated prompts drop to 0.50--0.55. (c) Cluster geometry: all prompts produce similar ID radius ($\sim$0.10) and gap ($\sim$0.25), yielding separation ratios of $\sim$2.5.}
\label{fig:prompt_geometry}
\end{figure}

The stability of the separation ratio across prompts ($2.3$--$2.8$) reveals a structural property of the VLA embedding space: regardless of the task prompt, ID and OOD inputs are mapped to regions with a consistent geometric relationship. This ratio is determined by the visual features, not the language features, of the input. The semantic clustering of centroids (stop/park/reverse forming a tight group at 0.82--0.85 similarity) confirms that the VLA has learned meaningful prompt semantics---yet this semantic organization does not compromise OOD detection because the ID-OOD separation axis is orthogonal to the prompt-conditioning axis.

\subsection{Action Token Position Analysis}
\label{app:action_position}

\textbf{Finding 179: The best single action dimension (x\_translation, AUROC$=$0.889) and combined 7-dim action vector (AUROC$=$0.787) are far inferior to embedding-based detection (AUROC$=$1.000), confirming that action space is a lossy projection that discards OOD signal.}

\textbf{Finding 180: All 7 action dimensions shift negative under OOD, with x\_translation shifting $-40.5$ bins. OOD inputs systematically produce more conservative, near-neutral actions.}

We test whether individual or combined action token values can serve as OOD detectors, comparing each of the 7 action dimensions and their L2 combination to the embedding-based detector (Figure~\ref{fig:action_position}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig122_action_token_position.png}
\caption{\textbf{Action token analysis.} (a) Per-dimension AUROC: x\_translation is best at 0.889; gripper is at chance (0.484). Combined 7-dim achieves only 0.787. (b) ID vs OOD profiles: all dimensions shift toward neutral under OOD. (c) Per-dimension shift: x\_translation ($-40.5$) and y\_translation ($-43.2$) show the largest shifts.}
\label{fig:action_position}
\end{figure}

The comparison between action-based and embedding-based OOD detection is stark: $d{=}1.1$ vs $d{\approx}50$---a $45\times$ difference in separation strength. This gap arises because the 7-dimensional action space is a highly compressed projection of the 4096-dimensional hidden state. The model must project the rich, OOD-discriminative embedding into a narrow action space optimized for control, not for uncertainty quantification. This information bottleneck is precisely why monitoring hidden states rather than outputs is essential for OOD detection in VLA systems.

\subsection{Hidden State Norm Analysis}
\label{app:norm_analysis}

\textbf{Finding 181: The final layer (L32) norm achieves AUROC$=$0.963 ($d{=}2.6$) for OOD detection---a useful signal, but $20\times$ weaker than cosine distance ($d{=}52$), confirming that the OOD signal is primarily directional, not in the magnitude.}

\textbf{Finding 182: Hidden state norms grow monotonically from 0.9 (L0) to 112.8 (L28) then drop to 75.5 at L32. OOD inputs have 14\% larger final-layer norms (86.1 vs 75.5), the only layer with strong norm separation.}

We test whether hidden state norms alone---without centroid reference---can serve as OOD detectors across 7 layers (Figure~\ref{fig:norms}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig123_hidden_state_norms.png}
\caption{\textbf{Norm analysis.} (a) ID vs OOD norms across layers: norms grow with depth, with OOD diverging only at layers 16 and 32. (b) Norm-based AUROC: most layers are at chance; only L32 achieves 0.963. (c) Comparison: norm-based methods (AUROC$\leq$0.963) are far weaker than cosine distance (AUROC$=$1.000).}
\label{fig:norms}
\end{figure}

This result decomposes the OOD signal into magnitude and direction components. The norm captures only the magnitude component, explaining why it achieves $d{=}2.6$ compared to cosine distance's $d{=}52$. Cosine distance is invariant to norm and captures the directional shift exclusively, which accounts for $95\%$ of the total OOD signal. The practical implication is that while norms provide a crude OOD signal requiring zero calibration (no centroid needed), cosine distance is overwhelmingly superior and should always be preferred when even a single calibration sample is available.

\subsection{OOD Category Difficulty Ranking}
\label{app:ood_difficulty}

\textbf{Finding 183: Fog is the only OOD category (out of 9 tested) that produces overlap with the ID distribution, achieving AUROC$=$0.976 rather than 1.000. All other categories---including desert, snow, construction, rain, underwater---are perfectly detected.}

\textbf{Finding 184: OOD detection difficulty correlates with structural similarity to ID scenes. Fog (50\% ID blend) preserves scene layout and is hardest ($d{=}3.1$). Rain, despite also being weather, is easy ($d{=}50.1$) because it fundamentally alters pixel statistics.}

We expand the OOD evaluation from 4 to 9 categories, adding fog, rain, construction zones, underwater, and desert scenes (Figure~\ref{fig:ood_difficulty}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig124_ood_category_difficulty.png}
\caption{\textbf{Category difficulty ranking.} (a) Distance distributions: fog (mean$=$0.108) sits close to ID, while rain and noise are distant. (b) Detection gap: only fog has negative gap (overlap). (c) D-prime: fog at $d{=}3.1$ is $16\times$ weaker than rain at $d{=}50.1$.}
\label{fig:ood_difficulty}
\end{figure}

The fog result is the most important finding for safety-critical deployment. Fog at 50\% opacity preserves the structural content of the driving scene---sky-ground boundary, road surface, lane markings---while merely reducing contrast. Because the VLA encodes \emph{structural} features rather than \emph{photometric} statistics, foggy inputs map near the clean ID cluster. This is precisely the failure mode that real-world safety systems must address: environmental conditions that preserve scene structure but degrade visual quality. We investigate fog detection in depth in the following experiment.

\subsection{Fog Detection Deep Dive}
\label{app:fog_deep_dive}

\textbf{Finding 185: Layer~3 perfectly detects fog from 30\% opacity onward (AUROC$=$1.000, $d{=}4.0$--$60.9$), while the last layer fails below 40\% opacity. At 50\% fog, L3 is $3.6\times$ more effective than L32 ($d{=}18.8$ vs $5.3$).}

\textbf{Finding 186: A dual-layer detector monitoring both L3 (photometric changes) and L32 (semantic changes) would achieve comprehensive OOD coverage, resolving the fog vulnerability discovered in Experiment~138.}

We sweep fog opacity from 10\% to 90\% and compare detection across 5 transformer layers (Figure~\ref{fig:fog_deep_dive}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig125_fog_deep_dive.png}
\caption{\textbf{Fog deep dive.} (a) AUROC vs opacity by layer: L3 (green) achieves perfect detection from 30\% opacity, while L32 (red) fails below 40\%. (b) D-prime comparison: L3 dominates at all fog levels. (c) L3 vs L32: early layers are $2$--$3.6\times$ better for fog, with the advantage peaking at 50\% opacity.}
\label{fig:fog_deep_dive}
\end{figure}

The layer-dependent sensitivity to fog reveals a fundamental architectural insight. Early layers (L3) encode \emph{photometric} features---brightness, contrast, edge sharpness---that fog directly disrupts. Late layers (L32) encode \emph{semantic} features---scene layout, object categories---that fog preserves. This complementarity motivates a practical dual-layer detection architecture: monitor L3 cosine distance for photometric corruptions (fog, low-light, glare) and L32 cosine distance for semantic domain shifts (indoor, underwater, construction). The combined detector would flag any input that is anomalous at either level, achieving the union of both detection capabilities at negligible additional cost (both hidden states are already computed).

\subsection{Dual-Layer Detector}
\label{app:dual_layer}

\textbf{Finding 187: Layer~3 alone achieves AUROC$=$1.000 with $d{=}128.2$ across all 10 categories including fog---$2.5\times$ the separation of the Layer~32 detector ($d{=}52$). L3 subsumes L32 for OOD detection.}

\textbf{Finding 188: Combining L3 and L32 (via max or mean) \emph{degrades} performance to AUROC$=$0.986 because L32's weak fog scores dilute L3's strong signal. The optimal detector uses L3 alone.}

We test four detection architectures across 10 OOD categories including fog at two opacity levels (Figure~\ref{fig:dual_layer}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig126_dual_layer_detector.png}
\caption{\textbf{Dual-layer detector.} (a) L3 achieves perfect AUROC$=$1.000; all other methods are 0.986. (b) Per-category scores: L3 distances are small but consistently separate ID from OOD; L32 distances are large but overlap for fog. (c) Per-category AUROC: all categories detected at 0.918+ including fog.}
\label{fig:dual_layer}
\end{figure}

This result overturns the initial assumption that a dual-layer detector would be optimal. Layer~3 alone captures low-level visual statistics (pixel distributions, edge patterns, spatial frequencies) that \emph{every} OOD category disrupts, including fog at 30\% opacity. The $d{=}128.2$ separation is the strongest we have observed---$2.5\times$ the best previous result. This suggests that the optimal OOD detector for VLA systems should monitor early hidden states rather than the commonly-used final layer. The finding has practical significance: L3 embeddings are computed first in the forward pass, enabling the earliest possible OOD detection in a pipelined architecture.

\subsection{Fine-Grained Early Layer Sweep}
\label{app:early_layer_sweep}

\textbf{Finding 189: Layer~3 is the globally optimal OOD detector with $d{=}175.2$---$5.2\times$ stronger than the last layer ($d{=}33.8$). Layers 1--7 all achieve perfect AUROC$=$1.000; a sharp cliff at Layer~8 marks the transition to mid-level features where fog breaks detection.}

\textbf{Finding 190: The d-prime profile across layers exhibits an asymmetric U-shape: early peak at L3 ($d{=}175$), mid-layer valley at L8--L16 ($d{\approx}35$--$40$, AUROC$<$1.0), and partial late recovery at L24--L32 ($d{\approx}35$--$55$, AUROC$=$1.0).}

We sweep all layers from 1 to 10 plus layers 16, 24, and 32 to identify the optimal monitoring point (Figure~\ref{fig:early_layer_sweep}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig127_early_layer_sweep.png}
\caption{\textbf{Early layer sweep.} (a) D-prime across all layers: L3 peaks at $d{=}175.2$, with the early layers dramatically outperforming late layers. (b) Layers 1--10 detail: L1--L7 achieve AUROC$=$1.000 (green); L8--L10 drop below (red). (c) AUROC across layers: a ``valley'' at L8--L16 where fog causes overlap.}
\label{fig:early_layer_sweep}
\end{figure}

The identification of Layer~3 as the optimal monitoring point is this paper's most actionable finding for system design. Previous OOD detection work in language models typically monitors the final hidden layer, but our results show this is suboptimal by a factor of 5$\times$ for VLA models. The mechanistic explanation is clear: Layer~3 encodes low-level visual statistics (pixel distributions, local textures, spatial frequency content) that \emph{every} OOD category disrupts---including fog, which preserves high-level scene structure but alters photometric properties. The sharp cliff at Layer~8 marks the transition from pixel-level to feature-level representations, where the model begins abstracting away the photometric details that distinguish clean from corrupted inputs.

\subsection{Layer~3 Comprehensive Evaluation}
\label{app:l3_comprehensive}

\textbf{Finding 191: The Layer~3 detector achieves perfect AUROC$=$1.000 on 12 out of 13 OOD categories spanning weather, lighting, domain shifts, and noise. Overall AUROC$=$0.998, $d{=}87.6$. Only 30\% fog ($d{=}3.8$, AUROC$=$0.973) is imperfect.}

\textbf{Finding 192: D-prime spans a $60\times$ range from 3.8 (fog 30\%) to 224.8 (random noise), revealing the detection difficulty hierarchy: lighting changes $>$ domain shifts $>$ weather corruptions $>$ near-ID fog.}

We evaluate the Layer~3 cosine distance detector across 13 OOD categories---the broadest evaluation in this study---including two new categories (night driving, forest) not previously tested (Figure~\ref{fig:l3_comprehensive}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig128_l3_comprehensive.png}
\caption{\textbf{L3 comprehensive evaluation.} (a) D-prime ranking across 13 categories: noise ($d{=}225$) to fog\_30\% ($d{=}3.8$). (b) AUROC: 12/13 categories at 1.000; fog\_30\% at 0.973. (c) By family: lighting and domain shifts are easily detected; weather (especially fog) is hardest.}
\label{fig:l3_comprehensive}
\end{figure}

This comprehensive evaluation establishes the Layer~3 cosine distance detector as a practical, near-universal OOD detection method for VLA systems. The only persistent failure mode is very light fog ($\leq$30\% opacity), which barely perturbs pixel statistics. At 50\% fog opacity, detection is already perfect ($d{=}16.2$, AUROC$=$1.000). The detection difficulty hierarchy---with fog as the hardest category---aligns with real-world driving safety priorities: fog is indeed the environmental condition most challenging for human and autonomous drivers alike.

\subsection{Layer~3 Sample Efficiency}
\label{app:l3_sample_efficiency}

\textbf{Finding 193: L3 achieves $5\times$ higher d-prime than L32 (149 vs 32) but plateaus at AUROC$\approx$0.94 due to fog overlap, while L32 reaches perfect AUROC$=$1.000 at $n{\geq}8$.}

\textbf{Finding 194: The L3-L32 tradeoff represents a speed-vs-completeness choice: L3 detects more categories more strongly but has a fog blind spot; L32 has weaker separation but no blind spots (excluding fog from test set).}

We replicate the sample efficiency analysis (Experiment~133) at Layer~3 and compare directly to Layer~32 (Figure~\ref{fig:l3_sample_efficiency}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig129_l3_sample_efficiency.png}
\caption{\textbf{L3 vs L32 sample efficiency.} (a) AUROC: L32 reaches perfect detection at $n{\geq}8$; L3 plateaus $\sim$0.94 regardless of sample size. (b) D-prime: L3 is consistently $5\times$ stronger. (c) Summary comparison across key metrics.}
\label{fig:l3_sample_efficiency}
\end{figure}

The layer selection tradeoff crystallizes the architectural design space for VLA OOD detection. For deployment environments where fog is uncommon or separately addressed (e.g., via weather sensors), Layer~3 is unambiguously optimal. For environments where fog detection is critical, a simple OR-gate combining L3 and L32 thresholds would catch fog via L32 while benefiting from L3's superior detection of all other categories.

\subsection{OR-Gate Dual-Layer Detector}
\label{app:or_gate}

\textbf{Finding 195: The OR-gate detector (L3$\lor$L32) achieves perfect recall$=$1.000 across all 8 OOD categories including fog at 30\% opacity, with F1$=$0.997 and FPR$=$2.5\%.}

\textbf{Finding 196: The OR-gate resolves the fog vulnerability: L3 catches non-fog categories with $d{>}100$, while L32 provides complementary coverage for fog. Neither layer alone achieves perfect recall, but their union does.}

We implement four detection strategies with 3$\sigma$ thresholds and evaluate on 160 OOD + 40 ID samples (Figure~\ref{fig:or_gate}).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig130_or_gate_detector.png}
\caption{\textbf{OR-gate detector.} (a) Strategy comparison: OR gate achieves optimal F1$=$0.997; AND gate sacrifices recall (0.912) for zero FPR. (b) OR gate recall: 1.000 on all 8 categories. (c) Layer complementarity: L3 catches everything; L32 misses fog\_30\% (0.350 recall); OR gate combines both strengths.}
\label{fig:or_gate}
\end{figure}

The OR-gate architecture represents our recommended production deployment: two cosine distance computations (one at L3, one at L32) against their respective centroids, with an OOD flag raised if either exceeds its 3$\sigma$ threshold. Total computational cost: 100~$\mu$s (two cosine distances on 4096-d vectors). Total false positive rate: 2.5\%, which can be reduced with larger calibration sets. The system requires only $\sim$8 calibration images per layer, no training, no gradient computation, and adds zero latency to the inference pipeline.

\subsection{Mahalanobis vs Cosine Distance}
\label{app:mahalanobis}

\textbf{Finding 197}: At layer 3, Mahalanobis distance (PCA-based, with covariance normalization) achieves AUROC$=$1.000 vs cosine's 0.950. At layer 32, cosine distance wins (AUROC$=$0.965 vs 0.942). The optimal metric depends on the layer.

\textbf{Finding 198}: Mahalanobis distance resolves the fog\_30\% vulnerability at L3 (AUROC 0.85$\to$1.00 for cosine$\to$Mahalanobis) and improves occlusion detection (0.75$\to$1.00). The covariance normalization amplifies OOD signal in low-variance directions where cosine distance is blind.

\textbf{Finding 199}: At L32, Mahalanobis degrades detection for noise (1.0$\to$0.983), snow (1.0$\to$0.983), and occlusion (1.0$\to$0.917). The L32 embedding space is more isotropic, so covariance normalization introduces noise.

\textbf{Finding 200}: The layer$\times$metric interaction reveals that OOD signals live in different subspaces at different depths. Early layers (L3) have anisotropic covariance where Mahalanobis excels; late layers (L32) have more balanced variance where cosine suffices.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig131_mahalanobis_vs_cosine.png}
\caption{\textbf{Mahalanobis vs cosine distance.} (A) Per-category AUROC: Mahalanobis at L3 achieves perfect detection on all categories including fog\_30\% (1.000 vs 0.850 for cosine). (B) d-prime shows cosine dominates at L32 for most categories. (C) Summary: L3-Mahalanobis achieves the highest overall AUROC (1.000).}
\label{fig:mahalanobis}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Threshold Sensitivity Analysis}
\label{app:threshold}

\textbf{Finding 201}: The OR-gate detector is robust to threshold choice. F1 exceeds 0.93 across the range $\sigma \in [2.0, 3.5]$, requiring no precise tuning. $\sigma{=}2.5$ maximizes F1$=$0.960 with 6.7\% FPR; $\sigma{=}3.5$ achieves zero FPR with F1$=$0.933.

\textbf{Finding 202}: Recall degrades gracefully from 0.984 at $\sigma{=}1.0$ to 0.828 at $\sigma{=}6.0$ with no cliff-edge behavior. This smooth degradation enables principled threshold selection based on application-specific precision-recall requirements.

\textbf{Finding 203}: At $\sigma{=}3.0$, the OR gate and L32-only strategies converge (both recall$=$0.875, FPR$=$0.067), while AND gate equals L3-only (recall$=$0.797, FPR$=$0.000). This confirms L3 is the conservative detector and L32 is the sensitive one.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig132_threshold_sensitivity.png}
\caption{\textbf{Threshold sensitivity.} (A) ROC curves: OR gate dominates the recall-FPR tradeoff at all thresholds. (B) F1 vs $\sigma$: peak F1$=$0.960 at $\sigma{=}2.5$; robust operating window $\sigma \in [2.0, 3.5]$. (C) Heatmap at $\sigma{=}3.0$: per-category recall across strategies.}
\label{fig:threshold}
\end{figure}

\subsection{PCA Dimensionality of ID Manifold}
\label{app:pca}

\textbf{Finding 204}: The L3 ID embedding manifold is essentially \textbf{2-dimensional}: just 2 PCA components capture 97.6\% of variance in 4096-D space. L32 requires 10 components for equivalent coverage (95.4\%). This 5$\times$ dimensionality gap explains L3's superior OOD discrimination.

\textbf{Finding 205}: The low intrinsic dimensionality of L3 creates a ``thin manifold'' effect: any perturbation is almost certain to push embeddings off the 2D ID subspace. OOD reconstruction error at $k{=}2$ ranges from 27$\times$ (fog\_30) to 388$\times$ (night) that of ID, providing an alternative detection signal.

\textbf{Finding 206}: L32's singular value spectrum decays gradually (112.8$\to$92.4$\to$25.1$\to$...) while L3's drops sharply after component 2 (0.797$\to$0.581$\to$0.085). This spectral gap at L3 creates a natural threshold between the ID subspace and its orthogonal complement.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig133_pca_dimensionality.png}
\caption{\textbf{PCA dimensionality.} (A) Singular value spectrum: L3 has a sharp spectral gap after 2 components; L32 decays gradually. (B) Cumulative variance: L3 reaches 97.6\% at $k{=}2$; L32 needs $k{=}10$ for 95\%. (C) OOD/ID reconstruction error ratio shows L3 at $k{=}2$ provides extreme anomaly signal.}
\label{fig:pca}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Cross-Prompt OR-Gate Validation}
\label{app:cross_prompt}

\textbf{Finding 207}: Cross-prompt transfer \textbf{fails catastrophically}. When the inference prompt differs from the calibration prompt, FPR$=$1.000 — every ID image is flagged as OOD. The prompt embedding shift is 3--5$\times$ larger than the ID cluster radius, causing the detector to degenerate to ``flag everything'' mode.

\textbf{Finding 208}: Same-prompt detection remains robust: diagonal F1 ranges from 0.933 (drive, follow, stop) to 0.957 (navigate, turn\_left), with FPR$=$0.000 for all prompts. The OR-gate is prompt-specific but reliable within a fixed prompt.

\textbf{Finding 209}: This is a critical deployment constraint: the calibration set must use the \textbf{exact} inference prompt. For multi-prompt systems, each prompt requires its own calibration centroid, motivating a prompt-aware multi-centroid architecture.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig134_cross_prompt_orgate.png}
\caption{\textbf{Cross-prompt OR-gate validation.} (A) F1 transfer matrix: diagonal (same-prompt) achieves F1$\geq$0.933; off-diagonal (cross-prompt) collapses to 0.857. (B) FPR matrix: cross-prompt FPR$=$1.000 universally. (C) Same vs cross-prompt F1: 0.933--0.957 vs 0.857 — the gap reflects catastrophic prompt mismatch.}
\label{fig:cross_prompt}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Multi-Centroid Prompt-Aware Detector}
\label{app:multi_centroid}

\textbf{Finding 210}: The nearest-centroid multi-prompt strategy \textbf{perfectly matches oracle (same-prompt) performance}: F1$=$0.933, FPR$=$0.000 across all 5 prompts. L32 embeddings reliably identify which prompt was used, routing to the correct per-prompt centroid.

\textbf{Finding 211}: Cross-prompt failure (Exp.~148, FPR$=$1.0) is \textbf{completely resolved} by maintaining one centroid per prompt and routing via nearest-centroid matching. The fix adds only $N$ cosine distance computations (negligible cost).

\textbf{Finding 212}: The recommended production architecture is: \textbf{multi-centroid OR-gate with nearest-centroid routing}. Calibrate once per prompt ($\sim$8 images each), store $N$ centroids per layer, and route at inference via L32 nearest centroid. Total overhead: $2N$ cosine distances on 4096-D vectors ($\sim$50$N~\mu$s).

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig135_multi_centroid.png}
\caption{\textbf{Multi-centroid prompt-aware detector.} (A) Strategy comparison: nearest centroid matches oracle F1$=$0.933; wrong-prompt collapses to 0.889 with FPR$=$1.0. (B) FPR: nearest centroid achieves 0.000 vs wrong-prompt's 1.000. (C) Per-prompt breakdown: nearest centroid is identical to oracle across all 5 prompts.}
\label{fig:multi_centroid}
\end{figure}

\subsection{PCA Reconstruction-Based OOD Detection}
\label{app:recon}

\textbf{Finding 213}: PCA reconstruction error with $k{=}2$ components achieves \textbf{perfect AUROC$=$1.000 at both L3 and L32}, surpassing cosine distance (0.983 and 0.963 respectively). This is the first metric to achieve perfect separation at both layers simultaneously.

\textbf{Finding 214}: The critical threshold is $k{=}2$: $k{=}1$ yields AUROC$=$0.988 (L3) and 0.958 (L32), but $k{=}2$ achieves perfection. Additional components add no value, confirming the 2D intrinsic dimensionality of the ID manifold.

\textbf{Finding 215}: Unlike the cosine-vs-Mahalanobis comparison where the optimal metric depended on the layer, PCA reconstruction at $k{=}2$ is \textbf{universally optimal} — the same method works best at both L3 and L32. This simplifies the detector architecture.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig136_recon_ood.png}
\caption{\textbf{PCA reconstruction-based OOD detection.} (A) AUROC vs number of PCA components: $k{=}2$ achieves perfect 1.000 at both layers, beating cosine baselines (dashed). (B) Per-category AUROC: reconstruction k$=$2 achieves 1.000 on all categories at both layers. (C) d-prime comparison across all methods.}
\label{fig:recon}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Unified Detector Comparison}
\label{app:unified}

\textbf{Finding 216}: PCA reconstruction error, despite achieving AUROC$=$1.000 (Exp.~150), fails as a threshold-based classifier: FPR$=$0.850 at 3$\sigma$ threshold. The reconstruction error distribution violates the Gaussian assumption underlying $\sigma$-based thresholds.

\textbf{Finding 217}: Cosine distance with nearest-centroid routing achieves \textbf{F1$=$0.958, FPR$=$0.000} across all 4 prompts, matching oracle (same-prompt) performance. This is the \textbf{recommended production architecture}: simple, robust, and prompt-agnostic.

\textbf{Finding 218}: The gap between AUROC$=$1.000 (ranking metric) and F1$=$0.904 (threshold metric) for PCA reconstruction highlights that \textbf{ranking performance does not guarantee threshold performance}. Calibration statistics must match the score distribution.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig137_unified_detector.png}
\caption{\textbf{Unified detector comparison.} (A) F1: cosine methods achieve 0.958; PCA-recon drops to 0.904 due to FPR. (B) Recall vs FPR: PCA-recon has perfect recall but unacceptable FPR; cosine achieves zero FPR. (C) Cosine same-prompt and nearest-centroid are identical per prompt.}
\label{fig:unified}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Calibration Set Diversity}
\label{app:diversity}

\textbf{Finding 219}: Calibration diversity is worth $\sim$15\% AUROC: diverse calibration (3 highway + 3 urban + 3 rural) achieves L3 AUROC$=$0.989 vs 0.846--0.896 for any homogeneous set. Same total sample count ($n{=}9$), dramatically different performance.

\textbf{Finding 220}: L32 benefits more from diversity than L3 (AUROC gain: +0.15 at L32 vs +0.09 at L3). L32's higher intrinsic dimensionality (10D vs 2D) makes the centroid more sensitive to calibration composition.

\textbf{Finding 221}: Practical calibration guideline: include $\geq$2--3 representative scene types even if total count stays fixed. \textbf{Diversity matters more than quantity} — 3$\times$3 diverse outperforms 9$\times$1 homogeneous by a large margin.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig138_cal_diversity.png}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration diversity.} (A) Overall AUROC: diverse (3 each) achieves 0.989 at L3, far exceeding any single-type calibration (0.80--0.90). (B) Per-category comparison at L3: diverse calibration improves ALL categories. (C) d-prime confirms diversity boosts separation at both layers.}
\label{fig:diversity}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Temporal Consistency}
\label{app:temporal}

\textbf{Finding 222}: The OOD detector exhibits \textbf{zero jitter} across all four temporal corruption sequences (fog, night, blur, noise). Once the detector transitions to OOD, it never reverts — producing stable, monotonic flags critical for real-time deployment.

\textbf{Finding 223}: L32 cosine distances increase \textbf{monotonically} with corruption severity (e.g., fog 0\%$\to$90\% maps to L32 distance 0.040$\to$0.394). This enables not just binary detection but \textbf{severity estimation} for graduated response (slow down vs emergency stop).

\textbf{Finding 224}: Blur shows saturation at L32 — distance peaks at radius$\approx$8 then plateaus. This is consistent with the vision encoder's effective receptive field, beyond which additional blur produces diminishing embedding shifts.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig139_temporal_consistency.png}
\caption{\textbf{Temporal consistency.} Distance profiles under gradual corruption (20 frames each). All sequences show monotonic L32 distance increase with zero jitter. L3 distances are 100$\times$ smaller but equally monotonic. Blur saturates at radius$\approx$8.}
\label{fig:temporal}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Adversarial Robustness}
\label{app:adversarial}

\textbf{Finding 225}: The OOD detector is robust to 4 of 5 gradient-free attack types (random patch, color shift, spatial shift, channel swap), detecting corruptions even at modest pixel MSE levels ($\sim$12--140).

\textbf{Finding 226}: \textbf{High-frequency checkerboard patterns evade detection}: amplitude 40 produces MSE$=$1491 (significant pixel corruption) but L32 distance$=$0.019 (below threshold). The ViT's 16$\times$16 patch embedding acts as a low-pass filter, smoothing away high-frequency perturbations before they reach the embedding layers.

\textbf{Finding 227}: This vulnerability has \textbf{low practical risk}: high-frequency patterns are destroyed by camera noise, JPEG compression, and sensor pipelines. Natural driving corruptions (fog, rain, blur, night) are all low-frequency and robustly detected.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig140_adversarial.png}
\caption{\textbf{Adversarial robustness.} (A) Pixel MSE vs L32 distance: most attacks follow the detection boundary; high-freq evades (bottom-right zone). (B) Detection rate: 100\% for random patch and color shift; 67\% for high-freq. (C) High-freq detail: MSE grows with amplitude while L32 stays below threshold up to amplitude 40.}
\label{fig:adversarial}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Action Corruption Under OOD Inputs}

Having established that the OOD detector reliably identifies distribution shifts, we now ask: \textbf{how much do OOD inputs actually corrupt the model's action outputs?} This is the safety-critical question — detection only matters if OOD inputs produce dangerous control errors.

We generate 7-token action sequences using \texttt{model.generate()} for 6 test images under 12 corruption conditions, comparing the resulting action tokens to clean baselines.

\textbf{Finding 228}: Night driving is the most action-disruptive corruption: all 7/7 action tokens change (L2 action shift = 0.789, MAE = 0.350), producing completely different control commands. This corroborates the detector's highest sensitivity to night (d' = 22.1 at L3).

\textbf{Finding 229}: Even the mildest corruption (fog at 20\% opacity) alters 3.3 out of 7 action tokens (47\%), demonstrating that OOD inputs produce safety-relevant control changes well before they become visually obvious to a human operator.

\textbf{Finding 230}: The \textbf{gripper dimension} (dim 6) is the most frequently affected action dimension across corruptions (8 of 12 types), likely because it is the least constrained dimension for driving tasks and thus most sensitive to embedding perturbations.

\textbf{Finding 231}: Action corruption severity is \textbf{non-monotonic} with visual corruption intensity: fog at 60\% produces larger action shift (L2 = 0.608) than fog at 80\% (L2 = 0.428); blur at radius 4 produces the highest overall shift (L2 = 1.078) while radius 16 is less disruptive (L2 = 0.394). This means simple corruption severity metrics cannot predict safety impact.

\textbf{Finding 232}: The non-monotonic corruption-to-action mapping provides a strong argument for dedicated OOD detection: since visual severity does not predict control error magnitude, a safety system cannot rely on pixel-level corruption metrics and must instead monitor the internal representation space where our detector operates.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig141_action_corruption.png}
\caption{\textbf{Action corruption heatmap.} Left: MAE per action dimension across 12 corruption types — gripper and z-axis are most affected. Right: L2 action shift and total MAE — blur\_4 and night produce the largest control deviations.}
\label{fig:action_corruption}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig142_token_disruption.png}
\caption{\textbf{Token disruption analysis.} Left: Mean tokens changed per corruption — night changes all 7/7, even mild fog changes 3.3/7. Right: Gripper is the most frequently affected dimension across corruption types.}
\label{fig:token_disruption}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Confidence Calibration: OOD Distance vs Model Entropy}

We investigate whether the model's own output uncertainty — measured via action token logit entropy — provides a complementary safety signal to our external embedding-based detector.

For 19 corruption conditions $\times$ 6 test images, we extract both cosine distances (L3, L32) and next-token logit entropy (computed over the 256 action token bins).

\textbf{Finding 233}: OOD distance and model entropy are \textbf{moderately correlated}: L32 distance vs action entropy $r = 0.604$, L3 vs action entropy $r = 0.461$. The signals are related but far from redundant.

\textbf{Finding 234}: Action token entropy perfectly tracks full vocabulary entropy ($r = 1.000$), meaning that OOD-induced uncertainty is not confined to the action head but propagates across the entire output distribution.

\textbf{Finding 235}: Night driving produces extreme model uncertainty: action entropy = 4.056 (2.6$\times$ clean), top-1 probability drops to 0.161. The model ``knows it doesn't know,'' making entropy a useful supplementary signal for severe corruptions.

\textbf{Finding 236}: \textbf{Entropy is dangerously unreliable for mild corruptions}: fog at 10--30\% opacity produces \emph{lower} entropy than clean images (1.35--1.46 vs 1.57), meaning the model becomes \emph{more confident} under mild OOD conditions. Our cosine distance detector correctly identifies these cases while entropy misses them entirely.

\textbf{Finding 237}: The inverse correlation between L32 distance and top-1 probability ($r = -0.516$) confirms that increased OOD severity generally reduces model confidence — but the relationship is too weak and non-monotonic to serve as a reliable detection threshold.

\textbf{Finding 238}: These results establish a clear hierarchy for safety monitoring: embedding-based OOD detection (cosine distance) is the primary safety signal; entropy-based confidence provides a useful \textbf{supplementary} signal for severe corruptions but must never serve as the standalone safety metric due to its paradoxical behavior under mild distribution shift.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig143_confidence_calibration.png}
\caption{\textbf{OOD distance vs model confidence.} Left: L3 distance vs action entropy ($r = 0.461$). Center: L32 distance vs entropy ($r = 0.604$). Right: L32 distance vs top-1 probability ($r = -0.516$). Colors indicate corruption category.}
\label{fig:confidence_calibration}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig144_confidence_summary.png}
\caption{\textbf{Confidence calibration summary.} Left: Distance and entropy by condition — note the non-monotonic entropy for fog. Right: Correlation matrix — L32 distance correlates best with entropy but remains moderate ($r \approx 0.6$).}
\label{fig:confidence_summary}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Embedding Space Visualization}

To build geometric intuition for the OOD detection mechanism, we project the full-dimensional embeddings to 2D via PCA centered on the ID mean.

\textbf{Finding 239}: At L3, the top two principal components capture 55.2\% of variance (PC1=37.9\%, PC2=17.4\%), while at L32 they capture only 40.9\% (PC1=25.7\%, PC2=15.2\%). This confirms that L3 embeddings are more concentrated in a low-dimensional subspace, consistent with the 2-dimensional ID manifold found in Experiment~147.

\textbf{Finding 240}: In the 2D projection, ID images form a tight cluster (radius 0.112 at L3) while OOD categories form distinct, well-separated groups. Night driving is the most distant (0.677 at L3, 50.7 at L32), while occlusion is closest to the ID boundary (0.060 at L3, within the ID radius).

\textbf{Finding 241}: The L32 embedding space has a much larger ID radius (32.3) compared to L3 (0.112), a 288$\times$ difference. This explains why L3 provides tighter calibration with smaller threshold margins, while L32 requires larger absolute thresholds but operates in a space with proportionally larger OOD separations.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig145_embedding_viz.png}
\caption{\textbf{Embedding space visualization.} 2D PCA projections at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Green markers: ID images (circle=highway, square=urban, triangle=rural). Colored crosses: OOD categories. Dashed circle: ID radius. Night (purple) is most distant; occlusion (teal) is closest to ID.}
\label{fig:embedding_viz}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig146_cluster_distances.png}
\caption{\textbf{OOD cluster distances.} Distance from ID centroid in 2D PCA space at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Dashed line: ID radius. Night and blur produce the largest separations; fog\_30 and occlusion are closest.}
\label{fig:cluster_distances}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Ensemble OOD Scoring}

We investigate whether combining multiple distance metrics (cosine, Mahalanobis, PCA reconstruction) across layers into ensemble scores can improve detection beyond individual metrics.

Six individual scores (3 metrics $\times$ 2 layers) are z-score normalized against calibration statistics and summed in 10 ensemble combinations.

\textbf{Finding 242}: The best individual metrics are PCA reconstruction at both layers (AUROC = 1.000) and cosine distance at L32 (0.992). Mahalanobis at L32 underperforms (0.829) due to numerical instability with $n_\text{cal} = 10 \ll d = 4096$.

\textbf{Finding 243}: Five ensemble combinations achieve perfect AUROC = 1.000, including the selective combination \textbf{cos\_L3 + maha\_L3 + cos\_L32}. This ensemble achieves perfect per-category detection across all 6 corruption types, including the challenging fog\_30 category.

\textbf{Finding 244}: \textbf{Adding more metrics can hurt}: the ``all\_6'' ensemble (AUROC = 0.829) performs worse than selective ensembles because the noisy Mahalanobis L32 score dominates the normalized sum. This demonstrates that \textbf{selective ensembling} — choosing complementary metrics and discarding unstable ones — outperforms naive ``use everything'' approaches.

\textbf{Finding 245}: The practical recommendation for deployment is a 3-score ensemble (cos\_L3 + maha\_L3 + cos\_L32): it achieves AUROC = 1.000 with the diversity benefits of multi-metric evaluation while avoiding the instability of high-dimensional Mahalanobis estimation at L32.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig147_ensemble_auroc.png}
\caption{\textbf{Ensemble OOD scoring.} Left: Individual metric AUROCs — recon and cosine lead, Mahalanobis L32 lags. Right: Ensemble combinations — 5 achieve perfect AUROC; ``all\_6'' is dragged down by maha\_L32.}
\label{fig:ensemble_auroc}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig148_ensemble_percategory.png}
\caption{\textbf{Per-category ensemble performance.} cos\_L3+maha\_L3+cos\_L32 achieves 1.00 across all categories. The all\_6 ensemble drops to 0.38 on occlusion and 0.65 on fog\_30 due to maha\_L32 noise.}
\label{fig:ensemble_percategory}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Layer-wise OOD Information Flow}

We trace how OOD signal propagates through all 33 transformer layers (0--32), revealing the architectural basis for our dual-layer detector design.

\textbf{Finding 246}: Layer 0 (raw token embedding) contains \textbf{zero OOD signal} ($d' = 0.0$ for all corruptions). OOD information is created entirely by the transformer's processing of visual tokens through attention and feed-forward layers.

\textbf{Finding 247}: Different corruptions peak at \textbf{different optimal layers}: night peaks at L7 ($d' = 30.1$), blur at L2 ($d' = 7.0$), noise at L32 ($d' = 13.6$), fog at L31 ($d' = 6.1$), and occlusion at L26 ($d' = 3.5$). No single layer is optimal for all corruption types.

\textbf{Finding 248}: OOD signal shows a characteristic \textbf{dip in middle layers} (L5--L20) before recovering in late layers (L29--L32). Night driving exhibits a bimodal profile with peaks at L7 and L30, suggesting the model processes visual anomalies in two distinct phases.

\textbf{Finding 249}: Early layers (L1--L4) provide strong initial OOD detection for all corruption types (night $d' \approx 24$, blur $d' \approx 7$, noise $d' \approx 8$), while late layers (L29--L32) recover signal for corruptions that early layers partially suppress (fog, occlusion). This validates the OR-gate dual-layer architecture: L3 captures early visual anomalies while L32 captures high-level semantic shifts.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig149_layer_flow.png}
\caption{\textbf{Layer-wise OOD information flow.} Left: $d'$ heatmap across all 33 layers and 6 corruption types — white stars mark peak layers. Right: $d'$ traces show corruption-specific profiles with early peaks (night, blur), late peaks (fog, noise), and middle-layer dips.}
\label{fig:layer_flow}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig150_peak_layers.png}
\caption{\textbf{Peak detection layers.} Left: Optimal layer per corruption — different corruptions peak at different depths. Right: Mean $d'$ by layer group — early layers detect all corruptions; late layers add value for fog and occlusion.}
\label{fig:peak_layers}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Deployment Simulation}

We simulate a complete deployment pipeline with detection, severity estimation, and graduated response to validate the system's behavior under realistic driving scenarios.

Three response levels above NORMAL are defined using calibration statistics: \textbf{ADVISORY} (3$\sigma$, reduce speed 20\%), \textbf{WARNING} (6$\sigma$, reduce speed 50\%), and \textbf{EMERGENCY} (9$\sigma$, initiate safe stop).

\textbf{Finding 250}: In the fog onset scenario, the system produces \textbf{smooth graduated escalation}: NORMAL at fog $\alpha < 0.4$, ADVISORY at $\alpha = 0.4$, WARNING at $\alpha = 0.5$, EMERGENCY at $\alpha \geq 0.6$. Fog clearing follows the reverse de-escalation pattern, demonstrating temporal consistency.

\textbf{Finding 251}: Night onset triggers \textbf{rapid escalation}: brightness dropping from 1.0 to 0.6 jumps from NORMAL directly to EMERGENCY, skipping WARNING entirely. This reflects the detector's high sensitivity to illumination changes and suggests the 3-level system may need temporal smoothing for rapid-onset conditions.

\textbf{Finding 252}: Scene transitions (highway $\to$ urban $\to$ rural $\to$ highway) produce \textbf{zero false alarms} — all 11 frames remain NORMAL. The diverse calibration set (3 scene types $\times$ 3 images) correctly prevents false positives during normal driving variations, validating the calibration diversity finding from Experiment~152.

\textbf{Finding 253}: The complete pipeline — calibration, detection, severity estimation, graduated response — runs with zero implementation overhead beyond a single forward pass with \texttt{output\_hidden\_states=True}, making it deployable as a lightweight safety wrapper around any VLA model.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig151_deployment_sim.png}
\caption{\textbf{Deployment simulation.} Top: Fog cycle — smooth escalation from NORMAL through ADVISORY and WARNING to EMERGENCY at fog $\alpha \geq 0.6$. Bottom: Day/night — rapid escalation to EMERGENCY at brightness $\leq 0.6$.}
\label{fig:deployment_sim}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig152_deployment_metrics.png}
\caption{\textbf{Deployment metrics.} Left: Scene transitions remain NORMAL with zero false alarms. Right: Response level distribution — fog cycle shows balanced escalation; night is dominated by EMERGENCY.}
\label{fig:deployment_metrics}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Feature Ablation: OOD Signal Localization}

We investigate \emph{where} in the 4096-dimensional embedding space the OOD signal resides.

\textbf{Finding 254}: Projecting embeddings onto the top-$k$ PCA directions of the ID calibration data yields AUROC $< 0.5$ (L3: 0.16--0.44, L32: 0.00--0.13). The OOD-discriminative signal is \textbf{orthogonal to the principal components of ID variance}, meaning the model's representations of OOD inputs differ from ID in dimensions that carry minimal ID variation.

\textbf{Finding 255}: Zeroing out blocks of 64--512 raw dimensions produces \textbf{zero AUROC change} from the baseline of 1.000. The OOD signal is holographically distributed across all dimensions, not concentrated in any localized feature subset.

\textbf{Finding 256}: These two findings together explain why \textbf{PCA reconstruction error is an effective OOD detector}: it measures exactly the embedding energy in the orthogonal complement of the low-dimensional ID manifold, which is precisely where the OOD signal resides. They also explain why cosine distance outperforms Euclidean distance --- cosine measures angular deviation in the full space, while Euclidean distance can be dominated by high-variance ID dimensions that carry no OOD information.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig153_feature_ablation.png}
\caption{\textbf{Feature ablation.} Left: PCA subspace detection fails (AUROC $< 0.5$) because the OOD signal is orthogonal to ID variance directions. Center: Rapid singular value decay confirms low-dimensional ID manifold. Right: L3 needs only 2 components for 95\% variance; L32 needs 4.}
\label{fig:feature_ablation}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Sample Efficiency with Confidence Intervals}

We systematically measure how detection performance scales with calibration set size using bootstrapped 95\% confidence intervals.

\textbf{Finding 257}: The detector is remarkably sample-efficient: \textbf{5 calibration images} achieve AUROC $= 0.90$ (95\% CI: 0.83--0.96) at L3, sufficient for practical deployment. Even $n = 2$ gives AUROC $= 0.78$.

\textbf{Finding 258}: Performance plateaus around $n = 10$--15, with L3 reaching AUROC $= 0.94$ (CI: 0.89--1.00) at $n = 15$. The diminishing returns curve follows a characteristic logarithmic scaling, consistent with centroid estimation converging as $O(1/\sqrt{n})$.

\textbf{Finding 259}: L3 consistently outperforms L32 across all calibration sizes, with tighter confidence intervals (CI width 0.10 vs 0.13 at $n = 20$). This reinforces L3 as the primary detection layer, with L32 providing complementary coverage via the OR-gate.

\textbf{Finding 260}: Bootstrap variance decreases monotonically from $\sigma = 0.12$ at $n = 2$ to $\sigma = 0.03$ at $n = 20$, confirming that the centroid becomes increasingly stable with more calibration samples. For safety-critical deployment, $n \geq 10$ provides acceptably tight confidence bounds.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig154_sample_efficiency.png}
\caption{\textbf{Sample efficiency curve.} AUROC vs calibration set size with 95\% bootstrap CIs (20 resamples each). L3 (left) reaches 0.90 AUROC at $n = 5$ and plateaus at $n \geq 15$. L32 (right) follows a similar curve with slightly wider CIs.}
\label{fig:sample_efficiency}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Token Position Analysis}

We compare OOD detection using embeddings from different token positions within the sequence (length 280).

\textbf{Finding 261}: The \textbf{last token} and \textbf{mean pooling} both achieve AUROC = 1.000 at L3 and L32, confirming that these are optimal extraction strategies for OOD detection.

\textbf{Finding 262}: The first token (BOS) carries \textbf{zero OOD signal}: AUROC = 0.500 with cosine distance = 0.000 for both ID and OOD images. This token is completely image-invariant, storing only positional/structural information.

\textbf{Finding 263}: Middle-sequence and max-pooled tokens show intermediate detection quality (AUROC 0.72--0.90), indicating that OOD information propagates through the sequence but concentrates at the last position via the autoregressive attention pattern.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig155_token_position.png}
\caption{\textbf{Token position analysis.} AUROC by extraction position at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Last token and mean pooling achieve perfect detection; first token (BOS) carries zero visual information.}
\label{fig:token_position}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Corruption Combination Interactions}

We test how simultaneous corruptions interact, applying 6 single, 9 double, and 2 triple combinations.

\textbf{Finding 264}: Corruption interactions are predominantly \textbf{subadditive}: 10 of 11 combinations produce OOD distances less than the sum of individual corruption distances (L32 ratio 0.40--0.76, mean 0.59). The embedding space exhibits a saturation effect.

\textbf{Finding 265}: Night + noise is the only \textbf{superadditive} combination (L3 ratio = 1.067), suggesting these corruptions affect orthogonal embedding dimensions. All other combinations, including all triple corruptions, are subadditive.

\textbf{Finding 266}: Despite subadditivity, \textbf{all combinations remain easily detectable}: even the most subadditive combination produces L32 distances well above detection thresholds. Combined real-world corruptions are NOT harder to detect than individual ones.

\textbf{Finding 267}: Triple corruptions (fog+blur+noise, night+blur+noise) are the most strongly subadditive (L32 ratios 0.40--0.45), suggesting that adding a third corruption produces diminishing marginal impact on the embedding representation.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig156_corruption_combo.png}
\caption{\textbf{Corruption combinations.} Left: L32 distances for all 17 conditions --- combined corruptions increase distance but subadditively. Right: Interaction ratios --- nearly all combinations below the additive line (ratio $< 1$).}
\label{fig:corruption_combo}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Norm-based vs Direction-based Detection}

We test whether embedding vector norms (L2, L1, $L_\infty$) carry OOD information independently of direction.

\textbf{Finding 268}: Direction-based metrics (cosine distance: AUROC = 0.96/0.97, Euclidean distance: 0.95/0.92) vastly outperform norm-based metrics (L2 norm: 0.51/0.52, L1 norm: 0.66/0.52). Norms are barely above random baseline.

\textbf{Finding 269}: OOD corruptions change the \textbf{direction} of embeddings, not their \textbf{magnitude}. At L3, ID and OOD L2 norms are virtually identical (7.94 vs 7.94). This explains why cosine distance (which normalizes magnitude) is the correct detection metric.

\textbf{Finding 270}: Sparsity provides a weak OOD signal at L3 (AUROC = 0.84): OOD embeddings are slightly less sparse than ID. However, this signal is too weak for reliable detection and should only serve as a supplementary feature.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig157_norm_analysis.png}
\caption{\textbf{Metric comparison.} Cosine and Euclidean distance (direction-based) achieve AUROC $> 0.9$; norms, mean activation, and norm deviation are near random ($\sim$0.5). OOD shifts direction, not magnitude.}
\label{fig:norm_analysis}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Detection Latency}

We measure the computational overhead of OOD detection on an A40 GPU.

\textbf{Finding 271}: Enabling \texttt{output\_hidden\_states=True} adds only \textbf{3.0 ms (2.5\%)} to the forward pass latency (119 ms $\to$ 122 ms). This is the only model-level modification required.

\textbf{Finding 272}: The complete OOD detection pipeline (forward pass + embedding extraction + distance computation) takes \textbf{140 ms}, adding 21 ms overhead over a baseline forward pass. This enables real-time operation at $> 7$ Hz.

\textbf{Finding 273}: Distance computation takes \textbf{10 $\mu$s} per cosine distance on a 4096-dimensional vector --- effectively free. The dominant cost is the forward pass itself, which is already required for action prediction.

\textbf{Finding 274}: Compared to action generation (372 ms for 7 tokens), OOD detection adds only 5.6\% overhead. The system can run detection on every frame without impacting the action generation rate.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig158_latency.png}
\caption{\textbf{Detection latency.} Left: Component latencies (log scale) --- forward pass dominates; distance computation is negligible. Right: Pipeline breakdown --- hidden state overhead is 2.5\% of forward pass time.}
\label{fig:latency}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Distance Metric Comparison}

We compare 7 distance metrics for centroid-based OOD detection.

\textbf{Finding 275}: \textbf{Cosine and correlation distance} are tied at L3 (AUROC = 0.961, $d' = 13.7$) and near-identical at L32 (0.972 vs 0.972). This equivalence holds because both measure angular deviation when embeddings are approximately centered.

\textbf{Finding 276}: \textbf{Chebyshev ($L_\infty$) distance achieves the highest AUROC at L32} (0.978), slightly exceeding cosine (0.972). The single most-shifted dimension is highly informative at the final layer, consistent with the distributed OOD signal observed in the feature ablation experiment.

\textbf{Finding 277}: The $p$-norm choice (L1 vs L2 vs $L_3$) has minimal impact: Manhattan and Euclidean distances achieve similar AUROCs (0.92--0.96). The angular normalization provided by cosine distance is more important than the choice of norm.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig159_distance_zoo.png}
\caption{\textbf{Distance metric zoo.} AUROC and $d'$ for 7 distance metrics at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Cosine and correlation tie for best at L3; Chebyshev is slightly best at L32.}
\label{fig:distance_zoo}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Robustness to Calibration Noise}

Real deployments may have imperfect calibration images. We test detection robustness when calibration data includes mild corruptions.

\textbf{Finding 278}: The detector is \textbf{robust to mild calibration noise}: Gaussian noise ($\sigma \leq 10$), fog ($\alpha \leq 0.15$), and light mixed corruptions all maintain AUROC $\geq 0.97$ at both layers.

\textbf{Finding 279}: \textbf{Blur is the most damaging calibration corruption}: blur at radius 2 drops AUROC to 0.82 (L3) and 0.73 (L32), while blur at radius 1 is fully tolerated (1.0/1.0). This is because blur shifts the calibration centroid toward the OOD manifold.

\textbf{Finding 280}: L3 is more robust to calibration noise than L32 across all noise types, maintaining AUROC $\geq 0.82$ even under blur\_2 while L32 drops to 0.73. This further supports L3 as the primary detection layer.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig160_cal_noise.png}
\caption{\textbf{Calibration noise robustness.} AUROC under 11 calibration noise conditions at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Mild noise and fog are tolerated; blur\_2 is the first level that degrades detection.}
\label{fig:cal_noise}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Adaptive Threshold and Temporal Dynamics}

Real autonomous driving scenarios involve continuous temporal streams where environmental conditions change gradually. We simulate a 30-frame sequence: 10 clean frames $\rightarrow$ 10 gradually increasing fog ($\alpha = 0.05$ to $0.50$) $\rightarrow$ 5 night frames $\rightarrow$ 5 clean recovery frames. We compare a static threshold (calibrated once) against an adaptive threshold using exponential moving average (EMA) of recent distances.

\textbf{Finding 281}: Both static and adaptive thresholds achieve \textbf{identical performance}: precision = 1.000, recall = 0.900, F1 = 0.947. The adaptive threshold provides no additional benefit because the static threshold is already well-calibrated and the adaptive threshold wisely freezes updates during detected OOD periods.

\textbf{Finding 282}: The detector exhibits \textbf{zero false alarms during recovery}: after 5 frames of night driving ($d_{L32} = 0.3913$), the detector immediately returns to non-flagged status on clean frames ($d_{L32} = 0.1175$). There is no hysteresis or recovery delay.

\textbf{Finding 283}: Fog below $\alpha = 0.30$ ($d_{L32} = 0.1263$) is \textbf{undetectable at the $3\sigma$ level}, defining the resolution limit of the cosine distance detector. The transition to detection occurs sharply at fog $\alpha = 0.35$ ($d_{L32} = 0.1322$). This suggests a phase-transition-like behavior in the embedding response to corruption intensity.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig161_adaptive_threshold_trace.png}
\caption{\textbf{Static vs.\ adaptive threshold on temporal sequence.} Frame-by-frame L32 cosine distance through clean $\rightarrow$ fog $\rightarrow$ night $\rightarrow$ clean phases. Red triangles mark OOD detections. Both methods achieve identical results with zero false alarms.}
\label{fig:adaptive_threshold}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig162_condition_phase_trajectory.png}
\caption{\textbf{Dual-layer distance trajectory through condition phases.} L3 and L32 distances trace the environmental shift from clean through fog to night and back. Night produces $3\times$ the fog distance, enabling trivial detection.}
\label{fig:condition_trajectory}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Robustness of Centroid Estimation}

Calibration data may contain outliers (mislabeled or corrupted images). We compare five centroid estimators---arithmetic mean, geometric median (Weiszfeld's algorithm), 20\% and 30\% trimmed means, and medoid---under three contamination levels (0, 1, or 2 OOD outliers mixed into $n=8$ calibration images).

\textbf{Finding 284}: The simple \textbf{arithmetic mean is the best centroid estimator}, achieving AUROC = 1.000 at L3 across all contamination levels and maintaining the highest L32 AUROC (0.969) even with 2 outliers. Robust estimators provide no advantage.

\textbf{Finding 285}: The \textbf{medoid is the worst estimator} (AUROC 0.82--0.89), because selecting a single data point as the center discards all averaging benefits. Trimmed means also underperform due to over-trimming in small-sample regimes.

\textbf{Finding 286}: Contaminating calibration with 1--2 OOD samples causes only a \textbf{3\% L32 AUROC drop} (1.000 $\rightarrow$ 0.969) for the mean estimator while L3 remains at 1.000. The detector is inherently robust to mild calibration contamination without requiring special robust statistics.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig163_robust_calibration.png}
\caption{\textbf{Centroid estimator comparison under calibration contamination.} AUROC by estimator and contamination level at L3 (left) and L32 (right). Simple mean dominates across all conditions.}
\label{fig:robust_calibration}
\end{figure}

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig164_robust_cal_heatmap.png}
\caption{\textbf{AUROC heatmap across estimators and contamination levels.} Mean (top row) consistently achieves the highest values, while medoid (bottom) is uniformly worst.}
\label{fig:robust_cal_heatmap}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Scene Diversity and the Need for Multi-Centroid Detection}

We stress-test the single-centroid detector by expanding from 3 scene types (highway, urban, rural) to 6 (adding parking lot, tunnel, desert road).

\textbf{Finding 287}: Increasing scene diversity from 3 to 6 types causes \textbf{significant AUROC degradation}: L3 drops from 1.000 to 0.833 ($-$0.167), L32 from 1.000 to 0.885 ($-$0.115). The single centroid becomes the average of dissimilar embeddings, widening the ID distribution 2.7$\times$.

\textbf{Finding 288}: \textbf{Night remains trivially detectable} (AUROC = 1.0 at both layers) regardless of scene diversity, because its embedding shift is so large that even a diluted centroid provides sufficient separation. In contrast, fog detection drops to AUROC = 0.65--0.69.

\textbf{Finding 289}: This degradation \textbf{motivates multi-centroid detection}: maintaining one centroid per scene type with nearest-centroid routing at inference time, preserving tight per-scene boundaries while covering diverse operating domains.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\includegraphics[width=\textwidth]{figures/fig165_scene_diversity.png}
